First Proof: The Dichotomy of Indivisible and Divisible
Razi presents the first proof against directionality using atomism and composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that God cannot be in a direction, based on the dichotomy of being either indivisible or divisible.

Core Idea
Anything in a direction must be either a tiny atom or a composite body—both impossible for God.

Razi begins his critique of directionality with a crisp logical proof. He argues that any entity occupying a direction must be either indivisible (like an atom) or divisible. Both options lead to conclusions that contradict God's transcendence. The passage also includes a parallel argument based on comparison to the Throne.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Focus on the overall structure of the proof: the initial dichotomy, the two branches, and the alternative argument.
Notice the two main sections: the dichotomy argument (indivisible/divisible) and the Throne-sizing argument.
2
Trace the dichotomy
Follow Razi's reasoning: if a thing is in a direction, it is either non-divisible or divisible. The non-divisible case leads to being like an indivisible atom, which the opponents agree is unworthy of God. The divisible case leads to being a composite body, which was already refuted.
The 'opponents' here are the groups (like the Karrāmiyya) who believed God is in a direction.
3
Understand the Throne argument
Razi presents a second version: if God were above the Throne, He would be either larger, equal, or smaller than it. Each possibility leads to composition or baseness. This shows the same conclusion from a different angle.
This argument is more specific to those who say God is above the Throne.
4
Reflect on the final simplification
Razi notes that if one accepts that every spatial entity is divisible, then the entire Throne argument is unnecessary. Simply being sensorily pointed to implies having distinct sides, hence composition.
This connects to atomistic principles accepted by some theologians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : كل ما كان في جهة فإما أن يكون غير محتمل للقسمة وإما أن يكون محتملا للقسمة، فإن لم يكن محتملا للقسمة، مع أنه مشار إليه بحسب الحس، كان في الصغر والحقرة كالجوهر الفرد والخصوم وافقوا على أنه يجب تنزيه الله هذه الصفة، وأما إن كان مشارًا إليه مع أنه محتمل للقسمة كان جسما مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض وحينئذ يرجع الكلام إلى المسألة الأولى، ولهذا السر اتفق أصحابنا على أن كل من أثبت الله تعالى في الحيز والجهة لا بد وأن يعترف بكونه مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض. واعلم أن أصحابنا عبروا عن هذه الدلالة بأن قالوا : لو كان في جهة فوق لكان إما أن يكون أكبر من العرش أو مساويًا له أو أصغر منه فإن كان أكبر من العرش كان القدر المساوي منه العرش مغايرا للقدر الفاضل منه من العرش، فيكون مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض، وإن كان مساويًا للعرش وثبت أن العرش منقسم من الأجزاء والأبعاض كان مساويًا له في المقدار منقسما، مركبًا من الأجزاء والأبعاض، وإن كان أصغر من العرش فإما أن يكون قد بلغ في الصغر إلى أن كان مساويًا للجوهر الفرد، والجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ، فيكون في غاية الصغر والحقارة - وجل ربنا عن باتفاق العقلاء - وإما أن يكون أكبر من الجوهر الفرد، ويعود القول بالتركيب والقسمة. ذلك واعلم أن على قول من يقول : كل متحيز فهو قابل للقسمة أبدا لا نحتاج إلى ذكر هذا التقسيم بل نقول : كل ما كان مشارًا إليه بحسب الحس فإنه لا بد وأن يتميز يمينه عن يساره وقدامه عن ورائه وفوقه عن تحته فيكون منقسمًا مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض، وذلك مما بينا امتناعه في المسألة السابقة.
"The first proof: Everything that is in a direction is either indivisible (غير محتمل للقسمة) or divisible (محتمل للقسمة). If it is indivisible, while being sensorily pointed to, it would be in smallness and lowliness like the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) – and the opponents agree that God must be declared transcendent of this attribute. But if it is sensorily pointed to while being divisible, it would be a body composed of parts and members, and then the discourse returns to the first question. For this secret, our companions (أصحابنا) have agreed that whoever affirms that God is in space and direction must inevitably acknowledge that He is composed of parts and members. Know that our companions expressed this indication by saying: If He were in the direction above, He would either be greater than the Throne, or equal to it, or smaller than it. If greater than the Throne, then the part of Him equal to the Throne would be different from the part exceeding the Throne, so He would be composed of parts and members. If equal to the Throne, and it is established that the Throne is divisible into parts and members, then that which is equal to it in magnitude would be divisible and composed of parts and members. If smaller than the Throne, then either He has reached in smallness to being equal to the indivisible atom, which is the part that cannot be divided, so He would be in extreme smallness and lowliness – and our Lord is exalted above that by agreement of the rational people – or He would be larger than the indivisible atom, and the assertion of composition and division returns. That is the case. Know that according to the position of those who say: Every spatial entity (متحيز) is always divisible, we have no need to mention this division. Rather we say: Everything that is sensorily pointed to must necessarily have its right distinguished from its left, its front from its back, its above from its below, so it is divided and composed of parts and members. And that is something whose impossibility we have demonstrated in the previous question."
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is the first proof (al-hujja al-ula) against God being in a direction. Razi presents a dichotomy: anything in a direction is either indivisible (like an atom) or divisible. If indivisible, it is extremely small and base, which is unbecoming of God. If divisible, it is a composite body, which has already been refuted in the earlier discussion (first question of the book). Then he presents an alternative argument used by his school: if God were above the Throne, He would be either larger, equal, or smaller. Each case leads to composition. Finally, he notes that if one accepts that every spatial entity is divisible (a principle from atomism), then the division argument is unnecessary; simply being sensorily pointed to implies having distinct sides and thus composition. Since composition has been proven impossible for God, He cannot be in a direction.

Key Terms
قسمة
محتمل للقسمة
جوهر فرد
مركب
أجزاء
أبعاض
متحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God is in a direction, He could be a simple indivisible point and still be majestic.
Correction: Razi argues that an indivisible entity in space is like the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which is extremely small and base (haqara), and thus unworthy of God. Even opponents agree God must be transcendent of that.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, what does the case of being in a direction and divisible lead to?
A) Being a simple, indivisible entity.
B) Being a body composed of parts and members.
C) Being infinite in size.
D) Being non-spatial.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof shows that any being in a direction must be either a base indivisible atom or a composite body, both of which are impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you imagine God, do you instinctively picture Him in a certain 'place' or 'direction'? What does this proof reveal about that mental image? Are you willing to surrender that image and affirm His absolute transcendence, even if it feels abstract?
?
Razi argues that any spatial attribute leads to composition or baseness. Have you ever thought of God's attributes like 'being above' as literal, physical qualities? What concrete change will you make in your understanding of God after reading this proof?
?
This proof forces you to accept that God is not in a direction. Is there a part of you that resists this because it feels like God is distant or less real? How will you cultivate a sense of nearness to God without relying on physical distance or place?

Second Proof: All Sense-Perceptible Beings Are Finite
Razi argues that anything perceptible is finite, and the finite is originated, so God cannot be such.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for God's existence, which argues that all perceptible beings are finite and thus originated, and why God cannot be such.

Core Idea
Everything we can point to is finite; only the Eternal can be infinite.

Razi presents a concise proof that anything detectable by the senses must have distinct sides and therefore be finite. Since the finite is contingent and originated, it cannot describe the Necessary Being. This proof relies on a key kalam argument about the finitude of dimensions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, supporting reasons, and conclusion.
The argument is cut off at the end, but the reasoning is clear.
2
Identify the two reasons for finiteness
The passage gives two reasons why everything sensorily pointed to is finite. Identify each one and how they support the premise.
First reason references a proof about dimensions; second is a reductio about God's infinity.
3
Reflect on the conclusion about God
Consider how this proof shows that God, being eternal, cannot be perceptible or finite. What does it imply about God's nature?
This excludes God from being a body or located in space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : كل ما كان مشارًا إليه بحسب الحس فإنه لا بد وأن يتميز يمينه عن يساره، فهو متناه من جميع الجوانب، وكل ما كان متناهيا من جميع الجوانب فهو محدث وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما كان مشارًا إليه بحسب الحس فهو متناه من جميع الجوانب لوجهين : الأول : البرهان المذكور في مسألة حدوث الأجسام على تناهي الأبعاد، وذلك ريب فيها. الثاني: أنه تعالى لو كان غير متناه لكان إما أن يكون غير متناه من جميع الجوانب أو بعض الجوانب، لا جائز أن يكون غير متناه من جميع الجوانب؛ لأن على هذا التقدير يلزم أن يكون العالم ساريًا في ذات الله تعالى وحالا فيه، ويلزم أن تكون ذاته مخالطة للقاذورات - تعالى الله عن هذه المقالة، وعن هذا الوهم والخيال - ولا جائز أن يقال إنه غير متناه من بعض الجهات دون البعض، وذلك لأن الجانب ذاته إما أن يكون مساويًا للجانب الآخر - الذي هو غير متناه في الحقيقة المتناهي من
"The second proof: Everything that is sensorily pointed to must have its right distinguished from its left, so it is finite (متناه) from all sides. And everything that is finite from all sides is originated (محدث). We only said that everything sensorily pointed to is finite from all sides for two reasons: First: the proof mentioned in the question of the origination of bodies (حدوث الأجسام) concerning the finitude of dimensions, and there is no doubt about it. Second: that if the Exalted were infinite, He would either be infinite from all sides or from some sides. It is not possible that He be infinite from all sides, because on this assumption it would follow that the world pervades the Essence of God (ذات الله) and is contained in Him, and it would follow that His Essence is mingled with filth (القاذورات) – exalted is God above such a statement and such a fantasy and imagination. And it is not permissible to say that He is infinite from some sides without others, because the side itself is either equal to the other side — which is infinite in reality — finite from..."
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is the second proof (al-hujja al-thaniya). Razi argues that anything that can be sensorily pointed to must have distinguishable sides (right/left, etc.), which implies it is finite in all directions. And everything finite is originated (hadith). Since God is eternal (qadim), He cannot be originated, so He cannot be such. He gives two reasons for the finiteness of perceptible objects: first, the established proof of the finitude of dimensions from the discussion on the origination of bodies (a standard kalam argument); second, a reductio ad absurdum: if God were infinite from all sides, the world would be inside Him, leading to contamination; if infinite only from some sides, that is also impossible because a side that is infinite would be equal to the other side that is finite, which is contradictory. The argument is cut off, but the reasoning is clear.

Key Terms
مشار إليه
متناه
محدث
غير متناه
قاذورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be infinite from some directions but not others.
Correction: Razi argues that if God were infinite from one side, that side would be equal to the opposite finite side, which is contradictory. So infinitude cannot be partial.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what follows if God were infinite from all sides?
A) He would be eternal.
B) The world would be inside His Essence.
C) He would be finite.
D) He would have no sides.

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof shows that what is perceptible must be finite and thus originated, so the eternal God cannot be perceptible or finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You rely on your senses every day to navigate the world. But this lesson teaches that the most important Reality—Allah—cannot be perceived by them. Does your heart truly accept that your inability to sense Him is a limitation of you, not of Him? How will you train yourself to seek Him beyond the physical today?

If Allah cannot be contained by space, then His closeness to you must be of a different order. Do you experience that closeness as a spiritual reality, or have you reduced it to a feeling you manufacture? What one concrete step will you take this week to seek His nearness through the means He has prescribed, rather than through your imagination?

The Disagreement on God's Creative Action: Power and Choice vs. Necessitation
Razi introduces the second chapter, states the consensus, presents the philosophers' counterposition, and begins a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central disagreement between Islamic theologians and philosophers regarding whether God creates by power and choice or by necessitation, and why this distinction matters for Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The nature of God's creative action is debated: is it voluntary or necessary?

This passage opens Razi's second chapter, where he aims to prove that God is a voluntary agent (qadir) rather than a necessitating cause (mujib). He first presents the consensus of religious communities that God creates by power and choice, contrasting it with the philosophers' view that God's action is like natural causes. The proof is begun but cut off; we will focus on the background and the starting point of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two opposing views
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two positions: 'agreed that the effect... is by power and choice' vs 'philosophers claimed that His effect is by necessitation'. Also note the analogy used for the philosophers' view.
The philosophers' view is analogous to natural causality: sun inevitably gives light, fire inevitably heats.
2
Understand the key terms
Focus on the terms: qadir (capable/voluntary agent), mujib (necessitator), qudra (power), ikhtiyar (choice), and ijad (necessitation by essence). These will recur throughout the chapter.
Qadir implies free agency; mujib implies deterministic causation.
3
Reflect on why this distinction matters
Ask yourself: If God were a necessitator, could He choose to do otherwise? Would the world be contingent? How does this affect God's attributes like will and power? The commentary will help connect these ideas.
This distinction affects the entire Islamic understanding of divine action and the world's contingency.
4
Note the incomplete proof
The passage ends mid-sentence: 'if the Creator were a necessitator...' The full argument would follow on the next page. We will cover only the setup here.
The proof is incomplete in this passage; it is a cliffhanger.

الفصل الثاني في إقامة الدلائل على أنه تعالى قادر. اتفق أرباب الملل والأديان على أن تأثير الباري تعالى في إيجاد العالم بالقدرة والاختيار، وزعمت الفلاسفة أن تأثيره في وجود العالم بالإيجاد كتأثير الشمس في الإضاءة، وتأثير النار في التسخين والإحراق. فنقول : الدليل على أنه تعالى قادر لا موجب أنه لو كان الباري تعالى موجبا
The second chapter: On adducing proofs that He, exalted, is capable (qadir). The adherents of religious communities (ahl al-milal wa al-adyan) agree that the effect of the Creator, exalted, in bringing the world into existence is by power (qudra) and choice (ikhtiyar). The philosophers claimed that His effect in the existence of the world is by necessitation (ijad), like the effect of the sun in illumination and the effect of fire in heating and burning. We say: The proof that He, exalted, is capable, not a necessitator, is that if the Creator, exalted, were a necessitator...
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage opens the second chapter (al-fasl al-thani) which aims to prove that God is qadir (voluntary agent) rather than a necessitating cause (mujib). Razi notes the consensus of religious communities (ahl al-milal wa al-adyan) that God's action in creating the world is by power and choice. The philosophers, however, claim that God's effect is by pure necessitation (ijad), analogous to natural causes like the sun giving light or fire heating. The proof is then introduced but is cut off mid-sentence at the page end. The intended argument would likely be that if God were a necessitator, certain absurdities would follow, but the text is incomplete.

God Acts by Choice: The Refutation of Necessary Causality
Razi presents a proof by contradiction that God is a free agent, not a necessitating cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof by contradiction that God is a free agent (qadir) and not a necessitating cause (mujib), and why infinite regress is impossible.

Core Idea
God's action is free, not necessary like fire burning.

This lesson presents a classic kalam argument from Imam al-Razi. He sets up a disagreement: religious people say God acts by power and choice, while philosophers liken God's action to natural causes. Razi then offers a rigorous proof that God cannot be a necessitating cause. As you read, pay attention to the logical options he considers and how each leads to an unacceptable conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Get a feel for the flow of the argument.
Notice that Razi starts by stating the disagreement, then launches into the proof.
2
Identify the assumption and the branches
The proof assumes God is a mujib (necessitating cause). Then Razi divides the possibilities: either God's effect depends on a condition or not. Trace each branch.
Each branch leads to a false conclusion.
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
For the branch with a temporal condition, Razi argues that it leads to an infinite chain of temporal events (tasalsul), which he says has already been refuted. Grasp why this is absurd.
Infinite regress is impossible in a temporal series.
4
See the conclusion
Since all branches lead to falsehood, the assumption that God is a mujib is false. Therefore, God is qadir, a free agent.
This conclusion matches the consensus of religious people.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في إقامة الدلائل على أنه تعالى قادر اتفق أرباب الملل والأديان على أن تأثير الباري تعالى في إيجاد العالم بالقدرة والاختيار، وزعمت الفلاسفة أن تأثيره في وجود العالم بالإيجاد كتأثير الشمس في الإضاءة، وتأثير النار في التسخين والإحراق. فنقول : الدليل على أنه تعالى قادر لا موجب أنه لو كان الباري تعالى موجبا بالذات لكان تأثيره في وجود العالم إما أن لا يكون موقوفا على شرط وإما أن يكون موقوفا على شرط، فإن لم يكن موقوفًا على شرط لزم من قدمه قدم العالم، أو من حدوث العالم حدوثه، وكلاهما باطلان، وأما إن كان موقوفاً على شرط فذلك الشرط إن كان قديما لزم أيضًا قدم العالم، وإن كان حادثا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، فيفضي إلى التسلسل، وهو أن يكون كل حادث مسبوقا بحادث آخر قبله، وذلك قول بحوادث لا أول لها، وقد أبطلناه في مسألة حدوث الأجسام، فثبت أن القول بكونه تعالى موجبًا بالذات يفضي إلى هذه الأقسام الباطلة فيكون باطلاً، وإذا بطل هذا ثبت أنه تعالی قادر فاعل مختار
The second chapter: On establishing proofs that He the Exalted is qadir. The people of religions and faiths agree that the effect of the Creator the Exalted in bringing the world into existence is by power and choice, while the philosophers claim that His effect in the world's existence is by necessitation, like the effect of the sun in illumination and the effect of fire in heating and burning. We say: The proof that He the Exalted is qadir and not a mujib is that if the Creator the Exalted were a mujib by essence, then His effect in the world's existence would either not be dependent on a condition or would be dependent on a condition. If it is not dependent on a condition, then either from His eternity follows the world's eternity, or from the world's temporality follows His temporality, and both are false. If it is dependent on a condition, then if that condition is eternal, the world's eternity also follows; if it is temporal, the discussion about it is as in the first case, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul), which is that every temporal event is preceded by another temporal event before it, and that is the assertion of temporal events with no beginning, which we have refuted in the problem of the temporality of bodies. Thus it is established that the assertion that He the Exalted is a mujib by essence leads to these false divisions, so it is false. When this is false, it is established that He the Exalted is qadir, an agent, a free chooser.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a formal proof that God is a free agent (qadir) and not a necessitating cause (mujib). Razi first notes the consensus of religious people that God acts by power and choice, contrasting with philosophers who liken His action to natural causes. The proof proceeds by assuming the opposite — that God is a mujib by essence — and deducing absurdities. If God's causality is unconditional, then either the world is eternal (like God) or God is temporal (like the world), both false. If it is conditional, the condition must be either eternal (leading again to an eternal world) or temporal, leading to an infinite regress of temporal events, which Razi says was already refuted in the discussion on the temporality of bodies. Since all possibilities lead to falsehood, the original assumption is false, proving that God is a free agent.

Key Terms
قادر
موجب
تسلسل
الفلاسفة
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God's act is necessary, it cannot be free, but the argument refutes the necessity itself.
Correction: Razi refutes the claim that God's act is necessary (mujib) by showing it leads to contradictions. Thus, free agency is established.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim is the consequence if a temporal condition is required for God's necessitating causality?
A) The condition would have to be eternal.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of temporal events.
C) The world would be eternal.
D) God would be temporal.

Key Takeaway
God's free agency is proven by showing that the alternative (necessitating causality) leads to contradictions, specifically either an eternal world or an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes treat God's actions as if they were automatic, expecting certain outcomes because you performed certain deeds? How does Razi's proof that God acts by free choice shatter that mechanical view and demand you rely on His personal will rather than a formula? What specific relationship pattern must you change today?
?
If God were a necessitating cause like fire, your obedience would guarantee reward mechanically. But since He acts by choice, your good deeds do not obligate Him. Does this truth make you despair of His mercy or humbly hope in His generosity? How will you cultivate a living trust in His free grace, not a transactional mindset?
?
Razi refutes the philosophers who compared God to natural forces. In what areas of your life have you unconsciously assumed God must act predictably—perhaps in prayer, repentance, or provision? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God chooses to act each moment, and how can you respond to that freedom with awe and surrender?

The Preponderant Cause Objection and Divine Agency by Choice
Razi presents the objection and his first response, concluding divine agency is by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the temporality of the world necessitates a free divine agent, not a necessary agent.

Core Idea
The impediment of pre-eternity requires a choice, not necessity.

The objector challenges Razi: if a free agent's power and will are equally related to all times, why not allow the same for a necessary agent? Razi's first response cuts to the core: the world is temporal, so its specific time demands that the impediment of pre-eternity be removed before that time. If God were a necessary agent, the world would have occurred earlier. This forces the conclusion that God acts by choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first answer
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to 'لا أنه موجب بالذات' and its translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors.
Note the two terms: 'فاعل بالاختيار' (free agent) and 'موجب بالذات' (necessary agent).
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'أزل' (pre-eternity) and 'علة موجبة' (necessitating cause). Understand how they function in the argument.
3
Understand the argument structure
Razi's argument has three premises: (1) The world is temporal. (2) Its occurrence at a specific time requires that the impediment (pre-eternity) be removed before that time. (3) If the necessitating cause were present before, the world would have occurred earlier. Conclusion: The agent is not necessitating but free.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes: 'فوجب القول بأنه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار'. Why does he say 'وجب' (it is necessary)? Think about the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية عشرة لمرجح أصلا، مع أن نسبة صلاحية القدرة والإرادة بالنسبة إلى كل الأوقات على السوية ؟ فلم لا يجوز مثله في الموجب بالذات ؟ والجواب عن الأول ثبت أن الأزل مناف لحدوث العالم، ولكن إذا كان العالم محدثًا كان حدوثه مختصا بوقت معين ولو كان حادثاً قبل أن حدث بتقدير عشرة أيام لم يصر بهذا القدر أزلياً، وإذا كان كذلك فلا وقت يفرض حدوثه فيه إلا وكان المانع - وهو الأزل - زائلا قبل ذلك الوقت، وإذا كان المانع زائلا قبل ذلكم الوقت وكانت العلة الموجبة حاصلة قبل ذلك لزم حدوثه قبل أن حدث، وذلك محال، فوجب القول بأنه تعالى فاعل بالاختيار لا أنه موجب بالذات. وقوله ثانيا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى موجب لذاته وقوع العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين؟ قلنا : إذن على هذا التقدير يكون تأثير ذات الله تعالى في وجود العالم مشروطا بحضور ذلك الوقت، وعند هذا يعود القسم الذي ذكرناه من أن ذلك الشرط إن كان قديما لزم قدم المعلول، وإن حادثا كان الكلام فيه كما في الأول، وهذا يقتضي اشتراط كل حادث يحادث آخر، لا إلى أول، وهذا هو القول بوجود حوادث لا أول لها، وقد أبطلناه.
The eleventh issue: Is there no preponderant cause originally? While the proportion of ability of power and will with respect to all times is equal? So why is the like of it not permissible in the necessary agent? The answer to the first: it is established that pre-eternity is incompatible with the temporality of the world. But if the world is temporal, its occurrence is specific to a definite time. And even if it had come into existence before it came into existence by an estimation of ten days, it would not have become eternal by this amount. And if it is like that, there is no time in which its occurrence is supposed except that the impediment – which is pre-eternity – is removed before that time. And if the impediment is removed before that time and the necessitating cause was present before that, then its occurrence before it occurred would be necessary, and that is impossible. So it is obligatory to say that He, exalted, is an agent by choice (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar), not that He is a necessitating agent (mujib bil-dhat). And secondly he said: Why is it not permissible to say that He, exalted, necessitates by His essence the occurrence of the world at that specific time? We say: Then on this supposition the effect of the essence of God, exalted, upon the existence of the world would be conditional upon the presence of that time. And with this, the division we mentioned returns: if that condition is eternal, then the effect must be eternal; if it is temporal, the discussion about it is like the first. And this implies that every temporal thing requires another temporal thing, without a first. And this is the doctrine of the existence of temporal events without a first, and we have already refuted it.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair within Razi's argument for divine free choice. The objector begins by asking: if for a free agent the power and will are equally related to all times, why cannot the same be said for a necessary agent? In other words, why not allow that a necessary agent also acts at one time rather than another without a preponderant cause? Razi answers in two parts. First, he argues that if the world is temporal, its occurrence at a specific time requires that the impediment of pre-eternity be removed before that time. But if the necessitating cause (the 'illa mujiba) were present before that time, the world would have occurred earlier, which is impossible. Therefore, the agent must be free (fa'il bil-ikhtiyar), not a necessary agent. Second, the objector suggests that perhaps God necessitates the world at that specific time. Razi responds that this would make the divine essence's effect conditional upon the presence of that time. The condition is either eternal (leading to an eternal effect) or temporal (leading to an infinite regress of temporal conditions), both of which are impossible. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار
موجب بالذات
أزل
علة موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is denying that God has a will; in fact, he is affirming that God's agency involves choice, not blind necessity.
Correction: Razi is arguing that God is not compelled to act by His essence without will; rather, He acts with free choice.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first response, what is the impediment that prevents the world from being pre-eternal?
A) The absence of a cause
B) Pre-eternity itself
C) The lack of divine will
D) The world's own nature

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the incompatibility of pre-eternity with temporality to prove that God must be an agent by choice, not a necessary agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that God is bound by the laws of cause and effect, like a cosmic machine, rather than being a personal Lord who freely chooses to act? How does Razi's argument confront that assumption?
?
When you face hardship, do you ever think 'God had no choice but to bring this upon me'? What does it mean to know that every trial is a deliberate choice from a free Agent? How can that change your response?
?
If God is not a necessitating agent, then He is not forced to answer your du'as. Do you truly believe He freely chooses to listen to you? What would change if you prayed with that certainty?

Refuting the Conditional Causation Objection
Razi responds to the objection that God necessitates the world at a specific time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second response to the preponderant cause objection, which shows that conditional causation leads to an infinite regress or eternal effect, both impossible.

Core Idea
Conditional causation leads to an impossible infinite regress or eternal world.

The objector persists: perhaps God necessitates the world at a specific time. Razi's second response examines this suggestion. If God's essence necessitates the world only at that time, then that time is a condition. If the condition is eternal, the effect is eternal; if temporal, it leads to an infinite regress of temporal conditions. Both options are impossible, so the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and Razi's response
Read from 'وقوله ثانيا' to the end. Notice the conditional structure.
Razi introduces a division: the condition (the specific time) is either eternal or temporal.
2
Analyze the two branches
If the condition is eternal, then the effect (the world) would be eternal, which contradicts its temporality. If the condition is temporal, then it itself requires another condition, leading to an infinite regress.
Infinite regress is impossible in kalam.
3
Connect to earlier refutation
Razi mentions that the doctrine of temporal events without a first has already been refuted. This reinforces the impossibility.
4
Conclude the overall argument
Thus, the preponderant cause objection fails, and divine free choice is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Three Objections Against Inferring Knowledge from Design
Razi presents three objections against inferring divine knowledge from the perfection of created acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections raised against the argument that the precision of created things proves the creator's knowledge, and why these objections challenge the proof from design.

Core Idea
Precision alone does not prove knowledge; animals, ignorance, and conjecture can also produce precise acts.

Philosophers and theologians often argue that the intricate design of the universe proves that its Creator must have knowledge. However, as Imam Razi reports, some raised objections: if design proves knowledge, then animals that build complex structures would be more knowledgeable than humans. Furthermore, precise acts sometimes occur from ignorant agents, and even if we grant awareness, it could be mere conjecture. In this lesson, we explore these three objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection about bees and spiders
Notice how the objector uses the example of hexagonal houses and spider webs to argue that if design implies knowledge, animals would be more knowledgeable than humans. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The objector assumes the design argument is being used to prove God's knowledge.
2
Read the second objection about the precise act from an ignorant person
The objector argues that if a precise act can come from an ignorant person once, it can occur multiple times, so design does not necessitate knowledge.
This challenges the link between precision and knowledge in the agent.
3
Read the third objection about conjecture vs. knowledge
Even if we accept that some awareness is needed, the objector asks why it must be certain knowledge and not conjecture, since people often produce wondrous acts based on guesswork.
This further weakens the necessity of certain knowledge.
4
Notice the logical flow of the three objections
Each objection weakens the link between precise action and certain knowledge. They are cumulative but independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية عشرة
عالما، والدليل عليه وجوه :
الأول : وهو أن البيوت المسدسة التي بنتها النحل من غير مسطرة ولا بركار، ولا يقدر عليها الإنسان والبيت الذي يتخذه العنكبوت من تلك الخيوط من غير شيء الآلات والأدوات لا يقدر عليه الإنسان، فلو دل ذلك على علم الفاعل لزم أن تكون هذه الحيوانات أكثر علما من الإنسان، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل.
الثاني : وهو أن الفعل المحكم المتقن قد يصدر مرة واحدة من الجاهل، وهذا مشاهد، وإذا جاز صدوره مرة واحدة جاز صدوره ثانيًا وثالثًا ؛ لأن الأشياء المتماثلة حكمها حكم واحد، وإذا كان كذلك بطلت دلالة الفعل المحكم على علم الفاعل.
سلمنا أن فاعل الفعل المحكم لا بد له من إدراك وشعور، لكن لم لا يكفي فيه الظن؟ ولم قلتم : إنه لا بد له من العلم؟ والدليل عليه هو أن أكثر هذه الأفاعيل العجيبة الصادرة من الناس إنما تصدر عنهم حال كونهم ظانين، لا حال كونهم قاطعين، أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إن الظان قد يخطئ كثيرًا إلا أنا نقول : المصالح الحاصلة في تركيبات هذا العالم غير خالية من المفاسد، ولعل هذه المفاسد إنما وقعت لأجل أن فاعلها ظان لا عالم.
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن من استدل في حدوث العالم بدليل الحركة والسكون كان هذا السؤال لازمًا عليه أما نحن لما بينا أن كل ما سوی الله تعالیٰ محدث سواء كان متحيزا أو قائما بالمتحيز أو لا متحيزا ولا قائما بالمتحيز سقط عنا هذا السؤال ؛ لأنه لما كان كل ما سوى الله محدثاً، كان تأثيره سبحانه وتعالى في إيجادها بالقدرة والاختيار لا بالطبع والإيجاب والموجد للشيء على سبيل القدرة والاختيار، لا بد وأن يكون له شعور بما يقصد على إيجاده واختراعه، وهذا القدر يكفي في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً، وأما أنه تعالى عالم بكل الأشياء فتلك مسألة أخرى، وبهذا الجواب سقط جميع ما ذكروه من احتجاج قدماء الفلاسفة على إنكار العلم بوجوه :
The Twelfth Issue. [He is] knowing, and the evidence for it has aspects: First: The hexagonal houses that bees build without a ruler or compass, and humans are not capable of them, and the web that the spider takes from those threads without any tools and instruments, humans are not capable of it. So if that indicated the knowledge of the agent, it would be necessary that these animals be more knowing than humans, and it is known that this is false. Second: The precise, perfected act may occur once from the ignorant, and this is observed, and if its occurrence once is permissible, its occurrence a second and third time is permissible, because similar things have the same judgment. If so, then the implication of the precise act for the knowledge of the agent is invalidated. We concede that the agent of the precise act must have perception and awareness, but why is conjecture not sufficient? And why do you say that he must have knowledge? The evidence for it is that most of these wondrous acts emanating from people emanate from them while they are conjecturing, not while they are certain. The most that can be said is that the conjecturer may err often, but we say: The benefits obtained in the compositions of this world are not free from harms, and perhaps these harms occurred only because their agent is a conjecturer, not a knower. The answer to the first question: He who argued for the temporal origination of the world by the argument of motion and rest — this question is necessary upon him. As for us, since we showed that everything other than God Most High is originated, whether it is spatially extended or subsisting in the spatially extended or neither spatially extended nor subsisting in the spatially extended, this question falls away from us. Because when everything other than God is originated, His effect — glory be to Him and Most High — in bringing them into existence is by power and choice, not by nature and compulsion. The bringer of a thing into existence by way of power and choice must have awareness of what he intends to bring into existence and create. This much suffices to prove that He Most High is knowing. As for Him Most High being knowing of all things, that is another issue. By this answer, all that they mentioned from the argument of the ancient philosophers for the denial of knowledge fell away, by aspects:
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to inferring God's knowledge from the perfection of created things, followed by Razi's refutation. The objector argues that if precise actions require knowledge, then animals like bees and spiders, which construct intricate structures without tools, would be more knowledgeable than humans — which is absurd. Second, a precise act can sometimes come from an ignorant person, so it does not necessarily imply knowledge. Third, even if we grant some awareness, why must it be certain knowledge rather than mere conjecture? Razi's answer distinguishes his own proof from those based on motion and rest. He states that he has already proven that everything other than God is originated, regardless of category. Thus, God's creative act is by power and choice, which inherently requires awareness of what He creates. This suffices to establish that God is knowing, though not yet that He knows all things. Therefore, the objection does not apply to his approach.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
ظن (conjecture)
فعل محكم (precise act)
نحل (bees)
عنكبوت (spider)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intricate design of the world directly proves that the creator has knowledge.
Correction: The objections show that design alone is insufficient; additional premises are needed to establish that the agent has certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection that Razi reports against the argument from design?
A) Animals are more knowledgeable than humans
B) The precise act may come from an ignorant person
C) Conjecture is sufficient for precise acts
D) The world contains too much evil

Key Takeaway
The three objections challenge the notion that precise acts necessarily entail certain knowledge in the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate design in nature, do you automatically assume it points to a knowing Creator? How do you personally respond to Razi's objection that animals also produce precise acts—does it ever make you doubt your conviction? What would it take for you to move beyond that doubt?
?
Think of a time you performed a precise or skillful act without full certainty—perhaps a spontaneous decision that turned out well. If your own actions can succeed without complete knowledge, what does that imply about your expectation that the universe's design must come from certain knowledge? Are you willing to let that question unsettle you?
?
The passage mentions that the benefits of the world are mixed with harms, suggesting the agent might be conjecturing. When you experience harm in your life, do you ever question the wisdom or knowledge behind it? How does this lesson challenge you to trust God's knowledge even when you don't understand the harms? What step can you take today to strengthen that trust?

Razi's Refutation: The True Proof of God's Knowledge
Razi provides a refutation of the objections and presents his own proof of divine knowledge based on contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the three objections by distinguishing his own proof based on contingency and explains why God's creative act by power and choice necessitates knowledge.

Core Idea
Razi's proof of God's knowledge does not rely on the precision of acts but on the fact that everything other than God is originated and thus requires a knowing creator.

After presenting the objections, Imam Razi provides a powerful refutation. He argues that the objections only apply to those who base their proof on the design argument using motion and rest. His own proof, which he has already established, shows that everything other than God is originated—whether material, abstract, or mental. Therefore, God's bringing things into existence is by power and choice, which inherently requires awareness. In this lesson, we examine Razi's answer and his positive argument for divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's initial statement that the objection only applies to those who argue from motion and rest
Notice how he first contextualizes the objection, showing it is not relevant to his own framework.
2
Understand his claim that everything other than God is originated
He says he has already proven that all things other than God are limited (muhdath), regardless of category. This is the foundation.
This includes substances, accidents, and even abstract entities.
3
See how he links origination to power and choice
Because everything is limited, God's creative act is by power and choice, not by nature or compulsion. An agent acting by choice must have awareness of what it intends to create.
4
Recognize the scope of this proof
Razi explicitly says this suffices to prove God is knowing, but not yet that He knows all things. That is a separate issue.
5
Reflect on why this answer defeats all the objections
The objections assume the proof relies on the precision of acts, but Razi's proof relies on the general contingency of all things, so the objections miss the mark.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية عشرة
عالما، والدليل عليه وجوه :
الأول : وهو أن البيوت المسدسة التي بنتها النحل من غير مسطرة ولا بركار، ولا يقدر عليها الإنسان والبيت الذي يتخذه العنكبوت من تلك الخيوط من غير شيء الآلات والأدوات لا يقدر عليه الإنسان، فلو دل ذلك على علم الفاعل لزم أن تكون هذه الحيوانات أكثر علما من الإنسان، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل.
الثاني : وهو أن الفعل المحكم المتقن قد يصدر مرة واحدة من الجاهل، وهذا مشاهد، وإذا جاز صدوره مرة واحدة جاز صدوره ثانيًا وثالثًا ؛ لأن الأشياء المتماثلة حكمها حكم واحد، وإذا كان كذلك بطلت دلالة الفعل المحكم على علم الفاعل.
سلمنا أن فاعل الفعل المحكم لا بد له من إدراك وشعور، لكن لم لا يكفي فيه الظن؟ ولم قلتم : إنه لا بد له من العلم؟ والدليل عليه هو أن أكثر هذه الأفاعيل العجيبة الصادرة من الناس إنما تصدر عنهم حال كونهم ظانين، لا حال كونهم قاطعين، أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال: إن الظان قد يخطئ كثيرًا إلا أنا نقول : المصالح الحاصلة في تركيبات هذا العالم غير خالية من المفاسد، ولعل هذه المفاسد إنما وقعت لأجل أن فاعلها ظان لا عالم.
والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن من استدل في حدوث العالم بدليل الحركة والسكون كان هذا السؤال لازمًا عليه أما نحن لما بينا أن كل ما سوی الله تعالیٰ محدث سواء كان متحيزا أو قائما بالمتحيز أو لا متحيزا ولا قائما بالمتحيز سقط عنا هذا السؤال ؛ لأنه لما كان كل ما سوى الله محدثاً، كان تأثيره سبحانه وتعالى في إيجادها بالقدرة والاختيار لا بالطبع والإيجاب والموجد للشيء على سبيل القدرة والاختيار، لا بد وأن يكون له شعور بما يقصد على إيجاده واختراعه، وهذا القدر يكفي في إثبات كونه تعالى عالماً، وأما أنه تعالى عالم بكل الأشياء فتلك مسألة أخرى، وبهذا الجواب سقط جميع ما ذكروه من احتجاج قدماء الفلاسفة على إنكار العلم بوجوه :
The Twelfth Issue. [He is] knowing, and the evidence for it has aspects: First: The hexagonal houses that bees build without a ruler or compass, and humans are not capable of them, and the web that the spider takes from those threads without any tools and instruments, humans are not capable of it. So if that indicated the knowledge of the agent, it would be necessary that these animals be more knowing than humans, and it is known that this is false. Second: The precise, perfected act may occur once from the ignorant, and this is observed, and if its occurrence once is permissible, its occurrence a second and third time is permissible, because similar things have the same judgment. If so, then the implication of the precise act for the knowledge of the agent is invalidated. We concede that the agent of the precise act must have perception and awareness, but why is conjecture not sufficient? And why do you say that he must have knowledge? The evidence for it is that most of these wondrous acts emanating from people emanate from them while they are conjecturing, not while they are certain. The most that can be said is that the conjecturer may err often, but we say: The benefits obtained in the compositions of this world are not free from harms, and perhaps these harms occurred only because their agent is a conjecturer, not a knower. The answer to the first question: He who argued for the temporal origination of the world by the argument of motion and rest — this question is necessary upon him. As for us, since we showed that everything other than God Most High is originated, whether it is spatially extended or subsisting in the spatially extended or neither spatially extended nor subsisting in the spatially extended, this question falls away from us. Because when everything other than God is originated, His effect — glory be to Him and Most High — in bringing them into existence is by power and choice, not by nature and compulsion. The bringer of a thing into existence by way of power and choice must have awareness of what he intends to bring into existence and create. This much suffices to prove that He Most High is knowing. As for Him Most High being knowing of all things, that is another issue. By this answer, all that they mentioned from the argument of the ancient philosophers for the denial of knowledge fell away, by aspects:
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to inferring God's knowledge from the perfection of created things, followed by Razi's refutation. The objector argues that if precise actions require knowledge, then animals like bees and spiders, which construct intricate structures without tools, would be more knowledgeable than humans — which is absurd. Second, a precise act can sometimes come from an ignorant person, so it does not necessarily imply knowledge. Third, even if we grant some awareness, why must it be certain knowledge rather than mere conjecture? Razi's answer distinguishes his own proof from those based on motion and rest. He states that he has already proven that everything other than God is originated, regardless of category. Thus, God's creative act is by power and choice, which inherently requires awareness of what He creates. This suffices to establish that God is knowing, though not yet that He knows all things. Therefore, the objection does not apply to his approach.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
اختيار (choice)
محدث (originated)
علم (knowledge)
شعور (awareness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi's answer is a cop-out; he does not address the objection directly.
Correction: He shows the objection is only valid against a specific proof, not his own. His proof is based on contingency, not design.

Checkpoint
What is the foundation of Razi's proof for God's knowledge?
A) The precision of animal constructions
B) The argument from motion and rest
C) Everything other than God is originated (muhdath)
D) The presence of evil in the world

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof for God's knowledge rests on the contingency of all creation, which necessitates a knowing agent acting by power and choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are one of the things other than God—originated, contingent, and entirely dependent on His power and choice. When you make a decision, do you sense that your very existence is being sustained every moment by the Creator who acts by awareness and will, or do you function as if you are self-sufficient? What would it mean for you to live today fully aware that your next breath is an act of His creation?
?
Razi proves that God must have awareness of what He creates, yet you often act as if your actions and thoughts go unnoticed by Him. Do you truly believe that God is aware of every intention and deed you produce, even the ones you hide? If you took this awareness seriously, what would you change about how you speak, think, or spend your time?
?
The passage says that everything other than God is originated by power and choice, not by nature or compulsion. Does your relationship with God feel like a matter of choice—or do you sometimes treat your faith as a mechanical habit, something that happens to you without your free engagement? What choice will you make today to act as someone who is deliberately created and accountable to a knowing Creator?

The First Proof for Seeing Allah: The Verse and Its Interpretation
Razi presents Quran 75:22-23 as proof and argues that 'nazar' must mean sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof for the vision of Allah based on Quran 75:22-23 and his reasoning that the word 'nazar' must mean sight.

Core Idea
Razi argues that 'nazar' in the verse means sight, not just turning the pupil.

Razi begins his first proof by citing the Quranic verse that describes faces looking toward their Lord. He then addresses a potential ambiguity: does 'nazar' here mean literal sight or merely directing one's gaze? His answer establishes that it must mean sight, because turning the pupil toward God is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Razi's initial statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi directly cites the verse as proof.
The verse is the foundation of the argument.
2
Understand the two possible meanings of 'nazar'
Identify Razi's two possibilities: either 'nazar' means 'ru'ya' (sight) or it means turning the pupil. Recognize that the latter is impossible for God.
3
Grasp Razi's argument that the cause-effect relation forces the meaning of sight
Razi argues that 'nazar' is the cause of sight, so the term for the cause is used to mean the effect. Also, the purpose of turning the pupil is sight itself.
This is a metonymy (cause for effect).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس: في إقامة الدلالة على أن المؤمنين يرون الله تعالى يوم القيامة ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى: قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة : ٢٢-٢٣] فنقول : النظر إن كان هو الرؤية، فالمطلوب حاصل وإن كان عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي، فنقول: هذا في حق الله تعالى محال، فوجب حمل لفظة النظر على الرؤية إطلاقا للفظ السبب على المسبب، فإن النظر سبب للرؤية، ولأن المقصود من تقليب الحدقة إنما هو الرؤية. فإن قيل : لا نسلم أنه حصل في هذه الآية لفظ النظر مقرونا بحرف إلى وقوله إلى ربها نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة : ٢٣] فلا نسلم أن (إلى) ههنا من حروف الجر، بل هو عندنا اسم. وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : أنه واحد الآلاء، على ما ذكره (الأزهري) في كتاب (التهذيب) قال الشاعر وهو (الأعشى) : أبيض لا يرهب الهزال ولا يقطع رحما، ولا يخون إلى أي لا يخون نعمه. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنا توافقنا على أن لفظ (ناظرة) إذا كان عاريا عن حرف (إلى) أفاد معنى الانتظار، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَنَاظِرَةٌ بِمَ يَرْجِعُ الْمُرْسَلُونَ﴾ [النَّمل : ٣٥] إذا عرفت هذا فتقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدير الآية وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظرة [القيامة : ٢٢ - ٢٣] أي نعمة ربها منتظرة؟ الثاني : إن لفظ ( إلي ) جاء بمعنى (عند)، قال الشاعر وهو (أوس) : فهل لكم فـيـمـا إلـي فإنني طبيب بما أعيي النطاسي حذيما؟ أي فهل لكم فيما عندي؟ إذا ثبت هذا فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدير الآية ورجوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةُ [القيامة : ٢٢] . عند ربها ثم قال بعد ذلك (ناظرة) أي منتظرة وهو خبر عن الوجوه والتقدير : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة عند ربها منتظرة نعمة ربها ؟ سلمنا : أنه حصل في هذه الآية لفظ النظر مقرونا بحرف إلي، لكن لا نسلم أنه
Chapter Five: On establishing the proof that the believers will see God Almighty on the Day of Resurrection. And it is indicated by several proofs: The first proof: His saying, Exalted: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking (nāẓira) toward their Lord' (75:22-23). So we say: If 'nazar' is sight (ru'ya), then the desired result is obtained. And if it is an expression for turning the pupil toward the direction of the visible object, then we say: This is impossible with respect to God Almighty, so the word 'nazar' must be interpreted as sight, by applying the name of the cause to the effect, for 'nazar' is the cause of sight, and because the purpose of turning the pupil is only sight. Then if it is said: We do not concede that in this verse the word 'nazar' occurs connected with the preposition 'ilā', and His saying 'ilā rabbihā nāẓira' [75:23] — we do not concede that 'ilā' here is a preposition; rather, in our view it is a noun. Its clarification is from two aspects: The first: That it is one of the 'ālā' (favours), as mentioned by (al-Azharī) in the book (al-Tahdhīb). The poet, who is (al-Aʿshā), said: 'White (skin), does not fear thinness, nor sever ties of kinship, nor betray 'ilā' — i.e., does not betray His favours.' If this is established, then we say: We have agreed that the word 'nāẓira' when devoid of the letter 'ilā' conveys the meaning of waiting, as in His saying: 'Then she is looking (nāẓira) to see what the messengers return with' (27:35). Once you know this, then why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of the verse is: 'Faces on that day are radiant; the favour of their Lord they are waiting for'? The second: That the word 'ilā' comes in the meaning of 'ʿinda' (with/near). The poet, who is (Aws), said: 'Do you have anything regarding what is with me (ilā)? For I am a physician for what baffles the clever doctor?' i.e., do you have anything regarding what is with me? If this is established, then why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of the verse is: 'Faces on that day are radiant, with their Lord,' then He said afterwards 'nāẓira' meaning 'waiting,' and it is the predicate of the faces, and the estimate: 'Faces on that day are radiant, with their Lord, waiting for the favour of their Lord'? We concede: that in this verse the word 'nazar' occurs connected with the letter 'ilā,' but we do not concede that...
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a proof and objection structure. Razi presents the first proof for the believers seeing Allah on the Day of Resurrection, based on Quran 75:22-23: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking toward their Lord.' Razi argues that if 'nazar' means 'ru'ya' (sight), then the proof is established. If it means turning the pupil, that is impossible for God, so it must be sight by a metonymy (cause for effect). Then an objection is raised: the word 'ilā' in the verse might not be a preposition but a noun meaning 'favour' (ālā') or meaning 'with/at' (ʿinda). The objector proposes two interpretations: first, reading 'ilā rabbihā' as 'the favour of their Lord' (with nāẓira meaning waiting), citing a line from al-Aʿshā where 'ilā' means 'favours,' and arguing that without the preposition 'ilā', the word 'nāẓira' means 'waiting,' as in Quran 27:35. Second, reading 'ilā' as 'ʿinda' (with), citing Aws's poetry, so the verse would mean 'faces radiant with their Lord, waiting for His favour.' Razi's response begins with 'We concede that in this verse the word nazar occurs with the letter ilā, but we do not concede that...' but the text cuts off. The incomplete response would presumably refute these alternative readings, maintaining that 'ilā' is a preposition and the meaning is literal looking/sight.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
رؤية (ru'ya)
تقليب الحدقة (taqlīb al-ḥadaqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'nazar' in the verse only means physical glancing or turning the pupil.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that this is impossible for God, so it must mean sight.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue is the meaning of 'nazar' in Quran 75:22-23?
A) Turning the pupil toward the seen object
B) Sight (ru'ya)
C) Waiting expectantly
D) Both A and B depending on context

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the verse and logical reasoning to establish that 'nazar' means sight, thus proving the believers will see Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة', do you truly long for that moment of seeing Allah? How does the hope of seeing Him shape your decisions today?
?
Razi argues that turning the pupil towards God is impossible because God is not in a direction. But have you been living as if God is far away or distant? What would it take for you to internalize that He is closer to you than your own self?
?
The verse describes faces radiant with joy. Is your face radiant with the anticipation of meeting your Lord? What needs to change in your daily worship and mindset for that radiance to become real and not just a future hope?

Objections to the Proof: Alternative Meanings of 'Ilā'
Razi records two objections interpreting 'ilā' as a noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections raised against the proof using 'ilā' as a noun meaning 'favour' or 'at'.

Core Idea
Objectors argue that 'ilā' in the verse may be a noun, not a preposition, changing the meaning to 'waiting for favour'.

After presenting his proof, Razi anticipates objections that challenge the grammatical reading of the verse. The objector proposes that the word 'ilā' is not a preposition but a noun meaning 'favour' or 'at', which would alter the entire meaning. This lesson explores these two interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection that 'ilā' is a noun meaning 'favours' (ālā')
Read the first part of the objection. The objector cites a poet to show that 'ilā' can mean 'favours'. Then, without the preposition, 'nāẓira' would mean 'waiting', as in another Quranic verse.
2
Understand the objection that 'ilā' means 'at' (ʿinda)
The objector provides another poetic example where 'ilā' means 'with/at'. This would mean the faces are 'with their Lord', waiting for His favour.
3
See how these reinterpretations would change the verse's meaning to 'waiting' rather than 'seeing'
Both objections aim to show that the verse does not necessarily indicate sight; it could be about waiting for blessings.
Razi's response is incomplete in this passage but will be addressed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس: في إقامة الدلالة على أن المؤمنين يرون الله تعالى يوم القيامة ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى: قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ﴾ [القيامة : ٢٢-٢٣] فنقول : النظر إن كان هو الرؤية، فالمطلوب حاصل وإن كان عبارة عن تقليب الحدقة إلى جهة المرئي، فنقول: هذا في حق الله تعالى محال، فوجب حمل لفظة النظر على الرؤية إطلاقا للفظ السبب على المسبب، فإن النظر سبب للرؤية، ولأن المقصود من تقليب الحدقة إنما هو الرؤية. فإن قيل : لا نسلم أنه حصل في هذه الآية لفظ النظر مقرونا بحرف إلى وقوله إلى ربها نَاظِرَةٌ [القيامة : ٢٣] فلا نسلم أن (إلى) ههنا من حروف الجر، بل هو عندنا اسم. وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : أنه واحد الآلاء، على ما ذكره (الأزهري) في كتاب (التهذيب) قال الشاعر وهو (الأعشى) : أبيض لا يرهب الهزال ولا يقطع رحما، ولا يخون إلى أي لا يخون نعمه. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنا توافقنا على أن لفظ (ناظرة) إذا كان عاريا عن حرف (إلى) أفاد معنى الانتظار، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَنَاظِرَةٌ بِمَ يَرْجِعُ الْمُرْسَلُونَ﴾ [النَّمل : ٣٥] إذا عرفت هذا فتقول لم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدير الآية وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظرة [القيامة : ٢٢ - ٢٣] أي نعمة ربها منتظرة؟ الثاني : إن لفظ ( إلي ) جاء بمعنى (عند)، قال الشاعر وهو (أوس) : فهل لكم فـيـمـا إلـي فإنني طبيب بما أعيي النطاسي حذيما؟ أي فهل لكم فيما عندي؟ إذا ثبت هذا فلم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدير الآية ورجوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةُ [القيامة : ٢٢] . عند ربها ثم قال بعد ذلك (ناظرة) أي منتظرة وهو خبر عن الوجوه والتقدير : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة عند ربها منتظرة نعمة ربها ؟ سلمنا : أنه حصل في هذه الآية لفظ النظر مقرونا بحرف إلي، لكن لا نسلم أنه

Misconception: Some may think that the only possible meaning of 'ilā' in the verse is the preposition 'toward'.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative meaning proposed for 'ilā' in the objection?
A) It means 'at' (ʿinda)
B) It means 'favours' (ālā')
C) It means 'toward' (as a preposition)
D) It means 'with' (maʿa)

Key Takeaway
The objections reinterpret 'ilā' to change the verse's meaning from seeing Allah to waiting for His favours, challenging the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the objector's two linguistic arguments, did you feel a temptation to dismiss them outright? Why? What does that reveal about your intellectual humility? How can you train yourself to consider challenging evidence honestly?
?
Think of a time when someone presented an alternative interpretation of a Quranic verse that contradicted your long-held understanding. How did you react? Did you seek to understand their reasoning, or did you defend your position? What would it take for you to change your mind on a belief you hold dear?

The objector relies on classical authorities like al-Azharī and established poets. In your own life, how do you ensure that your faith is built on solid knowledge rather than mere assumption? What specific step will you take this week to investigate a belief you have never questioned?

Three Types of Knowledge and Knowing God's Essence
Razi discusses three types of knowledge and whether God's essence can become known to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of knowledge Razi delineates and the debate over whether direct knowledge of God's essence is possible for humans.

Core Idea
God's essence can be known indirectly, but direct knowledge debates hinge on infinity.

Razi begins by distinguishing three levels of knowing: inferential, direct experiential, and sensory. He then applies this to whether humans can know God's essence, addressing both rational objections and the hopeful role of revelation. This passage introduces a classic theological puzzle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and distinguish three knowledge types
Identify the examples: burner for inferential, pain/pleasure for direct internal, and vision for sensory. Note the increasing clarity.
The first type is 'by way of consequence and accident', the second 'by essence and truth'.
2
Understand the application to God
Razi states that inferring the Maker from contingent things gives the first type of knowledge. The second and third types are debated.
3
Consider the debate on types two and three
Some deny possibility because God is infinite and the intellect finite. Others suspend judgment and rely on the revealed promise that believers will see God on the Day of Resurrection.
Razi does not take a side but prays to be worthy of that vision.
4

Razi concludes that no state is more honorable than these two higher degrees, and asks God for grace. This shows his hope despite rational difficulties.

The twentieth question: it is unknown [?] the knowledge of its being entailing the specific effects. And likewise here. And know that this inquiry produces great difficulties, the mention of which is not fitting in these places. The second chapter on clarifying whether the essence of the reality of God Almighty, even if not known now, can become known to humans? Know that when we see burning occurring in a body, we know that this burning must have a burner. And that burner is known, but our knowledge is by way of consequence and accident. For we do not know at this time what that burner is; rather it is something, determined in itself, unknown in its determination to the intellect. And from its necessary concomitants is the occurrence of this burning. Also, when we find in ourselves pain, pleasure, grief, and joy—these essences are known with a knowledge not by consequence and accident, but by essence and truth. And this degree is stronger and clearer than the first degree. Also, if we perceive by the eye a color and light, then close the eye, we perceive an intuitive distinction between the two states, while knowledge of that particular color is present in both states, and that distinction is called vision. If you understand this, we say: When we infer from the existence of contingent things the existence of the Maker, the first type of knowledge and cognition occurs. As for the second and third types, is their attainment possible? Some said: This is not possible because it (the object) is infinite and the intellect is finite, and perceiving the infinite by the finite is impossible. Among the verifiers, some suspended judgment regarding its permissibility and impossibility, saying: The intellect has no way to know these straits, but rather, since the transmitted evidence indicates that believers will see God Almighty on the Day of Resurrection, that indicates that these two degrees are possible. And there is no doubt that no state for humans is more honorable than these two states. So we ask God to make us, by His grace, worthy of them. And God is the Grantor of success and the Helper.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the possibility of knowing God's essence. Razi begins by mentioning a prior point (likely from earlier text) about knowledge of something being entailed by its effects, then moves to the main inquiry. He outlines three degrees of knowledge: first, inferential knowledge (e.g., knowing a burner from burning without knowing its identity); second, direct knowledge of internal states (pain, pleasure, etc.) known by essence; third, sensory knowledge (like vision) that provides an intuitive distinction. He then applies this to knowledge of God: inferential knowledge (type one) is attained by inferring the Maker from contingent things. The debate is over types two and three. Some deny their possibility because God is infinite and the intellect finite. Others suspend judgment, but rely on the transmitted evidence (sam‘) that the believers will see God on the Day of Resurrection, which proves these two degrees are possible. Razi concludes that no state is more honorable, and prays for divine grace.

Correction: Razi shows that some scholars deny possibility due to infinity, but others suspend judgment and rely on revelation that believers will see God, indicating possibility.

First Proof of Divine Unity: Composition Implies Contingency
Razi gives the first proof: two necessary beings would be composite, hence contingent, contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that only one necessary being can exist, because two would imply composition and thus contingency, which contradicts necessity.

Core Idea
If two necessary beings existed, they would be composite, hence contingent—a contradiction.

Imam al-Razi presents one of the most celebrated kalam arguments for divine unity. The proof moves from the assumption of two necessary beings to an impossibility by showing that any differentiation would introduce composition, making each contingent. This lesson unpacks the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently. Then read the English translation to get the gist of the argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: واجب الوجود لذاته, التركيب, etc.
2
Identify the initial assumption
The proof begins by supposing there are two things each necessary by itself. Understand why this is the starting point.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow how sharing necessity and differing by individuation leads to composition, and then how composition implies possibility. List each step in order.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Understand the contradiction
Realize that if the necessary becomes possible, that is impossible. Therefore the initial assumption must be false—only one necessary being exists.
The conclusion is that Allah is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية والعشرون في بيان أن صانع العالم سبحانه وتعالى واحد ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : لو كان في الوجود شيئان كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود لذاته لكانا مشتركين في الوجوب بالذات متباينين بالتعين فيلزم وقوع التركيب في ذات كل واحد منهما، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته ممكنا لذاته وهو محال . وتمام البحث فيه قد تقدم في مسألة حدوث الأجسام.
The twenty-first question: On clarifying that the Maker of the world, glorified and exalted, is one. And proofs indicate it. The first proof: If there existed two things each of which is necessary of existence by itself, they would share in necessity by essence and differ by individuation, necessitating the occurrence of composition in the essence of each. And every composite is possible by itself, so it would necessitate that the necessary by itself be possible by itself—and that is impossible. And the complete discussion of this has already preceded in the question of the origination of bodies.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's oneness based on the impossibility of two necessary beings. Razi states the first argument: if there were two entities each necessary of existence by itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi), they would share the attribute of necessity by essence (ishtirak fi al-wujub bi al-dhat) and differ by individuation (tabayun bi al-ta‘ayyun). This difference implies that each essence must have two aspects: a common element (necessity) and a differentiating element (individuation), resulting in composition (tarkib) within each. But every composite is possible by itself (mumkin li-dhatihi), meaning it relies on its parts. Thus the necessary by itself would be possible by itself—a contradiction. Therefore, only one necessary being can exist. Razi notes that the full treatment was given earlier in the discussion on the origination of bodies (huduth al-ajsam), likely referring to an argument against atomism or the composition of matter.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
الاشتراك في الوجوب بالذات
التباين بالتعين
التركيب
كل مركب ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two necessary beings could be distinct without implying any internal differentiation or composition.
Correction: Razi argues that any real distinction (other than mere conceptual) forces a composition in each essence, because each would have both the shared attribute of necessity and a unique individuating factor, making them composite and thus contingent.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, what would result if there were two necessary beings?
A) They would both be equally powerful
B) They would be completely identical
C) They would be composite, hence contingent, contradicting their necessity
D) They would be separate but both necessary

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof uses the concept of composition to show that the existence of more than one necessary being is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only Allah is truly self-sufficient and necessary, then everything else—including your health, wealth, and relationships—is contingent. Which created thing do you rely on most as if it were necessary, and how can you start relying solely on Allah for it today?
?
Razi argues that any composition implies need. Look at your own heart: what 'composites' of worldly attachments have you built that make you feel self-sufficient? How will you dismantle them to affirm Allah's oneness practically?
?
The proof shows that two necessary beings cannot coexist because each would have parts. Now consider your own life: are you trying to serve two masters—Allah and your desires? What step will you take this week to make your devotion single and undivided?
