The Second Proof of Divine Unity: The Impossibility of Conflicting Wills
Razi enumerates three possibilities for two gods with conflicting wills and explains their impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof for God's oneness, which shows that two gods with conflicting wills would lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot exist because their wills would conflict, leading to impossibility or impotence.

This lesson examines the second of Razi's proofs for tawhid, based on the nature of divine power. If two gods each willed opposite things, neither could achieve their will or only one could, but both scenarios are impossible. By analyzing this argument, you'll see how kalam uses logical reasoning to establish God's unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: Razi presents three possibilities and explains why each fails.
The passage is incomplete; the final conclusion is missing, but the reasoning is clear up to that point.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Razi considers three outcomes when two gods will opposites: both wills occur, neither occurs, or only one occurs. Find them in the text.
3
Understand the reasoning for impossibility
For each possibility, follow Razi's argument. The first is contradictory because both movement and rest cannot coexist. The second collapses into the first. The third is impossible due to impotence and equal capacity.
The second reason for the third possibility is explained in detail: the object of power is indivisible, so one power cannot be stronger.
4
Note the incomplete passage
The text cuts off during the third possibility. Based on the logic, what do you think the conclusion would be? Likely that the assumption of two gods leads to absurdity, proving oneness.
5
Reflect on the proof's significance
Consider how this logical argument supports the core Islamic belief in tawhid. It shows that reason alone can demonstrate the impossibility of multiple gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الإله هو الذي يكون قادرا على المقدورات. ولو فرضنا إلهين أراد أحدهما حركة زيد والآخر سكونه. فإما أن يحصل المرادان، وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مراد واحد منهما وذلك أيضا محال لأن المانع لكل واحد منهما عن حصول مراده ليس هو مجرد كون الآخر قادرًا، بل حصول مقدور الآخر. فإذن لا يمتنع حصول مراد هذا إلا إذا حصل مقدور ذاك ولا يمتنع حصول مراد ذاك إلا إذا حصل مقدور هذا . فلو حصل مراد كل واحد منهما لحصل مقدور كل واحد منهما. وحينئذ يرجع هذا القسم الثاني إلى القسم الأول. وقد بينا أنه محال . أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون الآخر. وهذا أيضا محال لوجهين: الأول : أن الذي حصل مراده فهو قادر والذي لم يحصل مراده فهو عاجز، والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية. والثاني : لما كانت لقدرة كل واحد منهما صلاحية التأثير في إيجاد ذلك المقدور. فنقول : إنه يستحيل أن يقال: إن إحدى هاتين الصلاحيتين أقوى الأخرى وذلك لأن المقدور شيء واحد لا يقبل القسمة أصلا وإذا كان كذلك، فهو إما أن من
The second proof: The god is one who is powerful over things that are possible. And if we suppose two gods, one willing Zayd's movement and the other his rest, then either both objects of will occur—and that is impossible—or neither object of will occurs—and that is also impossible, because the preventer for each of them from the occurrence of his object of will is not merely the other's being powerful, but rather the occurrence of the other's object of power. So the occurrence of this one's object of will is not prevented unless that one's object of power occurs, and the occurrence of that one's object of will is not prevented unless this one's object of power occurs. So if each one's object of will occurred, each one's object of power would occur. Then this second division reverts to the first division, and we have already shown that it is impossible. Or one's object of will occurs without the other. This too is impossible for two reasons: First, the one whose object of will occurred is powerful, and the one whose object of will did not occur is impotent, and the impotent is not fit for divinity. Second, since each one's power has the capacity to produce that object of power, we say: it is impossible to say that one of these two capacities is stronger than the other, because the object of power is a single thing that does not admit division at all. And if that is so, then it is either that... from
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is the second proof for God's oneness, based on the impossibility of two gods with conflicting wills. Razi begins by defining a god as one who is powerful (qadir) over possible things (maqdurat). He then presents a scenario: suppose two gods, one willing Zayd's movement and the other willing his rest. Three outcomes are possible: (1) both objects of will occur—impossible because a thing cannot be both moving and resting simultaneously; (2) neither occurs—but this reverts to the first case because each god's inability to achieve his will is not due to the other's mere power but to the actual occurrence of the other's object of power, so if neither occurs, it implies both objects of power (movement and rest) occur, contradiction; (3) only one god's will occurs. This is impossible for two reasons: the god whose will fails is impotent ('ajiz), and impotence is incompatible with divinity; and the two powers are equal in capacity over a single indivisible object, so neither can be stronger—thus it is impossible for one to prevail arbitrarily. The argument is cut off, but the conclusion is that the only coherent possibility is a single God.

Key Terms
قادر
المقدورات
المراد
العاجز
الصلاحية
لا يقبل القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proofs for God's oneness rely solely on revelation, not reason.
Correction: Razi uses logical reasoning (a priori) to demonstrate that multiple gods lead to absurdity, showing that reason alone can establish tawhid.

Checkpoint
In Razi's second proof, why is the third possibility (one god's will occurring) impossible according to the second reason?
A) Because the god whose will fails would be impotent, which is unfit for divinity
B) Because the two powers are equal over an indivisible object, so one cannot be stronger
C) Because the two gods would have to agree
D) Because the object of power is divisible

Key Takeaway
Razi's second proof demonstrates that logical consistency demands one divine will, hence only one God can exist.

If this proof is so clear, why do you find yourself sometimes acting as if there are multiple powers influencing your life—like fear of people or attachment to material things? How can you let this logical certainty transform your daily reliance on Allah alone?
?
The proof shows that any conflict of will leads to impotence. When you look at your own life, where do you see conflicting wills within yourself that make you spiritually impotent? How can you submit your will to the One God?

The First Proof for God's Oneness: The Argument from Composition
Razi presents a reductio ad absurdum proof for tawḥīd based on composition and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that establishes the oneness of God by showing that the existence of two necessary beings leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
Two necessary beings would imply composition, which contradicts necessary existence.

Imam Razi begins his proofs for God's oneness with a concise logical argument. This argument shows that if there were two necessary existents, they would have to be composite, but every composite is possible, not necessary — an impossibility. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its reliance on earlier discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
Notice the compact style: premise, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify the assumption and reasoning
Find the initial assumption (two necessary existents), the intermediate steps (sharing necessity, differing in individuation → composition), and the final conclusion (contradiction).
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect composition to possibility
Understand why every composite is possible by itself (depends on parts). Then see how this contradicts necessary existence.
This step relies on earlier discussion of composition in bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية والعشرون
في بيان أن صانع العالم سبحانه وتعالى واحد
ويدل عليه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : لو كان في الوجود شيئان كل واحد منهما واجب الوجود
لذاته لكانا مشتركين في الوجوب بالذات متباينين بالتعين فيلزم وقوع التركيب في ذات كل واحد منهما، وكل مركب فهو ممكن لذاته فيلزم أن يكون الواجب لذاته
ممكنا لذاته
وهو محال .
وتمام البحث فيه قد تقدم في مسألة حدوث الأجسام.
The twenty-first issue: On explaining that the Maker of the world, glory be to Him and exalted is He, is one. And there are proofs indicating it. The first proof: If there existed in existence two things each of which is necessary of existence by itself, they would share in necessity by essence and be distinct by individuation, so composition would follow in the essence of each of them. And every composite is possible by itself, so it would follow that the necessary by itself is possible by itself, and that is impossible. And the complete discussion of it has already preceded in the issue of the temporality of bodies.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the oneness of God (tawḥīd). It is a definition-and-proof passage that uses a reductio ad absurdum argument. The proof begins by assuming the existence of two necessary existents (wājib al-wujūd bi-dhātihā). Such two would share the attribute of necessary existence but differ in their individuation (taʿayyun). This difference implies composition (tarkīb) in each essence, because each would have a common attribute (necessity) and a distinguishing attribute (individuation). However, anything composite is possible (mumkin) by itself, since it depends on its parts. Therefore, the necessary being would be possible, which is a contradiction. Hence, two necessary existents cannot exist—God is one. The proof references an earlier discussion on the temporality of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajsām) for a fuller treatment of composition and possibility.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن لذاته
مركب
التعين
الوجوب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that composition is inherently impossible for God, so any composition shows that God cannot exist.
Correction: The argument does not assume composition is impossible for God; rather, it shows that composition entails possibility, which contradicts the very definition of necessary existence. The impossibility is in the contradiction, not in composition itself.

Checkpoint
Why does the first proof conclude that it is impossible for two necessary existents to exist?
A) Because they would necessarily disagree and cause conflict.
B) Because they would share in necessity but differ in individuation, leading to composition, which implies possibility.
C) Because each would be incapable of creating the other.
D) Because they would both be eternal and there is no room for two eternals.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for God's oneness shows that the existence of two necessary beings leads to a contradiction because they would be composite and hence possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that nothing can be both necessary and composite. Is there any created thing (a person, a possession, a status) that you treat as if it were necessary for your happiness? What would it take for you to recognize its possibility and release your attachment?
?
The proof begins with the assumption of two necessary beings and ends with contradiction. In your daily life, do you ever act as if there are two ultimate authorities—Allah and something else? What concrete step can you take today to remove that 'composition' from your heart and make Allah the sole necessary focus?
?
Razi uses a reductio: the impossibility of two necessary beings is proven by showing it leads to an absurdity. Are there areas of your faith where you hold contradictions (e.g., saying you rely on Allah but worrying excessively)? How can you apply the logical rigor of this proof to purify your own beliefs and actions?

Setting Up the Proof of Mutual Opposition
Razi introduces the scenario and analyzes the first two cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of two contradictory events occurring together or neither occurring disproves the existence of two gods.

Core Idea
Two gods willing opposite things leads to an unavoidable contradiction.

Imam Razi begins his second proof by establishing a simple but powerful scenario. If two gods existed and willed opposite things for the same object, we would face a logical dead end. This lesson explores the first two possible outcomes and shows why they are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the opening definition and the hypothetical scenario. Note how the author defines 'god' as omnipotent and sets up the conflict.
Focus on the logical structure: if both objects occur, that's a contradiction; if neither occurs, we get a circular prevention.
2
Understand the first case
The first case says both objects occur. This is impossible because motion and rest cannot coexist in the same subject at the same time.
3
Understand the second case
The second case says neither object occurs. The proof shows this collapses into the first case because the only prevention is the other's object occurring.
Notice the chain: if neither occurs, each object is prevented by the other's occurrence, but if neither occurs, then neither prevents—leading to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : الإله هو الذي يكون قادرا على المقدورات. ولو فرضنا إلهين أراد أحدهما حركة زيد والآخر سكونه. فإما أن يحصل المرادان، وهو محال، أو لا يحصل مراد واحد منهما وذلك أيضا محال لأن المانع لكل واحد منهما عن حصول مراده ليس هو مجرد كون الآخر قادرًا، بل حصول مقدور الآخر. فإذن لا يمتنع حصول مراد هذا . إلا إذا حصل مقدور ذاك ولا يمتنع حصول مراد ذاك، إلا إذا حصل مقدور هذا . فلو حصل مراد كل واحد منهما لحصل مقدور كل واحد منهما. وحينئذ يرجع هذا القسم الثاني إلى القسم الأول. وقد بينا أنه محال . أو يحصل مراد أحدهما دون الآخر.
وهذا أيضا محال لوجهين
الأول : أن الذي حصل مراده. فهو قادر والذي لم يحصل مراده، فهو عاجز، والعاجز لا يصلح للإلهية.
والثاني : لما كانت لقدرة كل واحد منهما صلاحية التأثير في إيجاد ذلك المقدور. فنقول : إنه يستحيل أن يقال: إن إحدى هاتين الصلاحيتين أقوى الأخرى وذلك لأن المقدور شيء واحد لا يقبل القسمة أصلا وإذا كان كذلك، فهو إما أن يكون موجودا أو معدوما. فأما القسم الثالث. وهو أن يقال: إنه يصير بعضه موجودًا وبعضه معدوما . فذلك محال . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لم يكن إيجاد مثل هذا المقدور قابلا للتفاوت وإذا لم يقبل التفاوت امتنع كون إحدى القدرتين أقوى من الأخرى.
إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : لما حصل التساوي في قدرة هذين القادرين، فلو حصل أحدهما دون الثاني، لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد المتساويين على الآخر، من غير مرجع البتة وذلك محال. فكان هذا القسم محالا . فثبت أن القول بوجود إلهين، يفضي إلى أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ولما ثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال باطل كان القول بوجود إلهين محالا باطلا.
The second proof: The god is the one who is powerful over all things that are objects of power (al-maqdūrāt). And if we suppose two gods, one wanting Zayd's motion and the other wanting his rest, then either both objects will occur—which is impossible—or neither of the two objects will occur—which is also impossible, because the preventer for each of them from the occurrence of his object is not merely the other's being powerful, but rather the occurrence of the other's object. So the occurrence of the object of this one is not prevented unless the object of that one occurs, and the occurrence of the object of that one is not prevented unless the object of this one occurs. Thus if the object of each were to occur, the object of each would occur, and then this second case reverts to the first case, which we have shown to be impossible—or [it means that] the object of one occurs without the other. This too is impossible for two reasons: First, the one whose object occurs is powerful (qādir), and the one whose object does not occur is incapable (ʿājiz), and the incapable is not fit for divinity. Second, since the power (qudra) of each has the capacity to bring about that object (al-maqdūr), we say: it is impossible to say that one of these two capacities is stronger than the other, because the object is a single thing which does not admit of division at all. And if that is so, then it is either existent or nonexistent. As for the third case—that it becomes partly existent and partly nonexistent—that is impossible. And if the matter is so, then bringing about such an object is not susceptible to variation, and if it is not susceptible to variation, it is impossible for one of the two powers to be stronger than the other. Once this is established, we say: since equality holds between the powers of these two powerful beings, if one [object] occurs without the other, this would be a preference of one equal over the other with no basis at all, and that is impossible. So this case is impossible. Thus it is established that the claim of the existence of two gods leads to one of these three cases, and since it is established that each of them is impossible and false, the claim of the existence of two gods is impossible and false.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction showing the impossibility of two gods (ilāhīn). It is an argument from mutual opposition (burhān al-tamānuʿ). The proof begins by defining God as omnipotent over all things (qādir ʿalā al-maqdūrāt). It then sets up a scenario: suppose two gods, one wills Zayd's motion, the other wills his rest. Three outcomes are possible: (1) both objects occur—impossible because they are contradictory; (2) neither object occurs—impossible because the only preventer would be the occurrence of the other's object, leading to a vicious circle; this case collapses into the first; (3) one object occurs without the other. This third case is also impossible for two reasons. First, the god whose object occurs is powerful, the other is incapable, and incapability disqualifies one from divinity. Second, since the object is unitary indivisible, the two powers are perfectly equal—neither can be stronger. If one object occurs rather than the other, it would be an arbitrary preference of equals with no basis, which is impossible. Thus all three possibilities are impossible, so the assumption of two gods is false.

Key Terms
قادر
مقدور
إله
التمانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes gods must disagree.
Correction: The proof shows that even the possibility of disagreement (given omnipotence) leads to contradiction; they need not actually disagree.

Checkpoint
Why does the second case (neither object occurs) revert to the first case?
A) Because if neither occurs, each god's object is prevented by the other's occurrence, but since neither occurs, no prevention happens, so both should occur.
B) Because the two gods would agree to cancel each other.
C) Because the objects are not real.
D) Because the scenario is impossible from the start.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of both or neither object occurring shows that the assumption of two gods with opposite wills leads to a logical dead end.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your heart, do you ever act as if there are competing authorities in your life—people, desires, or fears—that have ultimate say over your actions? How does this proof challenge the idea that you can serve two masters and yet avoid contradiction?
?
If you truly believed that only One God has absolute power and will, would you still worry about the opinions of others as if they could override His decree? What would change in your daily decisions if you internalized the impossibility of any real opposition to Allah's will?
?
The proof shows that even the logical possibility of two gods is absurd. In your own life, when you find yourself torn between two competing commitments, do you seek to reconcile them or do you recognize that one must ultimately submit to the other? How does this argument expose the futility of trying to please both Allah and your own ego?

The Third Case and Its Two Refutations
Razi refutes the third case with two arguments regarding incapability and equal powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third possibility (one god's object occurs) is impossible due to the equality of powers and the unfitness of incapability for divinity.

Core Idea
If one god's will is fulfilled, the other is incapable—and incapability negates divinity.

Having dismissed the first two outcomes, Imam Razi now tackles the remaining possibility: one god's object occurs while the other's does not. He provides two devastating reasons why this too is impossible. This lesson unpacks those reasons, showing how they rely on the indivisibility of the object and the equality of powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the third case and first reason
Read from 'or the object of one occurs without the other' to the end of the first reason. Note how the first reason points out that the god whose object does not occur would be incapable.
Incapability contradicts the definition of god as omnipotent.
2
Analyze the second reason
The second reason argues that since the object is single and indivisible, the two powers are perfectly equal. There is no basis to prefer one over the other.
This is a more subtle argument based on the nature of the object and power.
3
Connect to the overall proof
Understand that all three cases are impossible, so the original assumption of two gods is false.
This completes the proof by contradiction.

Source Text

وهذا أيضا محال لوجهين

إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : لما حصل التساوي في قدرة هذين القادرين، فلو حصل أحدهما دون الثاني، لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد المتساويين على الآخر، من غير مرجع البتة وذلك محال. فكان هذا القسم محالا . فثبت أن القول بوجود إلهين، يفضي إلى أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ولما ثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال باطل كان القول بوجود إلهين محالا باطلا.
The second proof: The god is the one who is powerful over all things that are objects of power (al-maqdūrāt). And if we suppose two gods, one wanting Zayd's motion and the other wanting his rest, then either both objects will occur—which is impossible—or neither of the two objects will occur—which is also impossible, because the preventer for each of them from the occurrence of his object is not merely the other's being powerful, but rather the occurrence of the other's object. So the occurrence of the object of this one is not prevented unless the object of that one occurs, and the occurrence of the object of that one is not prevented unless the object of this one occurs. Second, since the power (qudra) of each has the capacity to bring about that object (al-maqdūr), we say: it is impossible to say that one of these two capacities is stronger than the other, because the object is a single thing which does not admit of division at all. And if that is so, then it is either existent or nonexistent. As for the third case—that it becomes partly existent and partly nonexistent—that is impossible. And if the matter is so, then bringing about such an object is not susceptible to variation, and if it is not susceptible to variation, it is impossible for one of the two powers to be stronger than the other. Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction showing the impossibility of two gods (ilāhīn). It is an argument from mutual opposition (burhān al-tamānuʿ). The proof begins by defining God as omnipotent over all things (qādir ʿalā al-maqdūrāt). It then sets up a scenario: suppose two gods, one wills Zayd's motion, the other wills his rest. Three outcomes are possible: (1) both objects occur—impossible because they are contradictory; (2) neither object occurs—impossible because the only preventer would be the occurrence of the other's object, leading to a vicious circle; this case collapses into the first; (3) one object occurs without the other. This third case is also impossible for two reasons. First, the god whose object occurs is powerful, the other is incapable, and incapability disqualifies one from divinity. Second, since the object is unitary indivisible, the two powers are perfectly equal—neither can be stronger. If one object occurs rather than the other, it would be an arbitrary preference of equals with no basis, which is impossible. Thus all three possibilities are impossible, so the assumption of two gods is false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third case is possible because one god might be stronger.
Correction: But the object is indivisible, so both powers are equal; neither can be stronger.

Checkpoint
According to the second reason, why is it impossible for one power to be stronger than the other?
A) Because the object is divisible into parts.
B) Because the object is single and indivisible, so bringing it about is not susceptible to variation.
C) Because one god is stronger by nature.
D) Because the gods are actually the same.

Key Takeaway
The third case fails because one god would be incapable and because equal powers cannot arbitrarily prefer one object over the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the logical proof shows that only One truly has power, then every time I rely on a created being as if it had independent control, I am acting against this truth. What specific person or thing do I trust in my heart more than Allah? This week, consciously remind yourself that their power is only by His leave.
?
The argument proves that Allah cannot be incapable. Yet how often do I assume that my dua will not be answered, as if Allah is somehow unable? Ask yourself: Do I truly believe in His absolute power, or do I limit Him in my mind? Repent and pray with certainty.
?
If Allah's actions are based on perfect wisdom—not arbitrary preference—then every trial and blessing has a reason. What situation in your life are you struggling to accept as part of His wise plan? Commit to saying, 'I trust that Your choice is best.'

Objection and Answer: Two Gods Without Disagreement
Imam Razi answers the objection that two gods could exist without disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two gods is rationally impossible, even if they never disagree.

Core Idea
Two gods, even in perfect harmony, are impossible due to wisdom and the singularity of the best action.

Imam Razi continues his proof of divine oneness by addressing a subtle objection. The objector suggests that two gods could coexist if they never disagreed. Razi counters with two arguments: first, disagreement is actually unavoidable; second, even if it were avoidable, the attribute of wisdom would compel both gods to will exactly the same single best action, making one of them superfluous. This lesson unpacks both responses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection carefully: why does the objector think two gods could exist without disagreement? Note that he claims the previous proof assumes disagreement must occur.
The objector is trying to find a loophole by postulating perfect harmony.
2
Analyze the first response: disagreement is inevitable
Razi's first argument shows that if each god alone could will opposite actions (e.g., motion vs. rest), and their attributes are eternal and unchanging, then together they still retain those capacities, leading to potential disagreement. Trace this reasoning step by step.
This argument uses the principle that eternal attributes do not change.
3
Examine the second response: wisdom forces identical wills
Even if disagreement were avoided, each god must be wise, and the wise always chooses the single best action. Since the best action in any situation is unique, both gods would will the same thing, eliminating disagreement but implying one god is redundant.
This argument is more direct: it shows harmony leads to a different problem.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these two responses together close the loophole. The first makes disagreement likely, the second shows that even without disagreement, the existence of two gods is rationally untenable.
Note that the second argument is incomplete in the text; only the first of two points is given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذه الأقسام الثلاثة متفرعة على وقوع المخالفة بين الإلهين. فنقول : لم لا يجوز وجود الإلهين، بحيث يمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما، فعليكم أن تدلوا على صحة هذه المخالفة ولا يقال : الذي يدل على صحة وقوع المخالفة بينهما هو أنا لو قدرنا أحد هذين الإلهين منفردا بالوجود لصح منه أن يريد حركة زيد . ولو قدرنا الإله الثاني منفردا بالوجود، لصح منه أن يريد سكون زيد وإذا ثبت هذا حال الانفراد، وجب أيضا حال الاجتماع لأن ما لكل واحد من الذات والصفات. قديم. والقديم لا يجوز عليه التغير والتبدل. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل واحد منهما حال الاجتماع، كما كان حال الانفراد.
وهذا يوجب القطع بجواز المخالفة؛ لأنا نقول : أليس أنه لو انفرد أحد الإلهين لصح منه إيجاد الحركة. ولو انفرد الثاني لصح منه إيجاد السكون ثم إنهما لما اجتمعا تعذر على كل واحد منهما إيجاد ما لم يكن متعذرا عليه وقت الانفراد؟ وإذا فلم لا يجوز مثله كان الحال كذلك في القدرتين، في الإرادتين؟ سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم يدل على جواز المخالفة بينهما ، لكن معنا ما يدل على أن مع القول بوجود الإلهين يمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما . وهو من وجهين :
أحدهما : هو أنا لو فرضنا إلهين لوجب كون كل واحد منهما حكيما . والحكيم هو الذي لا يفعل إلا الأفضل والأولى. ولا شك أن الأفضل والأولى في كل شيء واحد.
وإذا كان كذلك، كان كل واحد منهما لكونه حكيما، لا يريد إلا ذلك الوجه الواحد. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك امتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما.
If it is said: These three cases are dependent on the occurrence of disagreement (al-mukhālafa) between the two gods. So we say: Why is it not permissible for two gods to exist in such a way that disagreement between them is impossible? So you must demonstrate the validity of this disagreement. And it should not be said: What demonstrates the validity of the occurrence of disagreement between them is that if we suppose one of these two gods existing alone, it would be possible for him to will Zayd's motion, and if we suppose the second god existing alone, it would be possible for him to will Zayd's rest. And if this is established in the state of solitude, it must also be the case in the state of conjunction, because whatever belongs to each of them in terms of essence and attributes is eternal (qadīm), and the eternal does not admit of change and alteration. This necessitates that each of them in the state of conjunction be as he was in the state of solitude. And this necessitates certainty in the permissibility of disagreement; for we say: Is it not that if one of the two gods were alone, it would be possible for him to bring about motion, and if the second were alone, it would be possible for him to bring about rest, then when they are together, each becomes unable to bring about what was not impossible for him at the time of solitude? Why is it not permissible for the same to be the case in powers and wills? We grant that what you mentioned indicates the permissibility of disagreement between them, but we have what indicates that with the claim of the existence of two gods, the occurrence of disagreement between them is impossible. And it is from two arguments: One of them is that if we suppose two gods, each of them must be wise (ḥakīm). And the wise is one who does nothing but the best and most excellent (al-afḍal wa-l-awlā). There is no doubt that the best and most excellent in everything is one. And if that is so, each of them, being wise, wills only that single aspect. And if that is so, the occurrence of disagreement between them is impossible.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector claims that the three impossibilities in the second proof depend on the assumption that two gods would disagree. So why can't two gods exist in perfect harmony without any disagreement? The response first argues that disagreement is inevitable: if each god alone could will opposite things, and their attributes are eternal and unchanging, then even when together they retain those capacities, so disagreement is possible. Then the responder concedes that the objector's argument shows the permissibility of disagreement, but provides an independent argument proving that with two gods, disagreement is impossible anyway. The argument is that each god must be wise (ḥakīm), and the wise always chooses the single best action. Since the best action in any matter is unique, both gods would necessarily will the same thing, making disagreement impossible. This, however, does not solve the problem—if they both will the same thing, then one of them is redundant, and the original proof's third case (one object occurs) would still lead to the same issues. Note: the second argument is missing from the text window.

Key Terms
مخالفة
انفراد
اجتماع
قديم
حكيم
الأفضل والأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could avoid all problems by always agreeing and never conflicting.
Correction: Perfect agreement is impossible because each god's wisdom forces them both to will the same unique best action, which reduces one to a redundancy, not a solution.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's second argument, why is disagreement between two gods impossible?
A) Because their attributes change when together.
B) Because each god, being wise, wills only the single best action, which is unique.
C) Because they would not have free will.
D) Because the objector's argument fails to prove harmony.

Key Takeaway
The existence of two gods is rationally impossible whether they disagree or agree perfectly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if there are multiple 'best' paths for your life, serving conflicting desires, when you know that Allah's wisdom demands a single best action in every situation? Will you commit to seeking that one best action through the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The argument shows that two gods cannot coexist because each would necessarily will the same best action. In your own heart, do you try to serve both Allah and your whims, as if they could agree? Which 'god' will you renounce today?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone is the Wise who chooses the best, why do you still doubt His decrees? How can you train your soul to accept that His choice is always the singular best, even when it is difficult?

The Third Division: Why Two Gods Cannot Share Creation
Imam Razi proves two gods impossible by three absurd consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third division in Imam Razi's proof that two gods are impossible, and how it leads to the final conclusion.

Core Idea
If two gods were equal, neither could act without the other—impossible.

Imam Razi completes his proof against the existence of two gods by examining the third division: that a single act of creation could be split between two deities. He shows that this is impossible because it would imply variation in power or an arbitrary preference. This final argument seals the case for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Razi begins with the third division and then builds his argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the third division
The third division is that an object of power (maqdur) is partially created by one god and partially by the other. Razi calls this impossible.
Why is it impossible? Because such an object would not admit variation.
3
Follow the equality argument
If the two powers are equal, their objects cannot vary in strength. This means neither power can be stronger, so any action by one over the other would be arbitrary.
Arbitrary preference (tarjeeh) without a cause is impossible.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Since all three divisions are impossible, the existence of two gods is impossible. This proves the oneness of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما القسم الثالث. وهو أن يقال: إنه يصير بعضه موجودا وبعضه معدوما . فذلك محال . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لم يكن إيجاد مثل هذا المقدور قابلا للتفاوت وإذا لم يقبل التفاوت امتنع كون إحدى القدرتين أقوى من الأخرى. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول : لما حصل التساوي في قدرة هذين القادرين، فلو حصل أحدهما دون الثاني، لكان هذا ترجيحا لأحد المتساويين على الآخر، من غير مرجع البتة وذلك محال. فكان هذا القسم محالا . فثبت أن القول بوجود إلهين، يفضي إلى أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ولما ثبت أن كل واحد منهما محال باطل كان القول بوجود إلهين محالا باطلا.
As for the third division: that it is said that some of it becomes existent and some non-existent. That is impossible. And if the matter is so, then the bringing into existence of such an object of power is not susceptible to variation. And if it does not admit variation, then it is impossible for one of the two powers to be stronger than the other. If this is established, we say: since equality occurred in the power of these two powerful agents, if one of them occurs without the other, this would be a preponderance of one of the equals over the other without any reference at all, and that is impossible. So this division is impossible. Thus it is established that the statement of the existence of two gods leads to one of these three divisions. And since it is established that each of them is impossible and false, the statement of the existence of two gods is impossible and false.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is the final part of Imam Razi's proof against the existence of two gods. He presents the third division, in which a single object of power (maqdur) would be partially created by one god and partially by the other. This is impossible because such an object would not admit variation in its creation—if it did, one power would be stronger, which contradicts equality. Then he argues that if the two powers are equal, it is impossible for one to act without the other without a preponderator (murajjih), which is absurd. Thus all three divisions are impossible, so the existence of two gods is impossible.

قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument assumes conflict between two gods, but it shows inherent logical impossibility even without conflict.
Correction: The argument does not require conflict; it shows that the very concept of two equal, independent gods leads to absurdity in the act of creation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows from the assumption that two gods exist?
A) They must agree on everything.
B) One of three impossible divisions must occur.
C) Their powers are unequal.
D) They cannot create anything.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi proves that the existence of two gods leads to logical absurdities, thereby affirming the oneness of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In what specific area of your life do you need to shift your trust from others to Allah alone, and what step will you take to do that right now?
?
The argument shows that any preponderance without cause is impossible. Reflect on your own choices: when you favor one thing over another, what cause underlies it? Are you aware that Allah's choices are never without wisdom? How does this realization change the way you respond to His decrees that you do not understand?

The Necessity of Conflict Between Two Gods
Imam Razi responds to the objection that two gods could avoid conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that conflict between two gods is unavoidable, based on the principle of divine immutability.

Core Idea
If each god alone could will opposite actions, their unchanging nature makes conflict necessary when together.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses an objection to the argument against multiple gods. The objector suggests that two gods could coexist without ever conflicting. Razi responds by showing that given each god's ability to will opposite actions in isolation, and the eternal unchanging nature of their attributes, conflict must be possible when they are together. Let's examine this powerful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the objector's challenge is placed first, then Razi's answer.
The objector says: why can't two gods exist without conflict?
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that the three divisions only apply if conflict occurs, but perhaps conflict can be avoided.
3
Understand Razi's reasoning
Razi argues from isolation to union: if each god alone could will opposite actions (e.g., movement vs. stillness of Zayd), and since their attributes are eternal and unchanging, they retain those wills when together, making conflict possible.
Key principle: the eternal cannot change.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that this proves conflict must be possible, which then supports the three divisions he previously set out.
This argument applies to both divine powers and wills.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذه الأقسام الثلاثة متفرعة على وقوع المخالفة بين الإلهين. فنقول : لم لا يجوز وجود الإلهين، بحيث يمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما، فعليكم أن تدلوا على صحة هذه المخالفة ولا يقال : الذي يدل على صحة وقوع المخالفة بينهما هو أنا لو قدرنا أحد هذين الإلهين منفردا بالوجود لصح منه أن يريد حركة زيد . ولو قدرنا الإله الثاني منفردا بالوجود، لصح منه أن يريد سكون زيد وإذا ثبت هذا حال الانفراد، وجب أيضا حال الاجتماع لأن ما لكل واحد من الذات والصفات. قديم. والقديم لا يجوز عليه التغير والتبدل. وهذا يقتضي أن يكون كل واحد منهما حال الاجتماع، كما كان حال الانفراد. وهذا يوجب القطع بجواز المخالفة؛ لأنا نقول : أليس أنه لو انفرد أحد الإلهين لصح منه إيجاد الحركة. ولو انفرد الثاني لصح منه إيجاد السكون ثم إنهما لما اجتمعا تعذر على كل واحد منهما إيجاد ما لم يكن متعذرا عليه وقت الانفراد؟ وإذا فلم لا يجوز مثله كان الحال كذلك في القدرتين، في الإرادتين؟
If it is said: These three divisions are dependent on the occurrence of conflict between the two gods. We say: Why is it not permissible for two gods to exist in such a way that the occurrence of conflict between them is impossible? So you must prove the validity of this conflict. And it is not said: What indicates the validity of the occurrence of conflict between them is that if we suppose one of these two gods alone existing, it would be valid for Him to will the movement of Zayd, and if we suppose the second god alone existing, it would be valid for Him to will the stillness of Zayd. And if this is established in the case of isolation, then it must also be the case in the state of union, because what each has of essence and attributes is eternal, and change and alteration are not permissible upon the eternal. This requires that each of them in the state of union be as he was in the state of isolation. And this necessitates the decisive judgment of the permissibility of conflict; for we say: Is it not that if one of the two gods were alone, it would be valid for Him to bring about movement, and if the second were alone, it would be valid for Him to bring about stillness? Then when they came together, each became unable to bring about what was not impossible for him at the time of isolation? So why is it not permissible that the situation be similar with respect to the two powers and the two wills?
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that the three divisions only follow if conflict occurs between the gods, but perhaps they can coexist without conflict. Imam Razi responds by arguing that conflict must be possible. He reasons that if each god were alone, each would be able to will opposite actions (e.g., movement vs. stillness of Zayd). Since their essence and attributes are eternal and unchanging, when they come together, each must retain the same will it had in isolation. Therefore, it is possible for them to will opposite things, making conflict possible. The author then emphasizes that this argument applies to both divine powers (qudra) and wills (irada).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist without conflict by simply avoiding opposing wills.
Correction: Razi proves that conflict must be possible because each god's will, being eternal and unchanging, remains the same in union as in isolation, making opposite wills possible.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of conflict between two gods is necessary because their eternal unchanging attributes would retain their independent wills even in union.

Possibility of Conflict Between Two Gods: Objection and Response
Imam Razi responds to the objection that only actual conflict leads to impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that the eternal possibility of conflict between two gods necessarily leads to actual conflict, and why this supports divine oneness.

Core Idea
Eternal possibilities cannot be removed, so conflict is inevitable.

This passage presents an objection to the proof of God's oneness: even if two gods could possibly conflict, actual conflict may not occur. Imam Razi responds by showing that the possibilities are eternal and must persist, leading to actual conflict. He also addresses a counterargument involving two powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation to understand the argument. Note that the text is cut off at the end.
The objector distinguishes between possibility and actuality.
2
Identify the objection and response
The objector claims that only actual conflict produces impossibilities, not the mere possibility. Razi's response begins with 'The proof for the validity of conflict...'. Identify the two parts.
3
Trace the proof of eternal possibilities
Razi gives two reasons: (1) each possibility is eternal and cannot cease, (2) if both possibilities contradict each other, neither can remove the other without absurdity. Follow this logic.
The 'two powers' counterexample is then refuted.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that the possibilities persist, so conflict is both possible and inevitable. The counterexample about two powers actually supports God's oneness.
The text cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is clear enough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه يصح وقوع المخالفة بينهما، لكن المحالات التي ألزمتمونا إنما تلزم من وقوع المخالفة، لا من صحة المخالفة فلما لم تثبتوا أن هذه المخالفة لا بد وأن تحصل وتدخل في الوجود لا يمكنكم أن تلزموا علينا تلك المحالات فأنتم وإن أقمتم الدلائل على صحة المخالفة، إلا أنكم تحتاجون بعدها إلى إقامة الدلالة على وقوع تلك المخالفة . حتى يتم دليلكم. والجواب : الدليل على صحة المخالفة بينهما أنه لو انفرد هذا صحت منه إرادة الحركة، ولو انفرد ذلك صحت منه إرادة السكون وعند اجتماعهما وجب القول ببقاء هاتين الصحتين ويدل عليه وجهان : منافاة. الأول : أن كل واحد من هاتين الصحتين أزلي والأزلي يمتنع زواله. والثاني : أن زوال إحدى الصحتين بالأخرى إنما يصح لو حصل بينهما نوع وإلا لم يلزم من حصول أحدهما زوال الثاني. وإذا حصلت المنافاة من الجانبين، لم يكن تأثير حصول أحدهما في عدم الآخر أولى من العكس. فلا بد وأن تزول كل واحدة من هاتين الصحتين بالأخرى. إلا أن هذا محال؛ لأن المؤثر في عدم كل واحدة من هاتين الصحتين وجود الأخرى والعلة واجبة الحصول عند حصول المعلول. فلو عدمت الصحتان معًا، لزم من عدمهما معًا، لزومهما معًا. وهذا محال . فعلمنا أنه لا تزول هذ الصحة، ولا تلك الصحة عند اجتماعهما فثبت أن صحة المخالفة حاصلة عند الاجتماع. وأما قوله : إن هذا ينتقض بالقدرتين فإنه يصح على كل واحدة منهما إيجاد أحد الضدين عند الانفراد ولا يصح منهما إيجادهما عند الاجتماع. قلنا : هذا عين دليلنا على كون الله تعالى واحدا لأنا نقول : هذه الصحة كانت ثابتة لكل واحدة من القدرتين عند الانفراد . فإذا بقيتا عند الاجتماع، يلزم منه صحة
We concede that the occurrence of conflict between them is valid [possible], but the impossibilities that you have required of us only follow from the actual occurrence of conflict, not from the validity [possibility] of conflict. Since you have not established that this conflict must necessarily occur and enter into existence, you cannot require those impossibilities from us. Even if you have established proofs for the validity of conflict, you still need, after that, to establish proof for the actual occurrence of that conflict, so that your proof is complete. And the answer: The proof for the validity of conflict between them is that if this one were alone, the volition of movement would be valid from him, and if that one were alone, the volition of stillness would be valid from him. And when they come together, it is necessary to affirm the persistence of these two validities [possibilities]. This is supported by two aspects: contradiction. First: that each of these two validities is eternal, and the eternal cannot cease. Second: that the removal of one validity by the other is only valid if there is a kind of contradiction between them; otherwise, the existence of one does not necessitate the removal of the other. And if contradiction obtains from both sides, then the effect of existence of one in the non-existence of the other is not more likely than the converse. So each of the two validities must be removed by the other. But this is impossible, because the efficient cause of the non-existence of each of these two validities is the existence of the other, and the cause is necessarily present when the effect is present. So if both validities were to cease, it would follow from their ceasing together that they exist together? That is impossible. So we know that neither this validity nor that validity ceases when they come together. Thus it is established that the validity of conflict is obtained at the time of union. As for his saying: This is refuted by the two powers, for it is valid for each of them to bring about one of the two opposites when alone, and it is not valid for them to bring about both when together. We say: This is the very proof of God being one. For we say: This validity was established for each of the two powers when alone. So if they persist when together, it would necessitate the validity of...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector says that the impossibilities (muhalat) follow only from actual conflict, not from the mere possibility (sihha). So even if conflict is possible, it might not occur, and the proof fails without demonstrating actual conflict. Imam Razi answers by proving that the possibility of conflict is eternal (azali) and cannot be removed. He argues that if each god alone could will opposite acts, those possibilities persist when together because they are eternal. Removing one possibility by the other would require a contradiction; but if both are contradictory, neither can eliminate the other without absurdity (both ceasing would be impossible). Therefore, the possibilities remain, so conflict is both possible and, given the persistence of opposite wills, inevitable—hence actual. He then addresses an objection about the two powers (qudra), showing that the same principle proves God's oneness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the author accepts the distinction between possibility and actuality without further argument, but he shows that eternal possibility must be actualized.
Correction: The author does not merely concede; he argues that eternal possibilities cannot remain merely possible—they must lead to actuality.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi argue that the possibility of conflict must lead to actual conflict?
A) By saying the objector concedes it.
B) By proving the possibilities are eternal and cannot cease, so they persist and actualize.
C) By invoking divine wisdom.
D) By showing the gods have equal power.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that the eternal nature of possibilities (sihha) ensures that conflict between two gods is both possible and inevitable, thereby reinforcing the proof of God's oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson argues that even the mere possibility of another god leads to logical absurdity. Do your convictions about tawhid rest on intellectual proofs like this, or do you feel a deeper certainty that transcends argument? Where, honestly, do you find your heart anchored—in logic or in direct awareness of Allah's sole sovereignty?
?
If eternal possibilities are unceasing, then your own soul has eternal implications. What eternal possibility (sihha) have you allowed to persist that contradicts what you know to be true? Is there a 'second god' of desire or ambition that you entertain as possible, even if not acted upon? Will you today declare that possibility null and void?
?
Razi's proof is exacting—it does not allow for coexistence of opposites. In your life, what contradictions do you tolerate that cannot logically coexist with your faith? What concrete step will you take this week to remove one such contradiction, so that your existence aligns with the oneness you claim to believe?

Second Proof: Two Gods Must Agree in Will
Razi presents a deductive argument from omniscience to agreement in will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that two gods necessarily will the same thing, and why conflict between them is impossible.

Core Idea
Omniscience forces all gods to will the same outcome.

Razi presents a sharp logical argument: if two gods exist, both are omniscient. Knowing exactly what will happen, they can only will what they know will occur. Since only one outcome is destined, both must will it. This eliminates any possibility of conflict.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Razi's second proof once to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the Arabic phrasing for 'the second aspect'.
2
Identify the premises
List the key premises: each god is omniscient, each knows which events will occur, and willing what is known not to occur is impossible.
3
Follow the logical chain
See how the premises lead to the conclusion: both gods must will the one specific event that will occur.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that conflict (المخالفة) is impossible between them. Reflect on why this matters for the oneness of God.
This argument uses only omniscience, not power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : هو أن كل واحد منهما، لما كان إلها وجب أن يكون كل واحد منهما عالما بكل المعلومات وكان كل واحد منهما عالما بأن أي المعلومات يقع؟ وأيها لا يقع ؟ وإرادة ما علم أنه لا يقع يكون محالا . وإذا كان الشيء الذي هو معلوم الوقوع، ليس إلا الواحد ويستحيل أن يريد إلا ما كان معلوم الوقوع، وجب أن يكون كل واحد منهما مريدا لوقوع شيء واحد بعينه. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يمتنع وقوع المخالفة بينهما.
The second aspect: That each one of them, since he is a god, must be knowing of all known things, and each one of them is knowing which of the known things occur and which do not occur. And willing what is known not to occur is impossible. And when the thing that is known to occur is only one, and it is impossible for him to will except what is known to occur, it is necessary that each one of them wills the occurrence of one specific thing. And upon this estimation, the occurrence of conflict between them is impossible.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (the second aspect) for the impossibility of two gods. It is a deductive argument. Razi reasons that if there were two gods, each would be omniscient (عالم بكل المعلومات). Consequently, each would know exactly which events will occur and which will not. Since willing what is known not to occur is impossible, each god can only will what will actually happen. Moreover, only one specific outcome is destined to occur. Therefore, both gods must will the same single outcome, making conflict between them impossible. The conclusion is that conflict cannot occur between two gods.

Key Terms
الوجه الثاني
كل واحد منهما
عالما بكل المعلومات
معلوم الوقوع
المخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument assumes both gods are equally powerful but does not address the possibility of different wills; they might think gods could disagree on what should happen.
Correction: Razi's argument does not rely on power but on omniscience. If both know the exact future, they cannot will anything other than that future, so disagreement is logically impossible.

Checkpoint

C) Because they would consult each other and agree
D) Because they have no free will

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that two gods cannot disagree because omniscience forces them to will the same actual outcome, making conflict impossible.

Possibility vs. Actuality of Conflict: Razi's Proof for God's Oneness
Razi refutes the objection that only actual conflict entails absurdity, proving possibility suffices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi proves that even the mere possibility of conflict between two gods leads to an impossibility, thereby establishing divine oneness.

Core Idea
Possibility alone, when eternal, necessitates actual contradiction — proving only one God can exist.

In this passage, Razi tackles a subtle objection: the absurdities of two gods only follow if they actually conflict, not if they merely could. Razi's reply unpacks why the possibility itself — being eternal and inseparable from each god's nature — already forces an impossible outcome. This argument is a cornerstone of Islamic kalam for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the entire passage to grasp the flow: the objector's challenge, then Razi's response and his two proofs.
Notice how Razi quickly concedes the possibility but then builds his case.
2
Examine the two proofs for persistence of validities
Focus on the two proofs Razi gives for why both 'validities' (the ability to will opposite actions) remain when gods coexist: eternality and impossibility of mutual cancellation.
Each proof is short but packed; write them in your own words.
3
Understand the counterexample and its refutation
Razi addresses an objection about two powers that cannot produce opposites together. He shows this actually supports his proof for oneness.
This step solidifies the lesson: the same logic that seems to refute him actually confirms his argument.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine of tawhid
Reflect on how this argument fits into the Islamic proof that God is one, and why possibility alone is sufficient to disprove multiple gods.
The key is that eternal attributes cannot be removed or overridden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه يصح وقوع المخالفة بينهما، لكن المحالات التي ألزمتمونا إنما تلزم من وقوع المخالفة، لا من صحة المخالفة فلما لم تثبتوا أن هذه المخالفة لا بد وأن تحصل وتدخل في الوجود لا يمكنكم أن تلزموا علينا تلك المحالات فأنتم وإن أقمتم الدلائل على صحة المخالفة، إلا أنكم تحتاجون بعدها إلى إقامة الدلالة على وقوع تلك المخالفة . حتى يتم دليلكم. والجواب : الدليل على صحة المخالفة بينهما أنه لو انفرد هذا صحت منه إرادة الحركة، ولو انفرد ذلك صحت منه إرادة السكون وعند اجتماعهما وجب القول ببقاء هاتين الصحتين ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : أن كل واحد من هاتين الصحتين أزلي والأزلي يمتنع زواله. والثاني : أن زوال إحدى الصحتين بالأخرى إنما يصح لو حصل بينهما نوع منافاة. وإلا لم يلزم من حصول أحدهما زوال الثاني. وإذا حصلت المنافاة من الجانبين، لم يكن تأثير حصول أحدهما في عدم الآخر أولى من العكس. فلا بد وأن تزول كل واحدة من هاتين الصحتين بالأخرى. إلا أن هذا محال؛ لأن المؤثر في عدم كل واحدة من هاتين الصحتين وجود الأخرى والعلة واجبة الحصول عند حصول المعلول. فلو عدمت الصحتان معًا، لزم من عدمهما معًا، لزومهما معًا. وهذا محال . فعلمنا أنه لا تزول هذ الصحة، ولا تلك الصحة عند اجتماعهما فثبت أن صحة المخالفة حاصلة عند الاجتماع. وأما قوله : إن هذا ينتقض بالقدرتين فإنه يصح على كل واحدة منهما إيجاد أحد الضدين عند الانفراد ولا يصح منهما إيجادهما عند الاجتماع. قلنا : هذا عين دليلنا على كون الله تعالى واحدا لأنا نقول : هذه الصحة كانت ثابتة لكل واحدة من القدرتين عند الانفراد . فإذا بقيتا عند الاجتماع، يلزم منه صحة الجمع بين الضدين. وهو محال وإن زالتا أو زالت إحداهما. فهو محال للوجوه التي ذكرناها. فعلم أن القول بوجود الإلهين يفضي إلى هذا المحال. فكان القول به محالا.
We grant that the occurrence of conflict between them is possible, but the absurdities that you have required of us are necessitated only by the occurrence of conflict, not by the possibility of conflict. Since you have not established that this conflict must necessarily occur and enter into existence, you cannot require those absurdities upon us. So even if you have established proofs for the possibility of conflict, you still need, afterwards, to establish proof for the occurrence of that conflict, so that your argument is complete. The answer: The proof for the possibility of conflict between them is that if this one were alone, the will to motion would be valid from him, and if that one were alone, the will to rest would be valid from him. And when they join together, it is necessary to say that these two validities remain. Two proofs indicate this: First: Each of these two validities is eternal, and the eternal is impossible to cease. Second: The cessation of one validity by the other is only valid if there is a type of opposition between them. Otherwise, the occurrence of one does not necessitate the cessation of the other. And if opposition occurs from both sides, then the effect of the occurrence of one in the non-existence of the other is not more likely than the opposite. So it is necessary that each of these two validities ceases by the other. However, this is impossible because the influencer in the non-existence of each of these two validities is the existence of the other, and the cause is necessarily present when the effect is present. So if both validities ceased together, the necessary consequence from both ceasing together would be that they are together. And this is impossible. Thus we know that neither this validity nor that validity ceases when they join together. So it is established that the possibility of conflict is present at the time of joining. And as for his statement: that this is refuted by the two powers, for it is valid for each of them to bring about one of the two opposites when alone, but not valid for both of them to bring them about when together. We say: This is exactly our proof for the oneness of God, for we say: This validity was established for each of the two powers when alone. So if they both remain when together, it necessitates the possibility of combining two opposites, which is impossible. And if they both cease, or one of them ceases, that is impossible for the reasons we mentioned. So it is known that the statement of the existence of two gods leads to this impossibility. So the statement of it is impossible.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. The objector concedes that conflict between two gods might be possible (صحة المخالفة) but argues that absurdities only follow from actual conflict (وقوع المخالفة), not from its mere possibility. He demands proof that conflict actually occurs. Razi's response proves that the possibility itself remains even when both gods coexist. He argues: If each god were alone, one could will motion, the other rest. When they are together, these individual validities (صحتان) cannot disappear because they are eternal (أزلي) and cannot cease. Moreover, for one to cancel the other, there would need to be a relation of opposition (منافاة) between them, but even if such opposition exists, it would not be one-sided; both would attempt to cancel each other, leading to mutual cancellation, which is impossible because the cause of each validity's cancellation would be the other validity's existence—but cause and effect must coexist. So both validities persist, meaning the possibility of conflict remains. Razi then addresses a potential counterexample: the case of two powers (قدرتان) where each can produce an opposite when alone but cannot when together because that would lead to combining opposites. Razi clarifies that this is exactly his proof for God's oneness: the coexistence of two omnipotent agents leads to the impossible scenario of combining opposites, thus the assumption of two gods is false.

Key Terms
المخالفة
صحة المخالفة
وقوع المخالفة
الصحتين
الأزلي
المنافاة
القدرتين
الجمع بين الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The possibility of conflict between two gods is harmless; only if they actually conflict does an absurdity arise.
Correction: Razi shows that the possibility itself is eternal and cannot be removed when gods coexist, leading necessarily to the impossible scenario of combining opposites (الجمع بين الضدين). Thus the mere possibility is sufficient to disprove multiple gods.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what crucial characteristic of each 'validity' (صحة) prevents it from ceasing when two gods join together?
A) It is eternal and cannot be destroyed
B) It is created and temporary
C) It is dependent on the other's permission
D) It is a non-essential attribute

Key Takeaway
The mere possibility of conflict between two eternal wills, when they coexist, forces an impossibility — proving that only one God can exist.

Razi's Refutation: Possibility Cannot Entail Absurdity
Razi refutes an objection about the necessity of actual conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that only actual conflict, not its possibility, leads to absurdity, and why the impossibility of conflict proves the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Possibility implies non-absurdity; actual absurdity proves impossibility.

Razi presents a crisp objection-and-answer. The opponent tries to escape the reductio by arguing that only actual conflict, not its mere possibility, generates absurdities. Razi counters with a certain premise: if something is possible, then imagining it actually happening cannot entail an impossibility. Since assuming conflict between two gods does lead to absurdity, conflict cannot be possible, and thus the hypothesis of two gods collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. The objector claims that only actual conflict (not its possibility) produces absurdities.
2
Identify the certain premise
Razi's premise: if something is possible, then assuming it occurs should not lead to an impossibility. This is key to his argument.
3
Understand the reductio
If conflict were possible, its actualization should not be absurd; but it is, so conflict is impossible. Notice the logical move.
4
See the conclusion
Razi concludes that both possibilities (conflict possible or impossible) are false, disproving the existence of two gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "هذه الأقسام الثلاثة إنما تتولد من حصول المخالفة لا من صحة المخالفة " . قلنا : ههنا مقدمة يقينية وهي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلو كانت المخالفة ممكنة لم يلزم من فرض وقوعها محال . لكنه قد لزم المحال من فرض وقوعها . وعند هذا نقول : لو فرضنا إلهين لكانت المخالفة بينهما. إما أن تكون ممكنة، وإما أن لا تكون ممكنة والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بوجود الإلهين.
His statement: 'These three categories arise only from the occurrence of conflict, not from the possibility of conflict.' We say: Here there is a certain premise, which is that for everything that is possible, it does not follow from assuming its occurrence that something impossible occurs. So if conflict were possible, it would not follow from assuming its occurrence that something impossible occurs. But an impossibility has followed from assuming its occurrence. And upon this we say: If we assume two gods, then conflict between them either is possible or is not possible. And both divisions are false, so the statement of the existence of two gods is invalid.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector says that the 'three categories' of absurdities (probably discussed earlier) result only from actual conflict, not from its possibility. Razi counters with a certain premise (مقدمة يقينية): if something is possible, then assuming it occurs should not lead to an impossibility. However, assuming actual conflict between two gods leads to absurdities, which shows that conflict is not possible. Therefore, the assumption of two gods is false. Razi formalizes: if we posit two gods, then conflict either is possible or not. Both options lead to a contradiction; hence the hypothesis of two gods is invalid.

Key Terms
المخالفة
المحال
المقدمة اليقينية
فرض الوقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only actual conflict, not its possibility, produces absurdities; so the possibility of conflict remains open.
Correction: Razi shows that if conflict were possible, its actualization would not lead to absurdity, but it does, so conflict is impossible. Thus the possibility is closed.

Checkpoint

A) If something is possible, then assuming it occurs should not lead to an impossibility
B) Two gods cannot exist
C) Conflict is impossible
D) The three categories are impossible

Key Takeaway
Razi's certain premise—that possibility cannot entail absurdity—proves that conflict between two gods is impossible, thereby refuting the possibility of multiple gods.

Third Proof: Omnipotence Leads to Contradiction with Two Gods
Razi begins the third proof against two gods using omnipotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's third proof against the existence of two gods, which shows that shared omnipotence leads to an impossible dilemma.

Core Idea
Two omnipotent gods cannot both create the same thing without contradiction.

Razi now presents a third argument against two gods, building on the attribute of omnipotence. He shows that if two gods exist, each must be capable of all things, which leads to a logical problem when both attempt the same act. This passage lays out the dilemma but leaves the conclusion for the next section.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice how the author structures the proof.
The passage ends abruptly; the conclusion is not given.
2
Understand the assumption and deduction
Razi starts by assuming two gods. He then deduces that each god must be omnipotent because power is an essential attribute. Identify the reasoning behind this step.
3
Analyze the dilemma
If both gods are omnipotent over the same act, what possibilities arise? The passage presents two options: either the act occurs by both together, or it does not occur. Think about why each option is problematic.
The passage does not complete the argument, but you can infer the impossibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على امتناع وجود الإلهين هي أنا لو فرضنا إلهين، لكان كل واحد منهما قادرا على جميع الممكنات، لأن صفة القادرية لما كانت في حق كل واحد منهما من لوازم الذات، وكانت نسبة تلك القادرية إلى السوية، وجب كونه تعالى قادرا على جميع الممكنات . وإذا ثبت هذا لزم أن كل ما كان مقدورا لأحدهما فهو بعينه مقدور للآخر من جهة واحدة وهي جهة الإيجاد والاختراع إلا أن ذلك محال، لأن كل ما كان مقدورا لقادر صح منه إيجاده. وإذا قصد كل واحد منهما إلى إيجاده. فإما أن يقع ذلك المقدور بهما معا، أو لا يقع
The third proof for the impossibility of the existence of two gods is that if we assume two gods, each of them would be powerful over all possible things, because the attribute of powerfulness (al-qadiriyya) is, in the case of each of them, among the necessities of the essence, and the relation of that powerfulness is equal. So it is necessary that He, exalted, be powerful over all possible things. And if this is established, it follows that everything that is within the power of one of them is exactly within the power of the other from one aspect, namely the aspect of bringing into existence and invention. However, that is impossible, because everything that is within the power of a powerful agent can be brought into existence by him. And if each of them intends to bring it into existence, then either that possible thing occurs by both of them together, or it does not occur.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of the third proof (الحجة الثالثة) against the existence of two gods. It is a deductive argument based on omnipotence. Razi assumes two gods and argues that each would be omnipotent (قادرا على جميع الممكنات) because power (القادرية) is an essential attribute (من لوازم الذات) and equal for both. Consequently, every possible thing is within the power of each god, and each can bring it into existence. However, if both intend to create the same thing, a dilemma arises: either the thing is created by both together (which is impossible because one creation suffices and two creators would be superfluous or contradictory), or it is not created by either (which conflicts with their omnipotence). The argument is cut off here, but the conclusion is that the assumption of two gods leads to an impossibility.

Key Terms
الحجة الثالثة
الإلهين
قادرا على جميع الممكنات
القادرية
لوازم الذات
جهة الإيجاد والاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two gods could divide creation between them, each ruling a separate domain.
Correction: Razi shows that omnipotence over all possibles means each god can create anything, so dividing creation is not possible because both have power over the same things, leading to conflict when they both attempt the same act.

Checkpoint

A) Each is limited in power
B) Each is powerful over all possible things (جميع الممكنات)
C) Each has power only over his own domain
D) Each is not really a god

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that assuming two gods implies each is omnipotent, leading to an impossible dilemma when both intend the same act.

If you truly believe that only God has absolute power, why do you sometimes feel fear or hope in created things? What does this lesson demand you to change about your daily reliance?
