The Core Argument: Combining Opposites
Razi states the impossibility of combining opposites and deduces that two gods cannot exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two gods would necessarily lead to the impossible combination of opposites.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot exist because they would either combine opposites or cease one another.

Imam Razi begins his refutation of the possibility of two gods by establishing a fundamental principle: combining opposites is impossible. This principle forms the backbone of the argument that the existence of two divine beings leads to a logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi states the principle first, then applies it.
2
Identify the principle of combining opposites
The passage states that combining opposites is impossible, even if they both cease or one ceases. Understand what 'combining opposites' means in theological context.
This is the foundational premise.
3
Understand the application to two gods
If two gods existed, their wills could conflict, leading to opposite commands in the same thing—that is combining opposites, which is impossible.
4
Reflect on why this argument is decisive
Think about why this logical deduction is considered a strong refutation of multiple gods. The impossibility of the consequence proves the impossibility of the premise.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية والعشرون الجمع بين الضدين. وهو محال وإن زالتا أو زالت إحداهما. فهو محال للوجوه التي ذكرناها. فعلم أن القول بوجود الإلهين يفضي إلى هذا المحال. فكان القول به محالا. قوله : لو حصل في الوجود إلهان لكان كل واحد منهما حكيما . وذلك يوجب توافقهما قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي أو لا يتوقف. فإن توقف لزم الجبر . وإذا لزم الجبر لم تكن فاعلية الله تعالى موقوفة على رعاية المصالح، فلم يلزم من خلو الفعل عن المصلحة أن لا يكون مراد الوقوع لله تعالى . فلم يلزم من كون المصلحة واحدة عدم المخالفة وإن لم يتوقف الفعل على الداعي، لم يلزم من استواء الضدين في المصلحة والمفسدة، عدم الترجيح في القصد والإرادة. فثبت أن هذا السؤال لا يمنع من صحة المخالفة. قوله : الشيء الذي هو معلوم الوقوع واحد وذلك يمنع من الاختلاف في الإرادة . قلنا : إرادة الوقوع متقدمة على الوقوع ، الذي هو متقدم على العلم بالوقوع فيمتنع أن يكون العلم بالوقوع سببا لإرادة الوقوع، وإلا لزم الدور. قوله : هذه الأقسام الثلاثة إنما تتولد من حصول المخالفة لا المخالفة . صحة من[؟] قلنا : ههنا مقدمة يقينية وهي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلو كانت المخالفة ممكنة لم يلزم من فرض وقوعها محال . لكنه قد لزم المحال من فرض وقوعها . وعند هذا نقول : لو فرضنا إلهين لكانت المخالفة بينهما. إما أن تكون ممكنة، وإما أن لا تكون ممكنة والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بوجود الإلهين.
The twenty-first issue: Combining opposites. It is impossible, even if they both cease or one of them ceases. So it is impossible for the reasons we have mentioned. Thus it is known that the claim of the existence of two gods leads to this impossible thing, so the claim of it is impossible. His statement: 'If there existed in existence two gods, each one of them would be wise, and that necessitates their agreement.' We say: The action either depends on the motive (al-da'i) or does not depend. If it depends, then determinism (al-jabr) follows. And if determinism follows, then the agency of Allah Most High is not dependent on observing interests (al-masalih). So it is not necessary, from the action being devoid of interest, that it should not be intended to occur for Allah Most High. So it is not necessary, from the interest being one, that there is no disagreement. And if the action does not depend on the motive, it is not necessary, from the equality of the two opposites in interest and harm, that there is no preference in intention and will. So it is established that this question does not prevent the correctness of disagreement. His statement: 'The thing whose occurrence is known is one, and that prevents difference in will.' We say: The will of occurrence precedes occurrence, which precedes knowledge of occurrence. So it is impossible that knowledge of occurrence should be a cause for the will of occurrence, otherwise a circle (al-dawr) would follow. His statement: 'These three divisions arise from the occurrence of disagreement, not from disagreement itself.' [unclear: 'sihha min'] We say: Here is a certain premise: that whatever is possible, it is not necessary that from assuming its occurrence an impossibility follows. So if disagreement were possible, it would not be necessary that from assuming its occurrence an impossibility follows. But an impossibility has indeed followed from assuming its occurrence. So at this point we say: If we suppose two gods, then disagreement between them is either possible or not possible, and both divisions are false. So the claim of the existence of two gods is refuted.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a series of three objections and answers defending the core argument that the existence of two gods would necessitate combining opposites, which is impossible. Imam Razi begins by restating the argument: combining opposites is impossible, and if two gods existed, their wills would either both be realized or not, leading to a contradiction. He then addresses an objection: if both gods are wise, they would agree. Razi responds by analyzing whether action depends on motive (da'i). If it does, determinism (jabr) follows, and God's agency is not tied to interests, so agreement is not forced. If it does not, equal interests do not prevent one will from being preferred. Thus the objection fails. The second objection: a single known occurrence would prevent difference in will. Razi replies that the will of occurrence precedes occurrence, which precedes knowledge, so knowledge cannot cause will (otherwise a circle (dawr) results). The third objection: the three divisions arise from actual disagreement, not from the possibility of disagreement. Razi presents a certain premise: if disagreement were possible, assuming it would not entail an impossibility. But since assuming it does entail an impossibility, disagreement must be impossible, proving that two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms
الجمع بين الضدين
الإلهين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could exist if they always agree.
Correction: Even the possibility of disagreement is fatal because it would require the impossible combination of opposites. The argument is about the logical possibility, not actual agreement.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the consequence of assuming two gods?
A) They would both be wise.
B) It leads to combining opposites.
C) They would necessarily agree.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods is derived from the impossibility of combining opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection 1: Two Wise Gods Would Agree
Razi responds to the objection that two wise gods would necessarily agree by analyzing whether action depends on motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's response to the objection that two wise gods would necessarily agree.

Core Idea
Divine agency is not constrained by human notions of interest; agreement is not forced.

The first objection claims that if two gods exist, both would be wise, and wisdom necessitates agreement. Razi carefully deconstructs this by analyzing the nature of divine action and its dependence on motive, showing that agreement is not necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation.
Notice the structure: objection, then Razi's reply with two cases.
2
Understand the two possibilities: action depends on motive or not
Razi says that if action depends on motive (da'i), determinism (jabr) follows; if not, then the will can be free. Understand each branch.
The term 'jabr' here refers to theological determinism.
3
See how each possibility leads to the conclusion that agreement is not required
In the first branch, God's agency is not tied to interests, so a single interest does not force agreement. In the second branch, equal interests do not prevent one will from being preferred.
4
Appreciate the subtlety of Razi's argument about divine agency
Razi shows that regardless of whether action depends on motive, the objection fails. This demonstrates the depth of his theological reasoning.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الحادية والعشرون الجمع بين الضدين. وهو محال وإن زالتا أو زالت إحداهما. فهو محال للوجوه التي ذكرناها. فعلم أن القول بوجود الإلهين يفضي إلى هذا المحال. فكان القول به محالا. قوله : لو حصل في الوجود إلهان لكان كل واحد منهما حكيما . وذلك يوجب توافقهما قلنا : الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي أو لا يتوقف. فإن توقف لزم الجبر . وإذا لزم الجبر لم تكن فاعلية الله تعالى موقوفة على رعاية المصالح، فلم يلزم من خلو الفعل عن المصلحة أن لا يكون مراد الوقوع لله تعالى . فلم يلزم من كون المصلحة واحدة عدم المخالفة وإن لم يتوقف الفعل على الداعي، لم يلزم من استواء الضدين في المصلحة والمفسدة، عدم الترجيح في القصد والإرادة. فثبت أن هذا السؤال لا يمنع من صحة المخالفة. قوله : الشيء الذي هو معلوم الوقوع واحد وذلك يمنع من الاختلاف في الإرادة . قلنا : إرادة الوقوع متقدمة على الوقوع ، الذي هو متقدم على العلم بالوقوع فيمتنع أن يكون العلم بالوقوع سببا لإرادة الوقوع، وإلا لزم الدور. قوله : هذه الأقسام الثلاثة إنما تتولد من حصول المخالفة لا المخالفة . صحة من[؟] قلنا : ههنا مقدمة يقينية وهي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال، فلو كانت المخالفة ممكنة لم يلزم من فرض وقوعها محال . لكنه قد لزم المحال من فرض وقوعها . وعند هذا نقول : لو فرضنا إلهين لكانت المخالفة بينهما. إما أن تكون ممكنة، وإما أن لا تكون ممكنة والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بوجود الإلهين.
The twenty-first issue: Combining opposites. It is impossible, even if they both cease or one of them ceases. So it is impossible for the reasons we have mentioned. Thus it is known that the claim of the existence of two gods leads to this impossible thing, so the claim of it is impossible. His statement: 'If there existed in existence two gods, each one of them would be wise, and that necessitates their agreement.' We say: The action either depends on the motive (al-da'i) or does not depend. If it depends, then determinism (al-jabr) follows. And if determinism follows, then the agency of Allah Most High is not dependent on observing interests (al-masalih). So it is not necessary, from the action being devoid of interest, that it should not be intended to occur for Allah Most High. So it is not necessary, from the interest being one, that there is no disagreement. And if the action does not depend on the motive, it is not necessary, from the equality of the two opposites in interest and harm, that there is no preference in intention and will. So it is established that this question does not prevent the correctness of disagreement. His statement: 'The thing whose occurrence is known is one, and that prevents difference in will.' We say: The will of occurrence precedes occurrence, which precedes knowledge of occurrence. So it is impossible that knowledge of occurrence should be a cause for the will of occurrence, otherwise a circle (al-dawr) would follow. His statement: 'These three divisions arise from the occurrence of disagreement, not from disagreement itself.' [unclear: 'sihha min'] We say: Here is a certain premise: that whatever is possible, it is not necessary that from assuming its occurrence an impossibility follows. So if disagreement were possible, it would not be necessary that from assuming its occurrence an impossibility follows. But an impossibility has indeed followed from assuming its occurrence. So at this point we say: If we suppose two gods, then disagreement between them is either possible or not possible, and both divisions are false. So the claim of the existence of two gods is refuted.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a series of three objections and answers defending the core argument that the existence of two gods would necessitate combining opposites, which is impossible. Imam Razi begins by restating the argument: combining opposites is impossible, and if two gods existed, their wills would either both be realized or not, leading to a contradiction. He then addresses an objection: if both gods are wise, they would agree. Razi responds by analyzing whether action depends on motive (da'i). If it does, determinism (jabr) follows, and God's agency is not tied to interests, so agreement is not forced. If it does not, equal interests do not prevent one will from being preferred. Thus the objection fails. The second objection: a single known occurrence would prevent difference in will. Razi replies that the will of occurrence precedes occurrence, which precedes knowledge, so knowledge cannot cause will (otherwise a circle (dawr) results). The third objection: the three divisions arise from actual disagreement, not from the possibility of disagreement. Razi presents a certain premise: if disagreement were possible, assuming it would not entail an impossibility. But since assuming it does entail an impossibility, disagreement must be impossible, proving that two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms
الداعي
الجبر
المصلحة
المخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two wise gods would automatically agree because wisdom implies perfect harmony.
Correction: Razi shows that divine wisdom does not force agreement; God's agency is not constrained by human notions of interest.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, if action depends on motive, what follows?
A) Determinism (jabr)
B) Free will
C) Agreement of wills
D) Different motives

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the objection by showing that in either scenario, agreement is not logically necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes assume that because God is wise, He must act in a way that makes sense to you? Where in your life have you imposed your own reasoning on God's decisions, and will you repent from that arrogance today?
?
Have you ever been in a disagreement with someone you respect and assumed that because they are wise, they should agree with you? How does Razi's argument challenge you to accept that wisdom does not force agreement, and will you apply that in your relationships starting now?
?
If God's actions are not bound by your understanding of 'interest,' then what do you truly rely on for your security? Are you willing to trust His will even when it seems to contradict your own sense of benefit, and what step will you take to deepen that trust today?

Objection 2: Knowledge of a Single Occurrence Prevents Difference
Razi responds to the objection that a single known occurrence would prevent difference in will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's argument that knowledge of an event cannot be the cause of the will for that event, thus the objection fails.

Core Idea
Will precedes occurrence, and knowledge follows occurrence; knowledge cannot cause will without circularity.

The second objection claims that if both gods know the same outcomes, they cannot will differently. Razi counters by pointing out the temporal priority of will over occurrence and knowledge, preventing a circular causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation.
This is a very concise exchange.
2
Understand the order: will -> occurrence -> knowledge
Razi says that will precedes occurrence, and occurrence precedes knowledge. So knowledge is after the will.
3
Recognize the circularity that would result if knowledge caused will
If knowledge caused will, then we would have a vicious circle (dawr) because will would be before and after occurrence.
This is a logical fallacy.
4
Conclude that the objection is invalid
Since knowledge cannot cause will, the objection that a known occurrence forces agreement fails.

Source Text

?

?

Objection 3: Disagreement Must Be Actual, Not Possible
Razi refutes the objection by applying a logical principle about possibility and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's final argument that if disagreement were possible, assuming it would not lead to impossibility, but it does, proving two gods cannot exist.

Core Idea
The impossibility that follows from assuming disagreement proves that disagreement itself is impossible.

The third objection tries to argue that the previous divisions only apply if disagreement actually occurs, not from the mere possibility. Razi brilliantly counters by using a principle: if something is possible, assuming it does not entail an impossibility; but assuming disagreement between gods entails an impossibility (combining opposites), so disagreement must be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note that there is an unclear phrase 'صحة من' which may be an OCR artifact.
The main idea is clear despite the textual issue.
2
Understand the principle about possibility
Razi states a certain premise: if something is possible, assuming it occurs does not lead to an impossibility.
This is a logical principle.
3
See how Razi applies it to disagreement
Assuming disagreement between gods leads to impossibility (combining opposites). Therefore, disagreement cannot be possible.
4
Realize that this completes the proof that two gods cannot exist
If we suppose two gods, disagreement is either possible or not possible. Both options lead to falsehood, so the existence of two gods is impossible.
This ties back to the core argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Third Proof Against Two Gods: Three Impossible Scenarios
Imam Razi presents the third proof: if two gods, any possible would be under both powers, leading to three impossible scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's third proof that two gods cannot coexist because any possible would be subject to both equal powers, resulting in three logically impossible outcomes.

Core Idea
Shared omnipotence over one act leads to contradictions.

Imam Razi presents a rigorous logical argument against the possibility of two gods. He begins with the premise that if there were two gods, each would be equally omnipotent over all possible things. Then he considers what would happen if both will the same act. The result is three scenarios, each shown to be impossible. This proof is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening lines: if two gods exist, each must be omnipotent over all possibles because the attribute of power is essential and equal in both.
This premise is crucial for the rest of the argument.
2
Identify the three scenarios
Given a single possible act intended by both, three outcomes are possible: it occurs by both, by neither, or by one only. List these in your mind.
Razi will refute each in turn.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Focus on why 'both together' is impossible: if the act occurs by both, it becomes necessary and cannot be attributed to a second cause, leading to a contradiction.
4
Examine the second and third refutations
See how 'neither' leads to mutual dependence and contradiction, and how 'one only' is impossible due to equal power and impossibility of difference in power over a single object.
Note the logical structure of each refutation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على امتناع وجود الإلهين هي أنا لو فرضنا إلهين، لكان كل واحد منهما قادرا على جميع الممكنات، لأن صفة القادرية لما كانت في حق كل واحد منهما من لوازم الذات، وكانت نسبة تلك القادرية إلى السوية، وجب كونه تعالى قادرا على جميع الممكنات . وإذا ثبت هذا لزم أن كل ما كان مقدورا لأحدهما فهو بعينه مقدور للآخر من جهة واحدة وهي جهة الإيجاد والاختراع إلا أن ذلك محال، لأن كل ما كان مقدورا لقادر صح منه إيجاده. وإذا قصد كل واحد منهما إلى إيجاده. فإما أن يقع ذلك المقدور بهما معا، أو لا يقع بواحد منهما، أو يقع بأحدهما دون الثاني. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة. أما القسم الأول: وهو وقوعه بهما جميعا - فهذا محال لأن إسناد الأثر إلى المؤثر إنما كان لإمكانه وجوازه والأثر إذا أخذ مع المؤثر التام، كان واجب الوجود وكونه واجب الوجود يمنعه من إسناده إلى القدرة الثانية. فكون الفعل مع القدرة الثانية يمنع من الإسناد إلى القدرة الأولى فيلزم أن يكون حال وقوعه بكل واحدة من القدرتين أن يكون واجب الانقطاع عن كل واحد منهما . وهو جمع بين النقيضين. وهو محال. وأما القسم الثاني : - وهو أن لا يقع بواحد منهما - فهو أيضا محال. لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذه القدرة، وقوعه بتلك القدرة والمانع من وقوعه بتلك القدرة، وقوعه بهذه القدرة فلا يمتنع هذا إلا إذا وجد ذلك، ولا يمتنع ذلك إلا إذا وجد هذا . فلو امتنعا معا لوجدا معا. وذلك محال فيلزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات. وهو محال. وأما القسم الثالث - وهو أن يقع بأحدهما دون الآخر - فهذا أيضا محال : لوجهين : الأول : أن كل واحد منهما فرضناه قادرا على جميع الممكنات، فلا يكون أحدهما أقدر من الثاني. وإذا كان كذلك امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر. الثاني : : أن وقوع التفاوت في القدرة على إيجاد المقدور الواحد محال، لما بينا أن التفاوت في الاقتدار يستدعي إمكان وقوع التفاوت في المقدور والشيء الذي يكون واحدا وحدة حقيقة يمتنع وقوع التفاوت فيه، فامتنع وقوع التفاوت في القدرة عليه . فثبت أن القول بوجود الإلهين يفضي إلى أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة وثبت أن كل واحد منها محال، فكان القول بوجود الإلهين محالا.
The third proof of the impossibility of the existence of two gods: If we suppose two gods, each one of them would be powerful over all possibles, because the attribute of being powerful (al-qadiriyya) in the case of each one of them is among the necessities of the essence, and the relation of that powerfulness is equal, so it is necessary that He Most High is powerful over all possibles. And if this is established, then it follows that everything that is within the power of one of them is exactly within the power of the other from one angle, namely the angle of bringing into existence and creation, except that this is impossible, because everything that is within the power of a powerful being, its bringing into existence is valid from him. And if each of them intends to bring it into existence, then either that thing within power occurs by both of them together, or it does not occur by either of them, or it occurs by one of them without the other. And the three divisions are false. As for the first division: its occurrence by both of them together—this is impossible because attributing the effect to the cause is only due to its possibility and permissibility, and the effect when taken with the complete cause is necessary of existence, and its being necessary of existence prevents its attribution to the second power. So the existence of the act with the second power prevents the attribution to the first power, so it follows that its state of occurring by each of the two powers is that it is necessarily disconnected from each one of them, which is a combination of two contradictories, and it is impossible. As for the second division: that it does not occur by either of them—this is also impossible, because that which prevents its occurrence by this power is its occurrence by that power, and that which prevents its occurrence by that power is its occurrence by this power. So this is not prevented unless that occurs, and that is not prevented unless this occurs. So if both were prevented, both would occur, and that is impossible, so a combination of negation and affirmation follows, and it is impossible. As for the third division: that it occurs by one of them without the other—this is also impossible for two reasons: first, each one of them we suppose to be powerful over all possibles, so one of them is not more powerful than the other, and if that is so, the preponderance of one over the other is impossible. Second, the occurrence of difference (al-tafawut) in power to bring into existence a single thing within power is impossible, since we have shown that difference in capability (al-iqtidar) requires the possibility of difference in the thing within power, and the thing that is one with a true unity, difference in it is impossible. So difference in power over it is impossible. So it is established that the claim of the existence of two gods leads to one of these three divisions, and it is established that each one of them is impossible, so the claim of the existence of two gods is impossible.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) against the existence of two gods. Imam Razi argues that if two gods existed, each would be omnipotent over all possibles because the attribute of power is a necessary concomitant of their essence and is equal in both. Consequently, any possible would be within the power of both gods equally. Then, if both simultaneously intend to bring it into existence, three scenarios arise: (1) it occurs by both together, (2) it occurs by neither, (3) it occurs by one only. Razi refutes each. The first is impossible because attributing an effect to a cause depends on possibility; once the effect is actual with a complete cause, it becomes necessary, and a necessary effect cannot be attributed to another power. This would force the effect to be attributed to both yet disconnected from each—a contradiction. The second is impossible because the prevention of one power's effect depends on the other's effect, leading to mutual dependence such that if both are prevented, both must occur—a contradiction. The third is impossible because both have equal power, so no preponderance can occur; also, difference in power over a single object is impossible because difference in capability would require difference in the object, which is impossible for a truly unitary object. Thus all three possibilities are impossible, proving that two gods cannot exist.

Key Terms

المقدور
الإيجاد
الاختراع
التفاوت
الاقتدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two omnipotent beings can cooperate to produce the same act.
Correction: Razi shows that a single act cannot be attributed to two independent omnipotent causes without logical contradiction, as it would either be necessary (and thus not attributable to both) or lead to other impossibilities.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three divisions refuted in the third proof against two gods?
A) The possible occurs by both gods together.
B) The possible occurs by neither god.
C) The possible occurs by one god after the other.
D) The possible occurs by one god without the other.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that the existence of two gods leads to logical contradictions in the coordination of their power over a single possible, proving that two gods cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if there were two gods, even the smallest event could not occur. How does this deepen your trust that only Allah's power is at work in every detail of your life? What specific worry can you hand over to Him today because He alone is the omnipotent cause?
?
The argument assumes both gods equally intend an act. In your own obedience to Allah, do you truly believe that His power alone brings about good deeds in your life, or do you sometimes attribute success to your own ability? How can you realign your heart to credit Him solely?
?
Razi emphasizes that difference in power over a single object implies difference in the object itself. Since Allah is One and His power is consistent, how does this truth challenge you to accept His decree even when you don't understand it? What situation are you currently struggling to see as part of His unified plan?

Refuting the Sophism: Good, Evil, and the Unity of God
Imam Razi answers the opponent's argument that good and evil require two creators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that good and evil in the world necessitate two gods, and why the single God is the creator of both.

Core Idea
Evil in the world does not imply a second god; a single God creates all, and evil is relative.

The presence of both good and evil in the world has been used by some to argue for two creators—one good, one evil. Imam Razi addresses this sophism with a clear logical refutation. In this passage, you'll see how he dismantles the opponent's argument by examining the implications of God's power and approval.

Guided Path
1
Read the sophism and its refutation
First, read the Arabic passage along with its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim followed by Imam Razi's answer.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that because the world contains both good and evil, a single agent cannot be both, so there must be two gods. Grasp this reasoning.
This is a common dualist argument.
3
Analyze Razi's two-part refutation
Razi presents two possibilities: either the good god is unable to prevent evil (thus weak), or able but does not (thus evil for approving). Work through each.
Note the logical 'if-then' structure.
4
Conclude the invalidity of the sophism
Both possibilities undermine the opponent's case. The sophism fails, affirming that a single God is the creator of all, including what we call evil.
5
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider why this refutation is important: it upholds tawheed (divine unity) and shows that evil is not an attribute of God but a relative concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شبهة للخصم : إنا نجد في العالم شرًا وخيرًا. والفاعل الواحد لا يكون خَيْرًا وشِرِّيرًا معا فلا بد من القول بالاثنين والجواب : أن الخير إن لم يقدر على دفع الشر، فهو عاجز لا يصلح للإلهية، وإن قدر ولم يفعل فهو أيضا شرير، لأن الراضي بأفعال الشر شرير. فثبت أن هذه الشبهة ساقطة والله أعلم.
A sophism from the opponent: We find in the world evil and good, and a single agent cannot be good and evil together, so it is necessary to affirm two [gods]. The answer: If the good [god] is not able to repel evil, then he is incapable, not fit for divinity; and if he is able but does not act, then he is also evil, because the one who approves of evil deeds is evil. So it is established that this sophism is invalid, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents a sophism (shubha) from the opponent and its refutation. The opponent argues that since the world contains both good and evil, a single agent cannot be both good and evil, so there must be two gods—one good and one evil. Imam Razi answers by considering two possibilities: either the good god is able to prevent evil or not. If he is unable, he is weak and not fit to be a god. If he is able but does not prevent evil, then he is also evil because he approves of evil actions. Thus the alleged dualism collapses; the single God is the creator of both good and evil, but evil is defined relative to human interest, not as an attribute of God.

الخير
الشر
العاجز
الراضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the presence of evil in the world implies a separate evil creator.
Correction: Classical kalam argues that God creates all actions, good and evil, and that evil is a privation or relative to human benefit; no second god is needed.

Checkpoint

A) He is incapable and not fit for divinity.
B) He is still good because he allows free will.
C) He is evil because he approves of evil deeds.
D) This is impossible because God is all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
The argument that good and evil require two gods is invalid; God is one, creator of all, and evil is defined relative to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face personal hardship or witness evil in the world, do you ever secretly question God's goodness or power? After learning that a single God creates both good and evil, how will you train your heart to trust Him in both situations? What specific dua or action will you commit to?
?
If you believe that God is the Creator of all things, including your own mistakes and sins, does that make you more or less responsible for your actions? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of personal accountability without falling into dualism?
?
Reflect on a moment when you blamed someone or something else for an evil you experienced. How does knowing that Allah alone is the Creator of all – and that He is not evil – change your response to hardship? Write down one concrete change you will make in your attitude toward trials.

Proof of the Impossibility of Two Gods and Response to a Shubha
Razi refutes belief in two gods via three-way division and answers a shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rational demonstration that belief in two gods leads to logical contradiction in every possible scenario, and how he refutes the objection based on the coexistence of good and evil.

Core Idea
Belief in two gods collapses into logical contradiction in every possible scenario.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a rigorous rational demonstration that the existence of two gods is impossible. He considers every conceivable way a single act could be attributed to two independent powerful beings and shows each leads to impossibility. He then addresses a common objection from the presence of good and evil in the world, which some claim requires two agents. This passage combines proof and refutation, illustrating the power of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Begin by reading the entire Arabic passage and its translation without analysis. Let Razi's argument flow naturally.
2
Understand the three divisions.
Razi divides the possibilities for an act being caused by two gods: by both together, by neither, by one alone. Grasp each division clearly.
3
Analyze the proof for each division.
Work through Razi's reasoning for why each division is impossible. Notice the logical contradictions he identifies, such as 'combination of contradictories' and circular dependence.
Pay attention to the use of 'necessary of existence' and 'equal power' arguments.
4
Examine the shubha and response.
Read the objection about good and evil requiring two agents, and Razi's answer. Understand how he refutes the need for two gods.
5
Review the overall conclusion.
Reflect on how the proof and response together establish tawhid (the oneness of God).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة.
·
أما القسم الأول: وهو وقوعه بهما جميعا وهو وقوعه بهما جميعا - فهذا محال لأن إسناد الأثر إلى المؤثر إنما كان لإمكانه وجوازه والأثر إذا أخذ مع المؤثر التام، كان واجب الوجود وكونه واجب الوجود يمنعه من إسناده إلى القدرة الثانية. فكون الفعل مع القدرة الثانية يمنع من الإسناد إلى القدرة الأولى فيلزم أن يكون حال وقوعه بكل واحدة من القدرتين أن يكون واجب الانقطاع عن كل واحد منهما . وهو جمع بين النقيضين. وهو محال. وأما القسم الثاني : - وهو أن لا يقع بواحد منهما - فهو أيضا محال. لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذه القدرة، وقوعه بتلك القدرة والمانع من وقوعه بتلك القدرة، وقوعه بهذه القدرة فلا يمتنع هذا إلا إذا وجد ذلك، ولا يمتنع ذلك إلا إذا وجد هذا . فلو امتنعا معا لوجدا معا. وذلك محال فيلزم اجتماع النفي والإثبات. وهو محال.
وأما القسم الثالث - وهو أن يقع بأحدهما دون الآخر - فهذا أيضا محال :
لوجهين :
،
الأول : أن كل واحد منهما فرضناه قادرا على جميع الممكنات، فلا يكون أحدهما أقدر من الثاني. وإذا كان كذلك امتنع رجحان أحدهما على الآخر. الثاني : : أن وقوع التفاوت في القدرة على إيجاد المقدور الواحد محال، لما بينا أن التفاوت في الاقتدار يستدعي إمكان وقوع التفاوت في المقدور والشيء الذي يكون واحدا وحدة حقيقة يمتنع وقوع التفاوت فيه، فامتنع وقوع التفاوت في القدرة عليه . فثبت أن القول بوجود الإلهين يفضي إلى أحد هذه الأقسام الثلاثة وثبت أن كل واحد منها محال، فكان القول بوجود الإلهين محالا. شبهة للخصم : إنا نجد في العالم شرًا وخيرًا. والفاعل الواحد لا يكون خَيْرًا وشِرِّيرًا معا فلا بد من القول بالاثنين والجواب : أن الخير إن لم يقدر على دفع الشر، فهو عاجز لا يصلح للإلهية، وإن قدر ولم يفعل فهو أيضا شرير، لأن الراضي بأفعال الشر شرير. فثبت أن هذه الشبهة ساقطة والله أعلم.
And the three divisions are invalid. As for the first division: that it occurs by both of them together - this is impossible because the attribution of the effect to the cause was only due to its possibility and permissibility, and the effect when taken with the complete cause, is necessary of existence, and its being necessary of existence prevents it from being attributed to the second power. So the action's being with the second power prevents attribution to the first power, so it follows that its state of occurring by each of the two powers is that it is necessarily cut off from each one of them. That is a combination of two contradictories, which is impossible. As for the second division: that it does not occur by either of them - it is also impossible. Because the preventer of its occurrence by this power is its occurrence by that power, and the preventer of its occurrence by that power is its occurrence by this power. So this is not prevented unless that exists, and that is not prevented unless this exists. If both were prevented, they would both exist. That is impossible, so the combination of negation and affirmation follows, which is impossible. As for the third division: that it occurs by one of them without the other - this too is impossible for two reasons: First: that each of them we have supposed to be powerful over all possibles, so one of them is not more powerful than the second. If that is so, the preponderance of one over the other is impossible. Second: that the occurrence of variation in the power to bring into existence a single object of power is impossible, because we have shown that variation in capability requires the possibility of variation in the object of power, and a thing that is one with a true unity is impossible for variation to occur in it, so variation in the power over it is impossible. So it is established that the assertion of the existence of two gods leads to one of these three divisions, and it is established that each one of them is impossible, so the assertion of the existence of two gods is impossible. A shubha for the opponent: We find in the world evil and good. The single agent cannot be both good and evil together, so one must assert two. And the answer: That if the good [god] is not able to repel evil, he is incapable, not fit for divinity; and if he is able and does not do it, he is also evil, because the one who is pleased with evil acts is evil. So it is established that this shubha is invalid, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a combined proof and refutation. Razi first proves that the claim of two gods leads to contradictions by dividing the possible ways a single act could be attributed to two gods. He identifies three divisions: (1) the act occurs by both together, (2) it occurs by neither, (3) it occurs by one alone. He shows each is impossible. In the first case, if the act is caused by both, it would be necessary relative to each cause, creating a contradiction because the act would be simultaneously necessary and not necessary from each perspective. In the second case, if neither causes it, we get a circular dependence preventing the act from occurring at all. In the third case, since both gods are equally powerful, there is no basis for one to prevail over the other; also variation in power over a single object is impossible. Therefore, belief in two gods is necessarily false. Then Razi presents a shubha from the opponent: the coexistence of good and evil in the world implies two agents. Razi answers: if the good god cannot stop evil, he is weak; if he can but does not, he is evil. Either way, the shubha fails.

Key Terms
قدرة
مؤثر
إلهين
شبهة
شر وخير
فاعل واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on the assumption that a single effect cannot have two causes.
Correction: Razi does not assume that; rather, he shows logical absurdities that follow from supposing two equally powerful independent causes.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the problem with the third division (the act occurs by one god alone)?
A) It leads to both gods being equal in power, preventing one from prevailing.
B) It requires the act to be impossible.
C) It implies both gods are powerless.
D) It allows variation in power over a single object.

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof shows that belief in two gods leads to logical impossibility in every conceivable scenario, and the objection from good and evil is refuted by showing that a good god either cannot or will not prevent evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever feel a flicker of doubt that there might be competing forces? How does Razi's response to the shubha challenge you to hold firm to tawhid even when your emotions waver?
?
Razi proves that two gods are logically impossible. But in your daily life, do you sometimes act as if there are other 'gods'—like money, status, or your own desires—that compete with Allah for your ultimate trust? What would it take to truly acknowledge that only Allah is the sole powerful agent over all things?
?
If you sincerely believe Allah is the only Creator and Controller, why do you still fear the outcomes of evil in your life? How does this lesson force you to re-examine the relationship between your belief in divine unity and your actual sense of security?

First Proof: God's Power Extends to All Possibles
Imam Razi presents the first proof that nothing acts independently of God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses the concepts of possibility and the equality of God's essence to prove that God's power encompasses all possible beings and that no other agent can bring anything into existence.

Core Idea
God's power covers every possible because possibility is common and His essence relates equally to all.

Imam al-Razi begins his first proof by laying a foundation: the cause of being a valid object of power is possibility. Since all possibles share this attribute, God's power must extend to all. He then eliminates any rival agent by showing that only absurd scenarios occur if another agent existed. This passage is a cornerstone of Islamic theology on divine power and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the English translation. Notice the two-part structure: first establishing God's power over all possibles, then eliminating other agents.
The passage uses dense kalam terminology; don't worry if some terms are unfamiliar yet.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage divides into two clear sections. The first part argues from possibility and divine essence to universal divine power. The second part uses a hypothetical scenario to rule out any other agent. Find the transition point.
The transition is at 'If we suppose another thing...'
3
Understand the role of possibility
Grasp the key claim: possibility (imkān) is what makes something a valid object of power. Since all possibles share possibility, they all equally qualify to be within God's power.
This establishes a universal scope for divine power.
4
Analyze the elimination of other agents
Focus on the three scenarios if there were two agents: effect by both, by neither, or by one only. Understand why each is impossible, leading to the conclusion that only God can truly bring things into existence.
This builds on earlier discussions in tawḥīd and the creation of acts.
5
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this proof is foundational: it establishes that no created entity has independent causal power, reinforcing tawḥīd (the oneness of God) in the domain of causality.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إن علة صحة المقدورية هي الإمكان والإمكان حكم مشترك فيه بين كل الممكنات وإذا كانت العلة مشتركة فيها، كان الحكم كذلك فإذن كل حكم الممكنات مشتركة في كونها بحيث يصح أن تكون مقدورة لله تعالى والمقتضي لكونه قادرا على المقدور هو ذاته ونسبة الذات إلى الكل على السوية. فلما اقتضت الذات كونه تعالى قادرا على البعض، وجب أن تقتضي كونه قادرا على الكل. فثبت أنه تعالى قادر على كل الممكنات، فلو فرضنا شيئا آخر يصلح لأن يكون مؤثرا في الوجود، فعند فرض اجتماع هذين المؤثرين إما أن يقع الأثر بهما، أو لا يقع بواحد منها، أو يقع بأحدهما دون الثاني والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، لما مرت الإشارة إليه في مسألة التوحيد ومسألة خلق الأفعال فوجب القطع بأنه لا شيء سوى قدرة الله تعالى يصلح للتأثير والتكوين.
The first: Indeed the cause of the validity of being an object of power (al-mawqūriyya) is possibility (al-imkān), and possibility is a predicate common among all possibles. When the cause is common among them, the judgment is likewise. Thus all judgments of possibles are shared in that they are such that it is valid for them to be objects of power for God Most High. And the determinant of His being powerful over the object of power is His essence, and the relation of the essence to all is equal. So when the essence necessitated that He be powerful over some, it must necessitate that He be powerful over all. Thus it is established that He is powerful over all possibles. If we suppose another thing suitable to be an agent (muʾaththir) in existence, then when these two agents are supposed to meet, either the effect occurs by both, or occurs by neither, or occurs by one of them without the other, and the three divisions are false, as has been indicated previously in the issue of tawḥīd and the issue of the creation of acts. So it must be decided that there is nothing besides the power of God Most High that is suitable for effect and bringing into being.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage contains Imam al-Razi's first proof for the claim that nothing comes into existence except by the power of Allah. It is a proof based on the nature of possibility and the equality of divine essence. The argument begins by stating that the cause for something being a valid object of power is its possibility (imkān). Since possibility is a common attribute among all possible beings, all possibles equally qualify as potential objects of divine power. That is, if God can be powerful over one possible, He can be powerful over every possible. The reason is that the determinant of God's power is His own essence, and His essence is equally related to all possibles. Therefore, if His essence necessitated power over some, it must necessitate power over all. This establishes that God is powerful over all possibles. Then, al-Razi introduces a hypothetical: if we suppose there is another being that could also be an agent in existence, then when two agents exist, only three scenarios are possible: either the effect is produced by both, or by neither, or by one alone. Each of these is impossible, as shown in earlier discussions on tawḥīd and the creation of acts. Hence, no agent besides Allah can truly bring anything into existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God is powerful over all possibles, then He must create everything that is possible.
Correction: The argument only shows that God has power over all possibles, not that He actually creates them. Power does not necessitate actualization; it is the capacity to bring about, not the act of bringing about.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the cause of something being a valid object of divine power?
A) Its existence
B) Its possibility
C) Its being created
D) Its agent

Key Takeaway
This proof establishes that God alone has the power to bring about all possible things, negating any real causal efficacy to created agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something difficult—a success in work, a personal goal, or a spiritual breakthrough—do you truly feel in your heart that it was entirely by Allah's direct power, or do you unconsciously credit your own skill, effort, or other people? Will you honestly examine the last major success you had and identify where your heart placed its trust?
?
This proof declares that nothing besides Allah can truly bring anything into existence—including your own actions, thoughts, and efforts. If you fully believed this right now, how would it change the way you prepare for your next big challenge? What would you do differently tomorrow morning to act on that belief?
?
The argument shows that the only real agent is Allah, and all other supposed causes are invalid. Is there a person, system, or habit you rely on as if it were an independent source of benefit or harm? What would it cost you to shift that reliance entirely to Allah today, and what is one step you can take to begin that shift within the next hour?

Contingency Demands a Single Necessary Being
Imam al-Razi's second proof: contingency requires a specific necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's second proof that contingency necessitates a single necessary being, and how he defends it against the objection that agency is a generic concept.

Core Idea
Contingency demands a specific Necessary Being, not just any agent.

Imam al-Razi presents a rigorous logical argument that all contingent beings require a specific necessary agent. He begins by noting that possibility (imkān) is a single attribute common to all possible beings, and it demands an agent. Through careful reasoning, he shows that this agent cannot be unspecified and cannot itself be contingent, leading to the conclusion that there must be a single necessary being. Al-Razi then anticipates a potential objection regarding the nature of agency and refutes it, demonstrating that the chain of dependency must end at a specific necessary essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of the proof carefully. Notice the logical flow and the key terms like إمكان, مؤثر, معين, واجب لذاته.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the main argument
Identify the structure: possibility necessitates a specific agent, that agent cannot be possible itself, so it must be necessary in itself. Trace the steps from the single nature of possibility to the conclusion.
The proof uses a dilemma: the agent is either specific or unspecified, and either possible or not.
3
Examine the objection and response
Notice how al-Razi anticipates the objection that agency might be a generic type concept. Understand his rebuttal that agency is accidental, leading to infinite regress unless stopped by a specific necessary essence.
This strengthens the proof by addressing a potential loophole.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Summarize the proof in your own words. Appreciate how this argument establishes tawheed and the necessary existence of Allah as the sole creator.
This proof is foundational in kalam arguments for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن صفة الإمكان صفة واحدة في الممكنات، وإنها محوجة إلى المؤثر. فإما أن يقال : الإمكان يحوج إلى مؤثر معين أو يحوج إلى مؤثر غير معين . والثاني محال . لأن ما لا يكون معينًا في نفسه لا يكون موجودًا وما لا يكون موجودًا استحال احتياج غيره إليه في الوجود . فإذن الإمكان يحوج إلى شيء معين. وذلك المعين إما أن يكون من الممكنات وإما أن لا يكون . لا جائز أن يكون من الممكنات وإلا لكان إمكان ذلك الشيء يحوجه إلى نفسه وحينئذ يكون موجدا لنفسه. وكل ما كان موجدا لنفسه كان واجبا لذاته فيكون الممكن لذاته واجبا . وهو محال . ولما بطل أن يكون ذلك الشيء من الممكنات، ثبت أنه واجب لذاته. فثبت أن الإمكان يحوج جميع الممكنات إلى الموجود الواجب لذاته، فيكون الواجب لذاته هو المبدأ لوجود جميع الممكنات وهو المطلوب. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر، من حيث هو مؤثر والمؤثر من حيث هو مؤثر ، له ماهية واحدة بالنوع وهي لا تمنع دخول أشياء كثيرة تحتها بالعدد. والجواب لما كان المؤثر من حيث إنه مؤثر مفهومًا واحدًا، مشتركًا فيه بين ذوات الماهيات المختلفة، يلزم أن تكون المؤثرية لاحقا من لواحق تلك الماهيات المختلفة وكل ما كان كذلك كان مفتقرًا إلى المؤثر، ويعود التقسيم الأول فيه، ولا ينقطع الافتقار والاستناد إلا عند الانتهاء إلى الذات ويجب أن تكون تلك الذات معينة - كما بيناه - وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب.
The second: That the attribute of possibility (al-imkān) is a single attribute in possibles, and that it necessitates the agent (al-muʾaththir). So either it is said: possibility necessitates a specific agent, or it necessitates an unspecified agent. And the second is impossible, because what is not specific in itself does not exist, and what does not exist, it is impossible for something else to be in need of it in existence. Thus possibility necessitates a specific thing. That specific thing is either from among possibles or not. It is not permissible that it be from among possibles; otherwise the possibility of that thing would necessitate itself, and then it would be self-existent. And everything that is self-existent would be necessary in itself, so a possible in itself would be necessary, which is impossible. Since it is false that that thing is from among possibles, it is established that it is necessary in itself. Thus it is established that possibility necessitates all possibles to the being that is necessary in itself, so the necessary in itself is the principle of the existence of all possibles—this is what was sought. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that possibility be the cause of the need for the agent, insofar as it is an agent, and the agent insofar as it is an agent has a single essence by type that does not prevent many individuals falling under it? The answer: Since the agent insofar as it is an agent is a single concept shared among different essences, it follows that agency is an accident attached to those diverse essences. Everything that is such is in need of an agent, and the first division recurs for it, and the chain of need and dependence does not stop until reaching the essence, and it is necessary that that essence be specific—as we have shown—and then the sought result is obtained.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage contains Imam al-Razi's second proof. It is a logical argument about contingency requiring a specific necessary being. The argument proceeds in stages: First, possibility (imkān) is a single attribute common to all possible beings, and it necessitates an agent. Since possibility is one, it must necessitate either a specific agent or an unspecified agent. An unspecified agent is impossible because what is not specific in itself cannot exist, and what does not exist cannot be the object of need. Therefore, possibility necessitates a specific agent. Now, that specific agent must either be itself a possible being or not. It cannot be a possible being, because then its own possibility would require itself, making it self-causing, which would make it necessary in itself—a contradiction for a possible. So the specific agent must be necessary in itself. Thus, all possibles depend on a single necessary being. Then al-Razi anticipates an objection: Could possibility not require a specific agent, but rather the agent is a generic concept allowing many individuals? He answers that agency is a concept attached to diverse essences, so each such essence is contingent and needs its own agent, leading to an infinite regress unless we stop at a specific necessary essence. Hence the proof concludes.

Key Terms
إمكان
مؤثر
معين
واجب لذاته
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof argues that possibility itself is the agent.
Correction: Rather, possibility is the reason for needing an agent; the agent is something else.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam al-Razi argue that the agent necessitated by possibility must be specific rather than unspecified?
A) Because an unspecified agent would be impossible to describe
B) Because what is not specific in itself does not exist, so cannot be needed
C) Because all possibles are specific
D) Because agents are always specific

Key Takeaway
Contingent existence points to a single necessary being, which is the foundation of all existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is contingent and sustained each moment by the Necessary Being, how does your daily anxiety about provision and safety expose a failure to truly believe this? What concrete action will you take today to trust in that reality?
?
Al-Razi argues that any unspecified agent is impossible. When you attribute events in your life to 'chance' or 'luck,' are you implicitly denying the specific Agent upon whom your very existence depends? What would change if you saw every event as directly from that Agent?
?
This proof shows that you cannot be self-sufficient even for a single moment. If you were to fully internalize that your being is borrowed, how would your prayer, patience, and purpose in life transform? What will you do to remind yourself of this dependency hourly?

Proof That Contingency Requires a Necessary Being
Razi completes a proof that contingency requires a necessary being and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi proves that all contingent things require a necessary being and how he answers an objection that contingency itself could be the cause of need.

Core Idea
Contingency forces every contingent thing to depend on a necessary being.

This passage from Razi completes his argument that contingency necessitates a necessary being, then tackles a potential objection. The objection asks why contingency itself cannot be the cause of need. Razi's answer shows that 'effecter' is a single concept attached to different essences, leading to infinite regress unless we reach a specific self-sufficient essence. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the classical proof from contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the proof, objection, and answer.
Notice the structure: proof by contradiction, then a question, then an answer.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the proof: start with the assumption of self-causation, derive a contradiction, then conclude that the cause must be necessary. Then follow the objection and see how Razi's answer prevents infinite regress.
3
Evaluate the objection and answer
Consider why the objection might seem plausible and why Razi's response is necessary to avoid assuming contingency as a cause.
The key point is that 'effecter' is a shared concept; if it attaches to contingent essences, they become effects themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل ما كان موجدا لنفسه كان واجبا لذاته فيكون الممكن لذاته واجبا . وهو محال . ولما بطل أن يكون ذلك الشيء من الممكنات، ثبت أنه واجب لذاته. فثبت أن الإمكان يحوج جميع الممكنات إلى الموجود الواجب لذاته، فيكون الواجب لذاته هو المبدأ لوجود جميع الممكنات وهو المطلوب. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الإمكان علة للحاجة إلى المؤثر، من حيث هو [؟] . والجواب لما كان المؤثر من حيث إنه مؤثر مفهومًا واحدًا، مشتركًا فيه بين ذوات الماهيات المختلفة، يلزم أن تكون المؤثرية لاحقا من لواحق تلك الماهيات المختلفة وكل ما كان كذلك كان مفتقرًا إلى المؤثر، ويعود التقسيم الأول فيه، ولا ينقطع الافتقار والاستناد إلا عند الانتهاء إلى الذات ويجب أن تكون تلك الذات معينة - كما بيناه - وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب.
And everything that is a producer of itself would be necessary by itself, so the contingent-by-itself would be necessary. And that is impossible. And when it is false that that thing is among the contingent things, it is established that it is necessary by itself. So it is established that contingency necessitates all contingent things to be in need of the existent necessary by itself, so the necessary by itself is the source of existence of all contingent things, and that is what is sought. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that contingency be the cause of the need for an effect, insofar as it is [?]? And the answer: Since the effecter insofar as it is an effecter is a single concept shared among different essences, it follows that effectership is an accident among the accidents of those different essences, and everything that is like that is in need of an effecter, and the first division returns upon it, and the need and dependence do not cease except when reaching the essence, and that essence must be specific - as we have shown - and then the sought is obtained.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a completed proof followed by an objection-and-answer. It begins by summarizing a demonstration: if something were self-causing, it would be necessary, but that leads to contradiction; therefore the cause of contingent things must be necessary. Then an objection is raised: why not say contingency itself is the cause of the need for an effect? The answer explains that 'effecter' is a single concept; if it attaches to different essences, those essences become contingent and need another effecter, leading to infinite regress. The only stopping point is a self-sufficient essence that is not contingent. Thus the argument stands that all contingent things require a necessary being.

Key Terms

ممكن لذاته
مؤثر
إمكان
افتقار
ذات معينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contingency itself can be the cause of the need for an effect, so no necessary being is needed.
Correction: The passage shows that contingency only explains the mode of need, not the cause that fulfills it. The concept 'effecter' leads to regress unless we reach a non-contingent essence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does contingency necessitate for all contingent things?
A) They are self-sufficient.
B) They are necessary by themselves.
C) They are in need of the necessary being.
D) They are the cause of their own existence.

Key Takeaway
The proof that contingency entails a necessary being is defended against the objection that contingency itself could be the cause, by showing that 'effecter' is a shared concept that leads to regress unless we reach a self-sufficient specific essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is contingent upon Allah every single moment, do you truly live with that awareness? When did you last pause to acknowledge that you are not self-sufficient for even a breath? How would your day change if you repeated to yourself: 'I am contingent, He is necessary'?
?
The proof shows that dependence ends only at a necessary being. Where do you actually place your ultimate trust—in your job, your health, your relationships, or in Allah? Are you treating something contingent as if it were necessary? What would it take for you to shift that trust entirely to the Necessary Being?

The objection in the passage tries to make contingency itself a cause of need. Do you ever make excuses to avoid relying on Allah, attributing your existence to secondary causes? The Third Proof: The Possible is Receptive, Not Effective
Razi presents the third proof based on the philosophers' principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the receptivity of contingent beings precludes them from being effective causes, establishing the necessity of a non-contingent cause.

Core Idea
Contingent beings can only receive, not cause; only the Necessary can be effective.

Razi's third proof draws on the philosophers' own definition of the contingent as being equally open to existence and non-existence. If a contingent could cause another, it would have to be both receptive and effective simultaneously—a logical contradiction. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing set the stage.
Notice the concise argumentative style of Razi.
2
Understand the definition of contingent
The passage states that everything contingent is 'receptive of existence and non-existence.' Grasp this as the starting point.
3
Follow the reductio argument
If a contingent were to cause another, it would be both receptive (to its own existence) and effective—a logical impossibility.
This contradiction is the heart of the proof.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Thus, only the Necessary Being has the property of effect; contingent beings only have the property of reception.
This establishes that a necessary cause is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : هي مبنية على أصول الحكماء - هي أن كل ما كان ممكنا لذاته، فهو قابل للوجود والعدم، فلو كان شيء من الممكنات مؤثرا في وجود شيء آخر، لزم كون الشيء الواحد قابلا وفاعلا معًا. وهو محال فإذن الممكن له خاصية القبول، وليس له صلاحية التأثير، كما أن الواجب لذاته له خاصية التأثير، وليس له صلاحية القبول.
The third proof: it is based on the principles of the philosophers - that everything that is contingent by itself is receptive of existence and non-existence. So if something among contingent things were an effecter in the existence of something else, it would necessitate that one thing be both receptive and effective together, which is impossible. Therefore, the contingent has the property of reception and does not have the capacity for effect, just as the necessary by itself has the property of effect and does not have the capacity for reception.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof. It builds on the philosophers' definition of the contingent as being equally receptive to existence and non-existence. If a contingent thing were to cause another, it would have to be both receptive (to its own existence from another) and effective (giving existence to another), which is a contradiction. Therefore, only the necessary being, which is purely effective and not receptive, can be a true cause.

Key Terms
ممكن
قابل
فاعل
خاصية القبول
خاصية التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contingent beings can cause other contingent beings (e.g., a parent causes a child).
Correction: This proof shows that because contingent beings are inherently receptive to existence, they cannot simultaneously be effective. True effect belongs only to the Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
What property does the contingent being have according to the third proof?
A) It is purely effective.
B) It is both receptive and effective.
C) It is receptive of existence and non-existence.
D) It is necessary by itself.

Key Takeaway
Contingent beings are inherently receptive and cannot be effective causes; only the Necessary Being can be truly effective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you are contingent, then your very existence is received from another. Do you live as if your existence is a gift that could be withdrawn, or do you, in your daily actions, act as if you are the source of your own achievements? When you feel proud of your own accomplishments, are you forgetting that you were not effective but merely receptive?
?
This proof argues that only the Necessary Being can truly cause effects. Consider your own sense of agency: do you sometimes think you are the ultimate cause of things in your life? Where in your life do you need to acknowledge that you are not the effector but merely a channel, and what would change if you truly believed that only Allah is effective?
?
If you believe that only Allah is the effective cause, then why do you still feel anxiety about outcomes? What would it mean for your trust in Allah to act on this conviction today?

The Point: Existence, Indivisibility, and the Reality of the Extremity
Razi answers an objection and proves three properties of the point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that the extremity of a line is not non-existence and that the point has three essential properties: existence, being indicated, and indivisibility.

Core Idea
The extremity of a line is real, not non-existence, and it is an indivisible, indicated entity.

Imam Razi begins by addressing an objection: how can the end of a thing be something real if it is simply its cessation? He answers by appealing to the necessary knowledge of contact between magnitudes. This leads to establishing three fundamental properties of the point, which we will explore in this lesson.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's answer
Read the objection that the end is non-existence and Razi's reply using the example of two magnitudes in contact. Notice how he argues that contact would be unintelligible if the extremity were nothing.
2
Examine the three required points
Razi then states three things that must be proved about the point: its existence, that it is something indicated, and that it is indivisible. Identify each one in the passage.
3
Understand the indivisibility proof
Follow Razi's argument that if the point were divisible, the extremity would not be a true extremity. This is a crucial argument for atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : نهاية الشيء عبارة عن انقطاعه، وانقطاع الشيء عبارة عن أنه فني وما بقي منه شيء البتة وعدم الشيء كيف يكون أمرا موجودا؟ قلنا : نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن المقدارين إذا تماسا فإنهما يتماسان بطرفيهما، فلو كان طرف الشيء نفس العدم لكان معنى المماسة هو عدم هذا مماس لعدم ذاك وهذا غير معقول. فعلمنا أن طرف الشيء يستحيل أن يكون نفس العدم. وأما المطلوب الثاني وهو أن النقطة شيء مشار إليه ـ فذلك ظاهر؛ لأنه يمكننا أن نشير بالحس إلى طرف الخط. وأما المطلوب الثالث - وهو أن النقطة غير قابلة للقسمة - فالذي يدل عليه، أنها لو انقسمت لافترض فيها جزءان وحينئذ يكون طرف الخط هو القسم الثاني فقط، وحينئذ لا يكون الطرف طرفا . هذا خلف. فالحاصل أن كل ما كان منقسما، لم يكن كله طرفا وكل ما كان كله طرفا، لم يكن منقسما والنقطة عبارة عن نفس الطرف، فوجب أن لا تكون منقسمة، ولما ثبت بالبرهان هذه المطالب الثلاثة ظهر أن النقطة موجودة مشار إليها، غير منقسمة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا الشيء إما أن يكون جوهرًا، وإما أن يكون عرضًا. لا جائز أن يكون عرضًا. وإلا لافتقر إلى محل ومحله إن كان منقسما لزم انقسامه بانقسام محله . وذلك محال . وإن لم يكن منقسما عاد التقسيم فيه. وهو أنه إما أن يكون جوهرًا أو عرضًا ويلزم إما التسلسل - وهو محال - أو الانتهاء إلى جوهر غير قابل للقسمة وهو المطلوب.
If it is said: The end of a thing is its cessation, and cessation of a thing means that it perished and nothing of it remains at all — how can non-existence be an existent thing? We say: We know by necessity that if two magnitudes are in contact, they are in contact by their extremities. If the extremity of a thing were non-existence itself, then the meaning of contact would be: this non-existence is in contact with that non-existence, which is unintelligible. So we know that the extremity of a thing cannot be non-existence itself. As for the second required [point] — that the point is something indicated — that is evident, because we can indicate by sense the extremity of a line. As for the third required [point] — that the point is indivisible — what indicates it is that if it were divisible, two parts would be assumed in it; then the extremity of the line would be only the second part, and then the extremity would not be an extremity. This is a contradiction. The conclusion: Everything that is divisible is not wholly an extremity, and everything that is wholly an extremity is not divisible. The point is precisely the extremity itself, so it must be indivisible. Since these three required points have been proved by demonstration, it appears that the point is existent, indicated, and indivisible. And when this is established, we say: This thing is either a substance (jawhar) or an accident ('araḍ). It cannot be an accident, otherwise it would need a locus. If its locus is divisible, then it would be divided by the division of its locus — that is impossible. And if its locus is not divisible, the same division recurs regarding it: either it is a substance or an accident, leading either to infinite regress — which is impossible — or to ending at an indivisible substance, which is the required [conclusion].
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a proof establishing three properties of the point and then a further proof that the point must be a substance. The objector argues that the end of a thing is its cessation and thus non-existence; how can non-existence be a thing? Razi answers by appealing to necessary knowledge: when two magnitudes are in contact, they touch at their extremities. If the extremity were non-existence, contact would be meaningless non-existence touching non-existence — this is unintelligible. Therefore the extremity is real. Next, Razi proves three required points (matalib) about the point: (1) that it is existent, (2) that it is something indicated (by sense, since we can point to the end of a line), and (3) that it is indivisible. For indivisibility, if the point were divisible, the line's extremity would be only one of its parts, not the whole; but the extremity is the whole, so it cannot be divided. Thus the point is existent, indicated, and indivisible. Then Razi asks: is this point a substance or an accident? It cannot be an accident because an accident needs a locus. That locus, if divisible, would cause the point to be divided with it (impossible); if indivisible, the same question recurs. This leads either to infinite regress or to stopping at an indivisible substance. Since infinite regress is impossible, we conclude the point is an indivisible substance (jawhar la yatajazza'). Note that this proof establishes atomism.

Key Terms
نقطة
طرف
مماسة
لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The extremity of a line is non-existence because it is the end.
Correction: Razi shows that contact between magnitudes proves the extremity is real and not non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the extremity of a line cannot be non-existence?
A) Because non-existence cannot be indicated by sense.
B) Because contact of two magnitudes would then require non-existence touching non-existence, which is unintelligible.
C) Because non-existence cannot be divided.
D) Because non-existence is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
The point is a real, indicated, and indivisible entity, proven by the contact argument and the impossibility of division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you take for granted that the 'end' of something is merely emptiness? How does this lesson's proof that the extremity is a real existent challenge you to see more deeply into the reality of things? What is one area of your life where you have dismissed something as insignificant, but perhaps it holds real meaning before Allah?
?
The proof begins with 'we know by necessity' — something that is self-evident. Have you ever rejected a truth that was obvious because it was uncomfortable? What would it take for you to submit to what is clearly true, even if it demands change from you?
?
The point is indivisible and whole. In your life, what parts of your character or faith have you allowed to become divided, inconsistent, or fragmented? How can you strive for a unity and integrity that reflects the oneness of Allah?

The Point: Proving It Is a Substance
Razi proves the point is a substance through elimination and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's proof that the point must be a substance (jawhar) and cannot be an accident ('araḍ), using the substance-accident dichotomy and the impossibility of infinite regress.

Core Idea
The point, being indivisible and indicated, must be a substance rather than an accident.

Having established that the point is existent, indicated, and indivisible, Razi now asks: what is its ontological category? Is it a substance or an accident? In this lesson, we follow his reasoning as he eliminates the possibility of it being an accident and concludes it is an indivisible substance, a cornerstone of atomist metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Recall the established properties
Remember from the previous lesson that the point is existent, indicated, and indivisible.
2
Follow the dichotomy argument
Razi says the point is either a substance or an accident. Read his argument that it cannot be an accident, because an accident requires a locus, and that locus would lead to infinite regress or to an indivisible substance.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, we must stop at an indivisible substance. This is the point itself, proving it is a substance.

Source Text

?
