First Proof: Finite Bodies Cannot Have Infinite Parts
Razi's first proof against infinite parts in finite bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first argument that a finite body cannot consist of an infinite number of parts.

Core Idea
A finite body cannot contain infinite parts because size and number are proportional.

Razi presents a rigorous proof using a dilemma: either adding parts increases size or it doesn't. Both cases lead to a contradiction, showing that infinite parts in a finite body are impossible. This lesson walks through that logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the flow. Then read the translation alongside it.
Note the use of conditional statements.
2
Identify the dilemma
Find the two branches of the dilemma: what happens if an increase in number does or does not cause an increase in magnitude.
The passage presents this with 'إما أن توجب... أو لا توجب'.
3
Trace each branch to contradiction
Follow the logic for each branch: the first leads to no magnitude at all, the second leads to a finite number of parts. Both contradict the assumption of infinite parts.
Notice the use of 'خلف' (contradiction) at each step.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Understand that this proof is the second premise in a larger argument for the existence of God (often the argument from contingency).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية: وهي قولنا: هذه الأجسام المتناهية في المقدار، يمتنع أن تكون مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية في العدد - فيدل عليه وجوه: الأول: أن زيادة العدد إما أن توجب زيادة المقدار أو لا توجب فإن أوجبت لم يكن تألفها سببا لازدياد المقدار، فلم تكن المقادير حاصلة فيها البتة. هذا خلف. وأما إن كان ازدياد العدد موجبا لازدياد المقدار كانت نسبة العدد إلى المقدار كنسبة العدد إلى العدد. وكما أن بعضها أزيد من البعض في المقدار وجب أن يكون بعضها أزيد من البعض في العدد. والعدد الناقص متناه، والزائد زائد على الناقص بقدر متناه، فيكون زائدا على عدده بعدد متناه فيكون عدد الزائد متناهيا. وقد فرضناه غير متناه هذا خلف.
As for the second premise: which is our statement: these bodies that are finite in magnitude, it is impossible that they be composed of parts infinite in number. This is indicated by aspects: The first: that the increase of number either necessitates an increase in magnitude or does not necessitate. If it necessitates, then their composition would not be a cause for the increase of magnitude, so magnitudes would not be obtained in it at all. This is contradictory. And if the increase of number necessitates an increase in magnitude, then the ratio of number to magnitude is like the ratio of number to number. And just as some of them are greater than others in magnitude, it must be that some of them are greater than others in number. And the deficient number is finite, and the excess is in excess over the deficient by a finite measure, so it is in excess over its number by a finite number, so the number of the excess is finite. And we have assumed it infinite; this is contradictory.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is a proof: Razi argues that a finite body cannot contain an infinite number of parts. He first states the second premise of a larger argument, then gives the first of several proofs. His reasoning is a dilemma: either an increase in number causes an increase in magnitude or it does not. If it does not, then composition (i.e., putting parts together) would not increase the size, so the body would have no magnitude at all—which is false. If it does cause an increase, then the ratio of number to magnitude is proportional: just as one body is larger than another, it must have more parts. In that case, the smaller body has a finite number of parts (since it is finite), and the larger body exceeds it by a finite amount; thus the larger body also has a finite number of parts, contradicting the assumption of infinite parts. Therefore, the initial claim that a finite body has infinite parts is impossible.

Key Terms
أجزاء غير متناهية
زيادة العدد
زيادة المقدار
عدد ناقص
عدد زائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A finite body could have infinite parts if the parts are 'potential' rather than actual.
Correction: Razi's argument targets actual infinite parts, not potential. Potential parts are not actual components, so the argument does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, what happens if an increase in number does not cause an increase in magnitude?
A) The body becomes larger
B) The composition fails to produce any magnitude
C) The number of parts becomes finite
D) The ratio becomes equal

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that infinite parts in a finite body lead to logical absurdity, establishing that bodies are composed of a finite number of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a finite body, composed of finite parts. Yet you often live as if your time, energy, and health are unlimited. If this proof forces you to accept the limits of your physical existence, what changes must you make today to align your priorities with that reality?
?
Razi uses rigorous logic to affirm a fundamental truth about creation. When you consider your own belief in Allah, do you rely more on intellectual arguments like this one, or on direct spiritual experience? What does your answer reveal about the strength of your faith and your need for continuous reflection?
?
This proof shows that even the simplest physical object—a finite body—cannot escape the principle of limitation. Look at your own body: is it a mere collection of parts that you take for granted, or does its finite, ordered nature lead you to greater awe of its Creator? How will you cultivate that awareness starting now?

The Half-Distance Paradox
Razi's second proof: paradox of half-distances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that the divisions of distance must be finite, derived from the paradox of traversing half-distances.

Core Idea
If distance had infinite parts, you could never reach the end.

Razi now presents a second argument based on the nature of movement through space. The idea is simple: to cross any distance you must first cross half of it, then half of the remaining, and so on. If there were infinitely many such intermediate points, you would never arrive in finite time. But we do arrive, so the number of sections must be finite.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption
Read the first conditional: 'if the distance were composed of infinite parts...' Understand what is being assumed.
This is a counterfactual assumption to be disproved.
2
Follow the logical consequence
Follow the reasoning: to reach the end you must first reach half, then half of the remaining, ad infinitum. Conclude that reaching the end would be impossible in finite time.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Observe the actual fact
Note that in reality, we do reach the end of distances in finite time. So the assumption leads to a contradiction with observed fact.
The observable world is the standard.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore the assumption must be false: the sections of distance are finite. Identify the final sentence as the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن المسافة لو كانت مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية، لكان لا يمكن الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها إلا بعد الوصول إلى نصفها ولا يمكن الوصول إلى نصفها إلا بعد الوصول إلى ربعها فلو كانت أجزاء غير متناهية، لامتنع الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها في مدة متناهية ولما لم يكن ذلك ممتنعا، علمنا: أن المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة متناهية. الثالث: لو كانت المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة غير متناهية، لامتنع أن يصل السريع إلى البطيء؛ وذلك لأنه إذا ابتدأ السريع بعده فإن وصل السريع إلى الموضع الذي وصل إليه البطيء، يكون البطيء قد قطع بعض الأجزاء وإلى أن يصل السريع أيضا إلى ذلك الموضع الثاني يكون قد وصل البطيء إلى موضع ثالث فلو حصل في الجسم مقاطع غير متناهية لكان كلما وصل السريع إلى موضع، يكون البطيء قد تعداه. وكان يجب أن لا يصل السريع إلى البطيء وحيث يصل إليه، علمنا المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة متناهية.
The second: that if the distance were composed of infinite parts, it would not be possible to reach from its beginning to its end except after reaching its half, and it would not be possible to reach its half except after reaching its quarter. So if the parts were infinite, it would be impossible to reach from its beginning to its end in a finite time. Since that is not impossible, we know that the sections (intervals) occurring in the distance are finite. The third: if the sections occurring in the distance were infinite, it would be impossible for the fast to reach the slow; that is because when the fast starts after it (the slow), then if the fast reaches the position which the slow had reached, the slow has covered some parts; and before the fast reaches that second position, the slow has reached a third position. So if there occurred in the body infinite sections, then whenever the fast reaches a position, the slow would have passed it. And it would be necessary that the fast does not reach the slow. But since it does reach it, we know that the sections occurring in the distance are finite.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage contains two more proofs, both based on motion. The second proof is the paradox of traversing a divided distance: if a distance has infinite intermediate points, you can never reach the end in finite time, because you must first traverse half, then half of the remaining, etc., infinitely. Since we do reach ends, the divisions must be finite. The third proof is the famous 'Achilles and the tortoise' paradox: if the slow moves ahead, the fast must first reach where the slow was, but by then the slow has moved further; if divisions are infinite, this continues forever and the fast never catches up. But in reality, the fast does catch up, so the divisions cannot be infinite. Both proofs rely on the observable fact of motion to refute infinite divisibility of space.

Key Terms
مسافة
مدة متناهية
مقاطع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is denying the possibility of motion itself.
Correction: Razi is not denying motion; he is using the fact that motion occurs as evidence to refute infinite divisibility of space.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof, what would happen if distance had infinite parts?
A) You could reach the end instantly
B) You could never reach the end in finite time
C) Motion would be impossible
D) The distance would be zero

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the observed fact of completing motion to prove that the divisions in distance are finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many times have you felt stuck because the task ahead seemed too vast? This lesson shows that the path is composed of finite steps. What is one 'infinite' goal you have been avoiding, and what would it look like to break it into its finite sections so you can actually begin?
?
The proof uses the simple fact that you are already moving. When was the last time you moved from one state to another, and did you pause to reflect that the fact of your motion itself proves the world is not infinite? How does that change the way you see your everyday actions?
?
Razi's argument demands that if the parts were infinite, you would never arrive. But you have arrived at many destinations in your life. Are you living as though you actually believe that each journey has a finite number of steps, or do you act as though the road is endless and never take the first step? What step today can you take to prove to yourself that the sections are finite?

The Achilles Paradox
Razi's third proof: Achilles and tortoise paradox

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that the sections of distance must be finite, based on the Achilles-and-tortoise paradox.

Core Idea
If divisions were infinite, the fast could never catch the slow.

The third proof is a variation of Zeno's famous paradox of Achilles and the tortoise. If space is infinitely divisible, then whenever the faster runner reaches the slower's previous position, the slower has moved ahead. This would repeat infinitely, and the faster would never catch up. But in reality, the faster does catch up, so infinite division cannot be true.

Guided Path
1
State the assumption
Read the first conditional: 'if the sections occurring in the distance were infinite...' Understand the hypothesis.
2
Set up the scenario
Imagine a fast runner starting after a slow runner. Understand that the fast must first reach where the slow was when the fast started.
This is the initial condition.
3
Follow the infinite regress
As the fast reaches each of the slow's previous positions, the slow has advanced to a new position. If divisions are infinite, this repeats forever - the fast never catches up.
This is the paradox.
4
Observe reality and conclude
In reality, fast runners do catch slow ones. Therefore the initial assumption of infinite divisions is false, and the sections of distance are finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن المسافة لو كانت مركبة من أجزاء غير متناهية، لكان لا يمكن الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها إلا بعد الوصول إلى نصفها ولا يمكن الوصول إلى نصفها إلا بعد الوصول إلى ربعها فلو كانت أجزاء غير متناهية، لامتنع الوصول من أولها إلى آخرها في مدة متناهية ولما لم يكن ذلك ممتنعا، علمنا: أن المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة متناهية. الثالث: لو كانت المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة غير متناهية، لامتنع أن يصل السريع إلى البطيء؛ وذلك لأنه إذا ابتدأ السريع بعده فإن وصل السريع إلى الموضع الذي وصل إليه البطيء، يكون البطيء قد قطع بعض الأجزاء وإلى أن يصل السريع أيضا إلى ذلك الموضع الثاني يكون قد وصل البطيء إلى موضع ثالث فلو حصل في الجسم مقاطع غير متناهية لكان كلما وصل السريع إلى موضع، يكون البطيء قد تعداه. وكان يجب أن لا يصل السريع إلى البطيء وحيث يصل إليه، علمنا المقاطع الحاصلة في المسافة متناهية.
The second: that if the distance were composed of infinite parts, it would not be possible to reach from its beginning to its end except after reaching its half, and it would not be possible to reach its half except after reaching its quarter. So if the parts were infinite, it would be impossible to reach from its beginning to its end in a finite time. Since that is not impossible, we know that the sections (intervals) occurring in the distance are finite. The third: if the sections occurring in the distance were infinite, it would be impossible for the fast to reach the slow; that is because when the fast starts after it (the slow), then if the fast reaches the position which the slow had reached, the slow has covered some parts; and before the fast reaches that second position, the slow has reached a third position. So if there occurred in the body infinite sections, then whenever the fast reaches a position, the slow would have passed it. And it would be necessary that the fast does not reach the slow. But since it does reach it, we know that the sections occurring in the distance are finite.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage contains two more proofs, both based on motion. The second proof is the paradox of traversing a divided distance: if a distance has infinite intermediate points, you can never reach the end in finite time, because you must first traverse half, then half of the remaining, etc., infinitely. Since we do reach ends, the divisions must be finite. The third proof is the famous 'Achilles and the tortoise' paradox: if the slow moves ahead, the fast must first reach where the slow was, but by then the slow has moved further; if divisions are infinite, this continues forever and the fast never catches up. But in reality, the fast does catch up, so the divisions cannot be infinite. Both proofs rely on the observable fact of motion to refute infinite divisibility of space.

Key Terms
مقاطع
سريع
بطيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof shows that motion is an illusion.
Correction: Razi uses the reality of motion as evidence; he does not deny motion but rather uses it to prove the finiteness of spatial divisions.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the observation that a fast runner catches a slow runner?
A) Space is infinitely divisible
B) The sections in the distance are finite
C) Motion is impossible
D) The fast runner is faster than the slow

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the observed fact that a fast runner catches a slow one to refute infinite divisibility of space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If infinite divisions cannot be traversed, what does that imply about the number of chances you have to turn back to Allah? Are you assuming infinite tomorrows?
?
Razi uses the fact that a fast runner catches a slow one to prove the world has a finite past. How does the reality of your own finite lifespan affect your urgency to seek Allah's pleasure?
?
The paradox shows that infinite regress is impossible. Where in your life are you stuck in an infinite loop of excuses? What finite step will you take today to break it?

Philosophers' First Proof: Infinite Division from Direction and Contiguity
Razi presents the philosophers' first proof using direction and contiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument attempting to prove infinite imaginary division by analyzing the directionality and contiguity of extended objects.

Core Idea
The very notion of an indivisible atom leads to logical absurdities when considered with sides and contact.

The philosophers aim to show that division can continue infinitely in the mind. Their first argument examines what it means for something to have distinct directions and how atoms would have to touch each other. By pointing out the absurdity of interpenetration, they argue that even the smallest imagined part must have sides, hence be divisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first proof
Read the Arabic passage that presents the first proof, including both the direction argument and the line-of-three-atoms illustration.
Notice that the proof has two formulations; both aim at the same conclusion.
2
Understand the direction argument
The first formulation argues that any extended thing has distinct right/left, front/back, etc., so it is composite, not an indivisible part.
3
Analyze the three-atom illustration
Consider a line of three contiguous atoms. If the middle atom touched both neighbors with the same part, it would interpenetrate, and three atoms would not be larger than one—an absurdity. So the middle atom must touch each neighbor with a different side, implying it has sides and is divisible.
This reductio ad absurdum is central to the argument.
4
Conclude the significance
The conclusion is that the indivisible atom is divisible, supporting the claim that imaginary division is infinite.
This proof challenges the concept of a minimal particle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد ادعوا أولا أن القسمة الوهمية غير متناهية. واحتجوا عليه بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: أن كل ما كان متحيزا مختصا بجهة فإن يمينه غير يساره، وقدامه غير خلفه وفوقه غير تحته وكل ما كان كذلك، فهو مركب من هذه الجوانب الستة، فلا يكون فردا بل يكون منقسما وربما عبروا عن هذا الكلام بعبارة أخرى. أنا لو فرضنا خطا مركبا من ثلاثة جواهر متماسة، فالوسط يلاقي ما على يمينه يلاقي ما على يساره أو بغيره؟ والأول باطل، وإلا لكان كل واحد من الطرفين ملاقيا كلية ذات الوسط. وهذا لا يكون ملاقاة بل يكون مداخلة، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد من الطرفين مداخلا بكليته في كلية ذات الوسط وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون مجموع الأجزاء الثلاثة أزيد في المقدار من الجزء الواحد. وحينئذ لا يكون تألف هذه الأجزاء سببا لازدياد القدر والحجم، ولا تكون الأعظام متألفة وكل ذلك باطل. ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن الوسط شيء يماس ما على يمينه بغير الجانب الذي به يماس ما على يساره. وإذا كان كذلك كان الجوهر الفرد منقسما. الحجة الثانية لهم: أنا إذا فرضنا سطحا مركبا من جواهر لا تتجزأ فإذا وقع الضوء على أحد ذلك السطح، صار ذلك الوجه مضيئا والوجه الآخر منه لا وجهي يصير مضيئا والمضيء مغاير لما ليس بمضيء فكل واحد من تلك الجواهر التي منها تركب ذلك السطح يكون أحد وجهيه مضيئا والآخر غير مضيء، فيكون كل واحد منهما منقسما.
As for the philosophers, they claimed first that imaginary division is infinite. They argued for it with proofs: The first proof: that every thing that occupies space and is distinguished by a direction, its right is not its left, its front is not its back, its above is not its below, and everything that is like that is composed of these six directions, so it is not an indivisible part but is divisible. And perhaps they expressed this statement in another way: if we assume a line composed of three contiguous atoms (jawhar), then the middle one touches what is on its right and what is on its left, or not? The first is false; otherwise each of the two ends would touch the entire essence of the middle. This would not be touching but interpenetration, then each of the two ends would interpenetrate in its entirety into the entirety of the essence of the middle, and on this assumption the total of the three parts would not be greater in magnitude than the single part. Then the composition of these parts would not be a cause for increase of size and volume, and bodies would not be composed. All that is false. Since this division is false, it is established that the middle thing touches what is on its right with a side other than the side with which it touches what is on its left. If that is the case, then the indivisible atom is divisible. The second proof for them: if we assume a surface composed of indivisible atoms, and if light falls on one side of that surface, that face becomes illuminated and the other face of it does not become illuminated; the illuminated is other than the non-illuminated. So each one of those atoms of which that surface is composed has one of its faces illuminated and the other not illuminated; so each one of them is divisible.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' case for infinite imaginary division. They give two proofs. The first proof argues that any extended thing has distinct sides (right/left, etc.), so it is composite, thus divisible. They illustrate this with a line of three atoms: if the middle atom touches both neighbors with the same part, it would interpenetrate, and three atoms would not be larger than one—absurd; so it must touch each neighbor with a different side, meaning the atom itself has sides, hence divisible. This leads to infinite division in principle. The second proof uses light: on a surface of indivisible atoms, one face is illuminated, the other is not; thus each atom has distinct parts (illuminated and non-illuminated), so it is divisible. Both arguments aim to show that the concept of an indivisible atom (جوهر فرد) is untenable, implying that division can proceed without end.

Key Terms
قسمة وهمية
جوهر فرد
متحيز
مداخلة
ملامسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that imaginary division is the same as physically dividing something into smaller pieces.
Correction: The philosophers are arguing that in the mind (وهم) we can always conceive of further division, not that physical division is actually infinite in act.

Checkpoint
In the philosophers' first proof, what would happen if the middle atom touched both neighbors with the same part?
A) The three atoms would increase in size
B) The ends would interpenetrate the middle
C) The middle would be smaller than the ends
D) The atoms would be indivisible

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that assuming indivisible atoms leads to contradictions, suggesting divisibility is inherent to extended things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers argue that anything occupying space has distinct directions and therefore parts. Reflect on your own body: do you experience it as a unity or as a composite of parts? How does this simple observation affect the way you see yourself as a creature dependent on a Creator? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not a simple, self-sufficient atom but a composite being in need of a Maker?
?
The middle atom in the proof must have distinct sides to touch its neighbors without interpenetration. Think about your relationships with the people around you. Do you sometimes try to connect with everyone using the same 'side' of yourself, expecting no conflict? Where in your life are you experiencing the interpenetration of roles or confusion of boundaries? What concrete step will you take this week to develop distinct 'sides' (aspects) for different relationships, so that you can touch each person genuinely without losing yourself?

Philosophers' Second Proof: Infinite Division from Light and Surfaces
Razi presents the philosophers' second proof using light on a surface.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that uses differing illumination on faces of atoms to argue that atoms are divisible.

Core Idea
If an atom has one illuminated face and one dark face, it must have distinct parts, thus divisible.

The philosophers present a second argument based on a surface composed of indivisible atoms. When light falls on one side, only that face becomes illuminated. Since the illuminated and non-illuminated faces are distinct, each atom must have at least two parts, making it divisible. This again supports infinite imaginary division.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic passage describing the surface of atoms and light falling on one side.
This proof is brief but powerful.
2
Understand the scenario
Assume a surface made of indivisible atoms. Light falls on one side of the surface, so that side becomes illuminated while the other side remains dark.
3
Identify the implication for each atom
Each atom has one face that is illuminated and one face that is not. Therefore, each atom has two distinct parts (the illuminated part and the non-illuminated part), so it is divisible.
The atom is not uniform; it has at least two distinguishable aspects.
4
Conclude the significance
Thus, even the smallest imagined particle must have parts, supporting the claim that imaginary division is infinite.
This proof relies on empirical observation of light.

As for the philosophers, they claimed first that imaginary division is infinite. They argued for it with proofs: The first proof: that every thing that occupies space and is distinguished by a direction, its right is not its left, its front is not its back, its above is not its below, and everything that is like that is composed of these six directions, so it is not an indivisible part but is divisible. Recreating the Non-Existent: Positions and Proof
Ar-Razi enumerates the positions on i'ādat al-ma'dūm and begins the Ash'ari proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on recreating the non-existent and the Ash'ari argument that God's power can recreate even after the essence has perished.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris affirm what seems impossible: recreating something from nothingness.

This lesson presents the classic theological debate over whether a thing that has completely ceased can be brought back into existence identically. Ar-Razi surveys the views of philosophers, Mu'tazila, and Ash'aris, then begins the Ash'ari proof based on the persistence of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three positions
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note how Razi classifies the philosophers, the Mu'tazila, and the Ash'aris. What does each group say about re-creating the non-existent?
The Mu'tazila base their view on the persistence of the essence; the Ash'aris on essential possibility.
2
Understand the key difference between Mu'tazila and Ash'aris
The Mu'tazila say the non-existent retains its particular essence. The Ash'aris say the essence perishes entirely. Despite this, both affirm re-creation is possible—but for different reasons.
The Ash'ari position is unique: they affirm re-creation without any substrate.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari proof
Razi argues: after non-existence, the thing remains 'possible of existence'. Since God is powerful over all possibles, He can re-create that thing. The possibility must be either essential (permanent) or accidental (but then why would it cease?).
The proof is cut off, but the core logic is clear: essential possibility does not cease with existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثلاثون
وفيه فصول :
في المعاد
الفصل الأول في إعادة المعدوم
اختلف العقلاء في أن الشيء إذا عدم وفني. فهل يمكن إعادته بعينه مرة أخرى، أم لا ؟
أما الفلاسفة فقد اتفقوا على أنه محال . وهو قول أبي الحسين البصري) و( محمود الخوارزمي).
وأما جملة مشايخ المعتزلة : فقد اتفقوا على أن إعادة المعدوم ممكنة، إلا أنهم فرعوا هذه المسألة على مذهبهم، وذلك بأن عندهم المعدوم شيء، والشيء إذا عدم لم تبطل ذاته المخصوصة، بل زالت صفة الوجود عنه، ولما كانت ذاته المخصوصة باقية حالتي العدم والوجود، لا جرم قالوا : إعادة المعدوم جائزة.
وأما أصحابنا فإنهم يقولون : الشيء إذا عدم فقد بطلت ذاته، وصار نفيا محضا وعدما صرفا ولم تبق له حال العدم هوية ولا خصوصية. ثم إنهم مع هذا المذهب قالوا : إنه لا يمتنع في قدرة الله إعادته بعينه. وهذا القول لم يقل به أحد من طوائف العقلاء إلا أصحابنا.
والذي يدل على صحة هذا القول : أن الشيء إذا صار معدوماً، فإنه بعد العدم بقي جائز الوجود، والله تعالى قادر على جميع الجائزات فوجب القطع بكونه تعالى قادرًا على إعادته بعينه، بعد العدم
وإنما قلنا : إنه بعد عدمه بقي جائز الوجود؛ لأنه قبل عدمه جائز الوجود وهذا الجواز إما أن يكون من لوازم حقيقته، وإما أن يكون من عوارض حقيقته. فإن كان من لوازم حقيقته، وجب أن لا يزول . وإن كان من عوارض حقيقته كانت تلك
The thirtieth issue. It contains chapters: On the Afterlife. The first chapter: On the re-creation of the non-existent. The rational thinkers disagreed: when a thing perishes and ceases, can it be re-created identically again or not? As for the philosophers, they agreed that it is impossible. This is also the saying of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and Mahmud al-Khwarazmi. As for the group of Mu'tazila scholars, they agreed that the re-creation of the non-existent is possible; however, they based this issue on their doctrine, namely that according to them the non-existent is a thing, and when a thing ceases its particular essence does not perish, rather the attribute of existence leaves it; and since its particular essence remains in both states of non-existence and existence, they necessarily said: re-creation of the non-existent is permissible. As for our companions (the Ash'aris), they say: when a thing ceases, its essence perishes, and it becomes pure negation and sheer non-existence, and in the state of non-existence no identity or particularity remains for it. Then, despite this doctrine, they said: it is not impossible in God's power to re-create it identically. No one among the groups of rational thinkers has said this except our companions. What indicates the correctness of this saying is that when a thing becomes non-existent, after non-existence it remains possible of existence, and God (exalted) is powerful over all possibles; therefore one must decisively affirm that He (exalted) is powerful to re-create it identically after non-existence. We say that after its non-existence it remains possible of existence because before its non-existence it was possible of existence, and this possibility is either a necessary concomitant of its essence or an accident of its essence. If it is a necessary concomitant, then it must not cease. If it is an accident of its essence, then that...
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the 30th issue on the afterlife, specifically the first chapter on re-creating the non-existent. It first classifies the positions: the philosophers and some Mu'tazila (like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri) deny it; the majority of Mu'tazila affirm it based on their view that the non-existent retains its essence; and the Ash'aris affirm it despite holding that the essence perishes entirely. Then it gives the Ash'ari proof: after non-existence, the thing remains possible of existence (since its possibility is either essential or accidental, and if essential it doesn't cease), and God is powerful over all possibles, so He can recreate it. The proof is cut off, but the argument is that the possibility persists, enabling divine power to re-create.

Key Terms
إعادة المعدوم
عدم
وجود
ذات مخصوصة
جائز الوجود
قدرة
لازم
عارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari position is contradictory: if the essence perishes, it cannot be re-created.
Correction: The Ash'aris argue that the thing's 'possibility of existence' (jā'iz al-wujūd) persists even after the essence perishes. This possibility is a property that does not depend on actual existence, so God can bring the thing back into existence.

Checkpoint
According to Ar-Razi, what is the unique Ash'ari position on re-creating the non-existent?
A) It is impossible because the thing's essence perishes.
B) It is possible because the thing's essence remains.
C) It is possible even though the thing's essence perishes.
D) It is impossible because God cannot repeat an act.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is unique: recreation of the non-existent is possible even though the essence perishes, grounded in the persistence of the thing's possibility of existence and God's unlimited power over all possibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can recreate you exactly as you are after your death, even when everything about you—your body, your memories, your very essence—has turned to nothing? What does that say about your trust in His power?
?
If the Ash'aris alone dared to affirm the recreation of the non-existent, what does that teach you about following truth even when it seems impossible to reason? Is there an area of your faith where you have settled for what seems 'reasonable' rather than what Allah has revealed?
?
The proof in this lesson rests on the persistence of possibility. In the same way, your deeds in this life are 'possible' for Allah to recreate and weigh. Does the thought that nothing—not even your smallest action—escapes His ability to bring back and judge move you to change how you live today?

Proof by Equality of Quiddities
Razi presents the first proof for the possibility of perforation and rejoining of celestial spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's first proof that all bodies are equal in essence, making perforation and rejoining possible for celestial spheres.

Core Idea
All bodies share the same essence; what is possible for one is possible for all.

Imam Razi begins constructing a rational case for the possibility of celestial bodies being rent and rejoined. He introduces a proof by way of the equality of all bodies in their complete quiddities, a principle already established in the earlier proof of the Creator. The passage lays out the premise and conclusion concisely, relying on prior arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis and the opponents
Read the opening sentence: the chapter announces that perforation and rejoining are possible on celestial spheres, and notes that the philosophers deny this. Notice that Razi frames the discussion as a refutation.
This sets up the dialectical context.
2
Find the number of proofs
Razi states there are two proofs. Our passage contains only the first. Make a mental note that the second will follow later.
3
Deconstruct the first proof
The proof consists of two premises: all bodies are equal in their complete quiddities; if that is so, then what is possible for one is possible for all. Trace how the conclusion follows logically.
The equality of quiddities was established earlier; Razi references that.
4
Reflect on the scope of the argument
Consider that this proof does not use empirical observation of celestial spheres but relies on an a priori metaphysical principle. This is typical of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث: في بيان أن الخرق والالتئام جائزان على أجرام الفلك. الفلاسفة ينكرون ذلك ولنا : على صحته وجهان الحجة الأولى : أن جميع الأجسام متساوية وتمام الماهيات، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك فكل ما صح على واحد منها صح على كلها وتقرير هاتين المقدمتين قد تقدم في مسألة إثبات الصانع سبحانه وتعالى.
The third chapter: On explaining that perforation and rejoining are possible for the celestial spheres. The philosophers deny that, and we have two proofs for its validity. The first proof: All bodies are equal in their complete quiddities, and when that is the case, whatever is possible for one of them is possible for all of them. The establishment of these two premises has preceded in the problem of proving the Creator, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage gives the first proof for the possibility of perforation (خرق) and rejoining (التئام) on celestial spheres (أجرام الفلك). The author states the thesis and then provides the proof: all bodies are equal in their complete essences (تمام الماهيات). Based on this equality, whatever is possible for one body is possible for all. The author notes that these premises were already established earlier in the argument for the existence of the Creator, so they are not repeated here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is based on empirical observation of celestial spheres.
Correction: No; the proof relies on the a priori principle of equality of quiddities, which was established in a previous argument for the existence of God.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Equality in quiddity across all bodies entails that what is possible for one body is possible for any other, including celestial spheres.

?
Razi used this proof to defend the possibility of miracles. Is there a situation in your life where you have dismissed a possibility because it seemed too 'heavenly' or beyond your experience, forgetting that the same God governs all? What would it look like to trust in His power over all bodies?

Introduction to the Annihilation of the World's Bodies
Razi introduces the topic of annihilation, two opinions, and the rational/revelation distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between rational possibility and actual occurrence regarding the annihilation of the world, and the two scholarly opinions presented by Razi.

Core Idea
Possibility is rational; actuality requires revelation.

Razi begins by reminding us that reason alone can establish that everything other than God is possible of non-existence, but possibility does not mean it will happen. He then presents two differing views among Muslim scholars on whether God annihilates the world's bodies. This lesson sets the stage by clarifying the epistemological boundary between what reason can prove and what must come from revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the flow: Razi starts with a rational premise, then mentions disagreement, then states the key methodological point.
The passage ends with a colon, indicating that Razi will bring proofs from revelation in the continuation.
2
Identify the two opinions
Look for the two groups: one says God annihilates essences and parts then recreates; the other says He only disintegrates and then re-forms. Understand the difference.
3
Grasp the epistemological distinction
Razi says rational possibility can be proven by reason, but the actual occurrence of what is possible can only be established by revelation. This is a key principle in Islamic theology.
This principle limits the scope of rational proof and shows dependence on scripture for matters of occurrence.
4
Note Razi's neutrality here
Razi does not favor either opinion in this introduction; he is simply presenting the spectrum of views and setting up the methodological framework. The actual argument will come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع في أن الله تعالى هل يعدم أجسام العالم أم لا ؟ اعلم أنا وإن بينا بالدلالة العقلية أن كل ما سوى الله تعالى فإنه يصح العدم عليه، ولكن ليس كل ما صح ،وقع، وقد اختلف علماء الإسلام في ذلك، فقال قوم: إنه تعالى يعدم الذوات والأجزاء ثم يعيدها وقال آخرون إنه تعالى لا يعدمها، بل يفرق أجزاء السماوات والأرض ويخربها، ثم إنه تعالى يؤلفها مرة أخرى كما كانت، واعلم أن الجواز العقلي يمكن إثباته بدلائل العقل أما وقوع الجائز فلا سبيل إلى إثباته إلا بالسمع. واحتج القاطعون على أنه تعالى يعدم العالم بآيات :
The fourth chapter: On whether God Almighty annihilates the bodies of the world or not. Know that although we have shown by rational proof that everything other than God Almighty is subject to non-existence, not everything that is possible occurs. Indeed, the scholars of Islam differed on this. Some said: He annihilates the essences and parts then recreates them. Others said: He does not annihilate them, rather He scatters the parts of the heavens and the earth and destroys them, then He composes them again as they were. And know that rational possibility can be established by rational proofs, but the actual occurrence of what is possible can only be established by revelation. And those who are certain that He annihilates the world adduced by verses:
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage introduces chapter 4. The author states that reason shows everything besides God is possible of non-existence (يصح العدم), but possibility does not entail actual occurrence. He then presents two scholarly opinions: one holds that God annihilates the essences (ذوات) and parts (أجزاء) and then recreates them; the other holds that He does not annihilate them but rather disintegrates the heavens and earth and then re-forms them. The author emphasizes a key methodological point: rational possibility can be proven by reason, but the actual occurrence of what is possible can only be known from revelation (السمع). He then mentions that those who assert annihilation have proof from verses (the text cuts off).

Key Terms

ذوات
أجزاء
جواز عقلي
وقوع
سمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is taking a side in this introduction.
Correction: Razi is not taking a side here; he is presenting the two opinions and clarifying the epistemological distinction between rational possibility and actual occurrence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, how can we know whether God actually annihilates the world?
A) Through rational proof alone
B) Through revelation
C) Through consensus of scholars
D) Through observation

Key Takeaway
The possibility of annihilation is rational, but its actual occurrence is known only through revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your death is logically possible at any moment, but do you live with the certainty that it will happen? What revelation from Allah do you need to transform that possibility into a lived actuality?
?
In what areas of your life have you assumed that just because something is possible, it will happen—without checking what Allah has actually revealed about it? How can you realign your expectations with divine guidance?
?
Razi separates rational possibility from actual occurrence. Do you treat the Day of Judgment as a mere possibility or as an actual certainty? If it is certain by revelation, why does your daily life not reflect that urgency?

The Argument from Pleasure for the Afterlife
Razi argues for the necessity of the afterlife from the nature of pleasure as pain-repulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the analysis of worldly pleasure as pain-repulsion to argue for the necessity of an afterlife where true ease is attained.

Core Idea
Worldly pleasures are only pain-repulsion, so true ease demands another world.

Razi offers a thought-provoking argument for the afterlife grounded not in scripture but in the nature of human experience. He examines what we call pleasure and finds it is merely the removal of pain, suggesting our ultimate purpose—ease—cannot be fulfilled in this life. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step, noting its persuasive rather than demonstrative character.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Razi's argument.
Notice the rhetorical question at the start and the example-driven reasoning.
2
Follow the argument's structure
Identify the main claims: (1) God created humans for ease, not pain; (2) worldly pleasures are only pain-repulsion; (3) therefore, ease must be in the afterlife.
Use the guided breakdown to see the logical steps.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples of eating and intercourse. How do they illustrate the idea that pleasure is merely pain-repulsion? Do you find this convincing?
Razi argues that all worldly pleasures follow the same pattern.
4
Understand the persuasive nature
Razi calls this a 'persuasive method' (طريقة إقناعية). Reflect on why he considers it not a demonstrative proof.
The argument relies on empirical observation and analogy, not necessary rational truth.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
Consider how this argument fits within Islamic theology as one of the proofs for the physical resurrection (المعاد الجسماني).
This argument complements scriptural and rational proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال : خلقهم للتعب والألم، لأن هذا لا يليق بالمحسن الرحيم، الغني عن الخلق، ولا جائز أن يقال : خلقهم لا للراحة ولا للتعب والألم ؛ لأنهم حال كونهم معدومين، كان هذا المعنى حاصلا فدل على أنه تعالى إنما خلقهم للراحة. ثم نقول : هذه الراحة إما أن تكون في هذا العالم أو في عالم آخر، لا جائز أن يقال: إنها في هذا العالم؛ لأن الذي يظنه الإنسان لذة في هذا العالم، فهو ليس بلذة، بل هو دفع الألم، مثاله : أن لذة الأكل ليست في الحقيقة لذة، بل هي دفع ألم الجوع، ولذلك فإن ألذ اللقم هي اللقمة الأولى؛ لأن هناك ألم الجوع أقوى، وكل هي أكثر تأخرًا، كانت أقل لذة، وذلك لأن ألم الجوع هناك أقل قوة، وكذلك لذة الوقاع عبارة عن دفع الألم، وذلك لأن الفضلة التي يتولد منها المني لما اجتمعت في أوعية المني حاولت الانفصال فقبل الانفصال يحصل نوع ألم بسبب اجتماعها في تلك الأوعية، فإذا انفصلت زال ذلك الألم، وعلى هذا القياس سائر اللذات الحاصلة في هذا العالم. وأيضا : فبتقدير أن تحصل في هذا العالم لذة جسمانية لكنها قليلة، والغالب إما الألم أو دفع الألم، وليس من الحكمة إلقاء الحيوان في بحر الآلام والمكروهات لأجل أن يعود إليه ذرة من اللذات ولما ثبت أن الحيوان إنما خلق لأجل اللذة والراحة ثبت أن ذلك المقصود غير حاصل في هذا العالم فلا بد من القطع بوجود عالم آخر بعد هذا العالم يحصل فيه هذا المقصود، وذلك هو الدار الآخرة. فهذه طريقة إقناعية والاعتماد على ما تقدم، وهذا حاصل الكلام في المعاد الجسماني.
It is said: He created them for fatigue and pain, because this is not befitting the Benevolent, the Merciful, free from need of creation. And it is not permissible to say: He created them neither for ease nor for fatigue and pain; because while they were nonexistent, this meaning was already present. So it indicates that He, exalted, created them only for ease. Then we say: This ease is either in this world or in another world. It is not permissible to say that it is in this world, because what a person supposes to be pleasure in this world is not in fact pleasure, but is the repelling of pain. For example: the pleasure of eating is not in reality pleasure, but it is the repelling of the pain of hunger. Therefore, the most delicious morsels are the first morsel, because there the pain of hunger is strongest, and every morsel that comes later is less delicious, because the pain of hunger is weaker. Likewise, the pleasure of intercourse is an expression of repelling pain, because when the surplus from which semen is generated gathers in the seminal vessels, it strives to separate, and before separation a kind of pain occurs due to its gathering in those vessels; when it separates, that pain ceases. And on this analogy are all other pleasures that occur in this world. Also, assuming that a bodily pleasure occurs in this world, it is little, and the dominant is either pain or the repelling of pain. It is not wise to cast the animal into a sea of pains and disliked things for the sake of returning to it a speck of pleasures. And since it has been established that the animal was created only for pleasure and ease, it is established that that purpose is not attained in this world, so it is necessary to affirm the existence of another world after this world in which this purpose is attained, and that is the Abode of the Hereafter. This is a persuasive method, and reliance is on what preceded. And this is the gist of the discourse on physical resurrection.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents a persuasive argument (طريقة إقناعية) for the necessity of the afterlife, specifically the physical resurrection. The argument begins by considering why God created humans. It first refutes the idea that He created them for fatigue and pain, as that is incompatible with His mercy and self-sufficiency. It also refutes the idea that He created them for neither ease nor pain, as that would add nothing to their state of nonexistence. Therefore, the purpose must be ease (الراحة). The author then asks whether this ease occurs in this world or another. To show it cannot be in this world, he argues that what we call pleasure in this world is merely the repulsion of pain. Examples are given: the pleasure of eating is relief from hunger, and the pleasure of intercourse is relief from built-up tension. All pleasures are only removal of pain. Moreover, any actual bodily pleasure in this world is minimal, while pain and pain-repulsion dominate. It would be inconsistent with divine wisdom (الحكمة) to expose animals to a sea of suffering for a speck of pleasure. Since the purpose is ease and it is not realized in this world, another world must exist where it is fully realized, which is the Hereafter. The author notes that this is a persuasive argument, not a demonstrative one, and the main dependence is on what preceded (referring to earlier proofs).

Key Terms
الراحة
اللذة
دفع الألم
المعاد الجسماني
طريقة إقناعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is a demonstrative proof (دليل قطعي) for the afterlife.
Correction: Razi explicitly states it is a persuasive method (طريقة إقناعية), not a certain proof, and reliance is on earlier demonstrative arguments.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why can the purpose of creation (ease) not be fully realized in this world?
A) Because God's mercy prevents it.
B) Because worldly pleasures are merely pain-repulsion and are minimal, while pain dominates.
C) Because humans are inherently sinful.
D) Because scripture tells us so.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument from pleasure shows that the human desire for ease cannot be fully satisfied in this world, pointing to the necessity of an afterlife where true pleasure and ease exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your daily pursuits—your work, your relationships, your hobbies—are you primarily seeking to remove discomfort (like hunger, boredom, loneliness) or are you seeking genuine, lasting ease? If worldly pleasures are merely pain-repulsion, why do you keep chasing them as if they will satisfy your soul? Today, will you stop pretending temporary relief is enough and instead fix your heart on the lasting ease of the Hereafter?
?
Razi says it is not wise to cast a being into a sea of pain for a speck of pleasure. Yet you often accept a life dominated by stress, worry, and toil for brief moments of happiness. Do you truly believe that your purpose is ease in the next world? If so, why do you still live as if this world owes you happiness? Name one specific area in your life where you can begin to detach from worldly expectation and redirect your hope to Allah's promise of rest.
?
You know that your deepest longings for peace and contentment are not fully met here. What is stopping you from letting go of the illusion that this world can give you true rest? Will you admit that the ache inside you is a signpost pointing to the afterlife? If you were to die tonight, where would your ease be? Let this awareness drive you to seek the only pleasure that is not merely the absence of pain: the pleasure of being with your Lord.

Second Proof: No Memory of Past States
Razi argues that if the soul were eternal, one would remember past states, but we do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second argument that the soul is created (not eternal) based on the fact that we have no memory of any past existence before our current life.

Core Idea
If the soul were eternal, we would remember its past; we don't, so it isn't.

Razi presents a second, powerful argument against the eternity of the soul. He grounds it in our immediate self-awareness: each of us refers to ourselves as 'I'. If that 'I' were eternal, logic demands that we would retain some memory of its infinite past. Since we have absolutely no such recollection, the soul cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage aloud or silently. Let the words and structure sink in.
Notice the key term الأنا (the 'I') – it is central to the argument.
2
Understand the core claim
Razi equates the soul with the particular self that says 'I'. Grasp this identification – it sets up the conditional argument.
3
Examine the analogy
Consider the craft analogy: someone who practiced a craft for years cannot completely forget it. This illustrates that an eternal being would necessarily have some memory of its infinite past.
The analogy is used to show implausibility, not logical impossibility.
4
Reflect on the observed fact
We have zero recollection of any pre-bodily state. This empirical observation is the crux of the proof.
Ask yourself: do I have any memory from before my birth?
5
Conclude with the author
Since the observed fact contradicts the necessary consequence of eternity, the premise that souls are eternal is false.
The conclusion is that souls are created (hadith).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني: أن نفسي عبارة عن ذاتي المخصوصة التي أشير إليها بقولي: "أنا" فلو كانت النفس قديمة لكنت أنا من حيث أنا أزلياً قديما، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لوجب أن أتذكر شيئًا من تلك الأحوال الماضية، فإن من المحال أن يمارس الإنسان سنين حرفة من الحرف ثم إنه ينساها بالكلية ولا يتذكر منها شيئًا من تلك الأحوال البتة. ولما لم يحصل عندنا تذكر شيء من تلك الأحوال الماضية علمنا أن القول يكون النفوس أزلية باطل، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن النفوس ليست أزلية.
The second reason: My soul is my particular self which I refer to by my saying 'I'. So if the soul were pre-eternal, then I, inasmuch as I am I, would be pre-eternal and ancient. And if that were the case, it would be necessary that I remember something of those past states, for it is impossible that a person practices a craft for years then completely forgets it and remembers nothing of those states at all. Since we do not have any recollection of those past states, we know that the statement that souls are eternal is false. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that souls are not eternal.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that souls are not eternal. The argument is based on the concept of the self (الأنا). The author equates the soul with the individual self that one refers to as 'I'. If the soul were pre-eternal, then that 'I' would be pre-eternal. But if the 'I' had existed for an infinite time before the body, it would inevitably have experienced numerous past states and events. Given the human capacity for memory, it is inconceivable that such a long history would leave no trace. Just as a person who practiced a craft for many years must remember something of it, the eternal soul would surely retain some memory of its pre-bodily existence. Since we have absolutely no recollection of any such past states, the premise that the soul is eternal must be false. Therefore, the soul is not eternal; it comes into existence at some point. This concludes the refutation of the second view that all souls perish because they are not eternal and thus not everlasting.

Key Terms
الأنا
الذات المخصوصة
التذكر
الأحوال الماضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the lack of memory could be due to divine will or trauma, so the argument is not persuasive.
Correction: Razi's point is not that it is logically impossible to forget, but that it is implausible that an eternal being would have absolutely zero memory of its infinite past. The analogy of the craftsman shows that even with great effort, some memory persists. The complete absence is incompatible with eternity.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence used in the second proof to deny the soul's eternity?
A) The soul is simple.
B) There is no recollection of any past states before bodily life.
C) The body is the substrate of the soul.
D) The soul cannot be known.

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be eternal because if it were, the self would have some memory of its infinite past, yet we have none.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have no memory of any existence before this life. If the soul were eternal, wouldn't you expect to have at least a faint recollection? What does this total blank in your memory tell you about your soul's origin? How does that shape your gratitude to the Creator who brought you into being from nothing?
?
Consider the 'I' that you are so familiar with—your continuous sense of self. Razi argues that this 'I' cannot be eternal because it doesn't remember the past. When you reflect on your own consciousness, do you sense that it had a beginning? How does that affect your understanding of your own mortality and your relationship with the Eternal?
?
If your soul were uncreated and eternal, you would be in some sense coeternal with God. Does that thought make you uncomfortable? Why? This argument seeks to affirm that you are a contingent being, entirely dependent on Allah. Do you live your life as if you are contingent, or do you sometimes act as if you are self-sufficient?

Two Proofs That Souls Are Not Pre-Eternal
Al-Razi refutes the pre-eternity of the soul with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments from Al-Razi proving that human souls are not pre-eternal (azali), and why this refutes the view that souls perish at bodily death.

Core Idea
Souls cannot be pre-eternal because pre-eternal multiplicity is impossible and personal memory fails.

Al-Razi here tackles a crucial question in Islamic theology: are human souls pre-eternal? He presents two crisp arguments to show they are not. Understanding these proofs will sharpen your grasp of how kalam reasoning uses categories like unity/multiplicity and essential/accidental distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi presents two distinct proofs introduced by 'الوجه الأول' and 'الوجه الثاني'.
2
Analyse the first proof's disjunction
The first proof argues: if souls were pre-eternal, they would be either one or many. Both options lead to absurdity. Work through each horn.
The proof uses the principle that pre-eternal multiplicity requires a distinguisher (mumayyiz), which cannot be essential or accidental before bodies exist.
3
Understand the second proof from memory
The second proof appeals to the fact that we have no memory of past states before birth. If the soul were eternal, it would have experienced countless states and some memory should remain.
This is an argument from self-evident experience (we remember nothing before birth).
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Both proofs conclude that souls are not pre-eternal. This is used to argue that they are not everlasting either, refuting the view that they perish at death.
The lesson only establishes non-pre-eternity; the link to non-everlastingness is given at the beginning of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القول الثاني - وهو أنها تفنى عند موت البدن ـ فقد احتجوا بأن هذه النفوس ليست أزلية، فوجب أن لا تكون أبدية.
وإنما قلنا : إنها ليست أزلية لوجهين :
الوجه الأول : أن النفوس لو كانت أزلية لكانت إما أن تكون واحدة أو كثيرة، والقسمان باطلان فبطل القول بكونها أزلية.
إنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونها واحدة لأنها بعد التعلق بالأبدان إن بقيت واحدة كان نفسي نفسك، وبالعكس، فكل ما كان معلوما لإنسان يجب أن يكون معلوما لكل إنسان. هذا خلف . وإن تعددت فهو محال ؛ لأن هاتين النفسين اللتين هما موجودتان الآن إن قلنا : إنهما كانتا موجودتين قبل التعلق بالأبدان، فقبل التعلق بالأبدان كانت النفوس كثيرة لا واحدة وإن قلنا بأنهما ما كانتا موجودتين، فهما قد حدثنا الآن فلا تكون هذه النفوس قديمة .
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونها متعددة في الأزل ؛ لأن ذلك التعدد لا بد فيه من مميز، وذلك المميز إما أن يكون ذاتيّا أو عرضياً، لا جائز أن يكون ذاتيا ؛ لأن النفوس البشرية متحدة بالنوع ولا جائز أن يكون عرضيّاً ؛ لأن الامتياز بالصفات العرضية تابع لاختلاف المواد ومادة النفس هي البدن فقبل التعلق بالأبدان ليس لها شيء من المواد فامتنع اختلافها بسبب الصفات العرضية، فثبت أن النفوس البشرية لو كانت أزلية لكانت في الأزل إما واحدة أو متعددة، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بكونها أزلية. والوجه الثاني: أن نفسي عبارة عن ذاتي المخصوصة التي أشير إليها بقولي: "أنا" فلو كانت النفس قديمة لكنت أنا من حيث أنا أزلياً قديما، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لوجب أن أتذكر شيئًا من تلك الأحوال الماضية، فإن من المحال أن يمارس الإنسان سنين حرفة من الحرف ثم إنه ينساها بالكلية ولا يتذكر منها شيئًا من تلك الأحوال البتة.
ولما لم يحصل عندنا تذكر شيء من تلك الأحوال الماضية علمنا أن القول يكون النفوس أزلية باطل، فثبت بما ذكرنا أن النفوس ليست أزلية.
As for the second statement — that they (souls) perish at the death of the body — they have argued that these souls are not pre-eternal (azaliyya), so it is necessary that they are not everlasting (abadiyya). And we say: they are not pre-eternal for two proofs. The first proof: that if souls were pre-eternal, they would be either one or many, and both divisions are false, so the statement that they are pre-eternal is false. We say: it is impossible for them to be one because, after attachment to bodies, if they remained one, then my soul would be your soul, and vice versa, so everything known to a person would have to be known to every person. This is absurd. And if they become multiple, it is impossible; because these two souls which now exist, if we say they existed before attachment to bodies, then before attachment to bodies souls were many, not one; and if we say they did not exist, then they have originated now, so these souls are not eternal (qadima). And we say: it is impossible for them to be multiple in pre-eternity (al-azal); because that multiplicity necessarily requires a distinguisher (mumayyiz), and that distinguisher must be either essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi). It cannot be essential because human souls are one in species; and it cannot be accidental because distinction by accidental attributes depends on difference of matters (mawadd), and the matter of the soul is the body, so before attachment to bodies they have no matters at all, so their difference due to accidental attributes is impossible. So it is established that if human souls were pre-eternal, they would in pre-eternity be either one or many, and both divisions are false, so the statement that they are pre-eternal is false. And the second proof: that my soul (nafsi) is an expression of my particular self (dhati al-makhsusa) which I refer to by my saying 'I'; so if the soul were eternal (qadima), then I, insofar as I am I, would be pre-eternal (azaliyan qadiman), and if that were the case, I would necessarily remember something of those past states; for it is impossible that a person practices a craft for years, then forgets it completely and does not remember any of those states at all. Since we have no remembrance of any of those past states, we know that the statement that souls are pre-eternal is false. So it is established by what we have mentioned that souls are not pre-eternal.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the view that the soul perishes at bodily death by first proving that the soul is not pre-eternal (azali), using two proofs. The passage begins by stating the opponents' claim: souls are not pre-eternal, so they cannot be everlasting. The author then supplies two proofs for the non-pre-eternity of the soul. The first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) argues by disjunction: if souls were pre-eternal, they would have to be either one or many. Both options are then shown to be impossible. If they were one, after attachment to bodies my soul would be identical to your soul, leading to universal knowledge — absurd. If they were many in pre-eternity, the multiplicity would require a distinguisher; this distinguisher cannot be essential because all human souls share the same species, nor accidental because accidental distinctions depend on matters (bodies), which do not yet exist. Hence, multiplicity in pre-eternity is impossible. Therefore, the disjunction is false. The second proof (al-wajh al-thani) appeals to personal identity and memory. The 'I' of each person refers to a unique self; if the soul were eternal, one would remember past states, yet we remember nothing from before birth. Therefore, the soul is not pre-eternal.

Key Terms
أزلية
نفس
تعلق
مميز
ذاتي
عرضي
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'pre-eternal' (azali) means 'everlasting' (abadi) and that proving non-pre-eternity automatically disproves existence after death.
Correction: The passage only argues that souls are not pre-eternal (without beginning). The conclusion that they are not everlasting (without end) is presented as following from the claim of opponents, not directly proven here.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why can souls not be one in pre-eternity?
A) Because they would then be identical after attachment, leading to universal knowledge.
B) Because they would be multiple by essence.
C) Because they would lack a distinguisher.
D) Because they would then be corporeal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's two proofs establish that human souls cannot be pre-eternal, using logical disjunction and the empirical absence of pre-natal memory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul has a beginning—it was created at a specific moment. Do you live as if you are a creature accountable to a Creator, or do you act as if you have always existed? What would change if you really internalized that your 'I' began at a point in time?
?
Al-Razi says that if your soul were eternal, you would remember something from before birth. You don't. Does this lack of memory make you stop and think about where your consciousness came from? How does this affect your sense of purpose?
?
If your soul is not pre-eternal, then it will not be everlasting unless God wills it. Are you preparing for an everlasting existence after this short life? What specific step will you take today to ensure your soul's well-being in the hereafter?

Razi's Defense of Ibrahim's Consistency
Razi defends Ibrahim's argument against the charge of inconsistency by distinguishing between the core proof and its examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi explains that Ibrahim's argument remained consistent despite changing examples.

Core Idea
The core argument was one; only the examples shifted.

This passage addresses a common objection that Ibrahim appeared to change his argument when faced with Nimrod's counter. Razi clarifies that the underlying proof—pointing to phenomena beyond human power—remained constant. The examples of life/death and the sun's rising are merely different illustrations of the same logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the accusation that Ibrahim's argument was defeated based on the verse (al-Baqara 258).
The verse shows Nimrod's challenge about the sun.
2
Identify Razi's answer
Notice that Razi states the argument was one thing: the origination of what man cannot produce.
This is the core logical proof.
3
See the two examples
The first example is giving life and causing death; the second is the sun's rising from the east.
Examples illustrate the same proof.
4
Grasp the distinction
Understand that moving from one example to another does not change the argument itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: قوله تعالى: {فَإِنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِبِ} [البقرة: ٢٥٨] وهذا يدل على أنه صار منقطعًا في الدليل الأول. والجواب: فيه أبحاث لطيفة مذكورة في كتاب أسرار التنزيل، والذي نقوله ههنا: إن الدليل كان شيئًا واحدا وهو حدوث ما لا يقدر الإنسان على إحداثه، فهو يدل على قادر آخر غير الخلق. ثم هذا المعنى له أمثلة: أحدها: الإحياء والإماتة. الثاني: طلوع الشمس من مشرقها، وهذا كان انتقالا من مثال إلى مثال، أما الدليل فشيء واحد في الحالين.
The fourth objection: His saying, exalted: 'For Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west' (al-Baqara 258). This indicates that he was defeated in the first argument. The answer: There are subtle investigations mentioned in the book Asrar al-Tanzil. What we say here is that the argument was one thing, namely the origination of what man is unable to produce, so it indicates a power other than the creation. Then this meaning has examples: one of them is giving life and causing death; the second is the rising of the sun from its east. This was a transition from example to example, but the argument itself is one thing in both cases.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents an objection that Ibrahim appeared to change his argument when Nimrod countered his first claim about giving life and death. The objection says Ibrahim was defeated because he moved to a different argument about the sun. Razi answers that the core argument remained the same: pointing to phenomena that humans cannot produce, thereby proving a transcendent creator. The examples—giving life and death, and the sun's rising—are merely different illustrations of the same underlying proof. Thus, there was no inconsistency or defeat.

Key Terms
دليل
مثال
إحياء وإماتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ibrahim's argument was weak because he changed topics when Nimrod countered.
Correction: Razi shows that the argument was always the same: pointing to phenomena humans cannot produce. The examples changed, but the proof remained unified.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what was the core argument that Ibrahim used against Nimrod?
A) The sun rises from the east
B) Humans cannot create life or cause the sun to rise
C) Idols are powerless
D) The moon sets

Key Takeaway
Razi teaches that a single rational proof can be illustrated by multiple examples without changing its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever dismissed a valid argument because its examples seemed inconsistent with each other? What would change if you looked for the underlying principle first?
?
Think of a belief you hold dear but struggle to defend. Where is the one proof that stays the same even when your examples run dry? Will you commit to finding and clinging to that proof?

Razi's Response to the Fourth Doubt
Razi refutes the doubt that Ibrahim was silenced.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends the consistency of Prophet Ibrahim's argument against Nimrod, showing that the proof remains the same despite changing examples.

Core Idea
One proof, many examples: Ibrahim's argument never changed.

A common doubt against Prophet Ibrahim's dialogue with Nimrod is that he was silenced when he switched from the example of life and death to the example of the sun. Imam Razi refutes this by clarifying that the underlying proof is singular and unchanging. This passage explains that the proof is the inability of humans to originate events, which points to a divine creator. The examples are merely different manifestations of the same logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Pay attention to the structure: doubt, answer, and explanation.
Notice the phrase 'الدليل كان شيئاً واحدا' — this is the key claim.
2
Identify the doubt and the response
The doubt claims Ibrahim was silenced. Razi's response states that the proof remained one and the same. Identify what the doubt is based on (the verse) and how Razi counters it.
3
Understand the proof's identity
Razi says the proof is 'حدوث ما لا يقدر الإنسان على إحداثه' (the origination of what man cannot produce). This is a single proof that indicates a powerful creator beyond creation.
This is the core theological principle: inability to originate points to a necessary creator.
4
Examine the two examples
Razi gives two examples: giving life and death, and the rising of the sun from the east. Notice that these are illustrations of the same proof, not different arguments.
Ibrahim moved from one example to another, not from one proof to another.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the transition was from example to example, while the proof itself remained one in both cases. Consider why this matters for the integrity of theological debate.
This refutes the claim that Ibrahim was silenced and shows the consistency of his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة: قوله تعالى : فَإِنَّ اللهَ يَأْتِي بِالشَّمْسِ مِنَ الْمَشْرِقِ فَأْتِ بِهَا مِنَ الْمَغْرِب [البقرة : ٢٥٨] وهذا يدل على أنه صار منقطعًا في الدليل الأول. والجواب : فيه أبحاث لطيفة مذكورة في كتاب أسرار التنزيل، والذي نقوله ههنا : إن الدليل كان شيئًا واحدا وهو حدوث ما لا يقدر الإنسان على إحداثه، فهو يدل على قادر أخر غير الخلق ثم هذا المعنى له أمثلة : أحدها : الإحياء والإماتة. الثاني : طلوع الشمس من مشرقها، وهذا كان انتقالا مثال إلى مثال، أما من الدليل فشيء واحد في الحالين.
The fourth doubt: His saying: 'Indeed Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west' [Al-Baqarah 258]. This indicates that he (Ibrahim) was silenced in the first argument. The answer: there are subtle investigations mentioned in the book 'Asrar al-Tanzil'. What we say here is: the proof was one and the same, namely, the origination of what man cannot produce, and it indicates another powerful being besides the creation. Then this meaning has examples: first: giving life and causing death. Second: the rising of the sun from its east. This was a transition from one example to another; as for the proof, it is one thing in both cases.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer. The doubt claims that when Nimrod said 'I too give life and death' and then Ibrahim switched to the sun argument, Ibrahim was defeated. Razi answers that the proof was one: the inability of humans to originate events demonstrates a powerful creator. The examples of life/death and the sun's rising are just different illustrations of the same logical proof. Thus Ibrahim was not silenced; he simply gave another example.

Misconception: Some think Ibrahim changed his argument because he was defeated by Nimrod's initial response.
Correction: Razi shows that Ibrahim did not change his argument; the proof remained the same (origination of what man cannot produce), only the example changed.

Key Takeaway
The proof in Ibrahim's argument remained consistent: the inability of creation to originate events points to a Creator beyond creation.

Examine your heart: do you truly live as if every event—from the rising of the sun to your own breath—originates from Allah alone? Where do you subtly attribute power to yourself or others? What would change if you fully internalized this proof?
