First Proof: Gratitude Precludes Reward Entitlement
Imam Razi presents the first proof that obedience does not entitle reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's first proof that obedience does not create an entitlement to reward, based on the principle that gratitude for past favors is already obligatory.

Core Idea
Past favors obligate gratitude; fulfilling a duty cannot create a new right.

Imam Razi begins his discussion of reward and punishment by addressing a fundamental disagreement with the Mu'tazila: does obedience to God give the servant a right to reward? He presents the first proof, which draws on the overwhelming nature of God's past favors. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the opposing view
Read both the Arabic and translation. Notice that Razi first states the Mu'tazila claim that obedience is a cause for deserving reward, then states his school's position that no one has a right against God.
This sets up the debate.
2
Understand the key premise about God's favors
Razi argues that God's favors are countless, as the Quran says. These favors obligate the servant to be grateful and obedient.
This is the foundation.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
Since the servant is already obligated to obey because of past favors, performing that obedience cannot be a cause for deserving something extra. Obligation fulfilled does not create entitlement.
This undermines the Mu'tazila claim.
4
Reflect on the implication
This proof does not deny that God may reward—it only denies that the servant can claim it as a right. Reward remains a pure gift.
Important nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون وفيها فصول : في أحكام الثواب والعقاب الفصل الأول : في حكم الثواب والعقاب اعلم أن المكلف إما أن يكون مطيعا أو عاصيًا، فإن كان مطيعا فالله تعالى يثيبه، وزعم البصريون من المعتزلة أن أداء الطاعة علة لاستحقاق الثواب على الله تعالى. ومذهبنا أنه ليس لأحد على الله تعالى حق لنا وجوه : · الحجة الأولى : أن الإنعام يوجب على المنعم عليه الاشتغال بالشكر والخدمة، ونعم الله تعالى على العبد في الماضي وفي الحاضر كثيرة خارجة عن الحصر والإحصاء، كما قال تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَتَ اللَّهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا [إبراهيم: ٣٤] وإذا كان كذلك فتلك النعم السالفة توجب على العبد الاشتغال بالطاعة وبالشكر، وأداء الواجب لا يكون سببًا لاستحقاق شيء آخر، فوجب أن لا يكون اشتغال العبد بالطاعة على لاستحقاق الثواب على الله تعالى.
The thirty-fifth question, and it contains chapters: on the rulings of reward and punishment. The first chapter: on the ruling of reward and punishment. Know that the morally responsible person is either obedient or disobedient. If he is obedient, then God Most High rewards him. The Basrans among the Mu'tazila claimed that performing obedience is a cause for deserving reward from God Most High. Our school is that no one has a right against God Most High. We have proofs: The first proof: that bestowal of favor obliges the one favored to engage in gratitude and service. The favors of God Most High upon the servant in the past and present are many, beyond enumeration and counting, as He said: 'And if you count the favors of Allah, you cannot enumerate them' [Ibrahim: 34]. If that is so, then those past favors oblige the servant to engage in obedience and gratitude, and performing an obligation cannot be a cause for deserving anything else. So it is necessary that the servant's engagement in obedience is not [a cause] for deserving reward from God Most High.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage begins the thirty-fifth question on reward and punishment. It first defines the two categories of the morally responsible person (mukallaf): obedient or disobedient. For the obedient, God rewards him. The author then states the opposing view of the Basran Mu'tazila that obedient acts are a cause for God to be obligated to reward. The author's position is that no one has a right against God. The first proof is presented: God's countless past favors oblige the servant to gratitude and obedience. Since the servant is already obligated to obey, that obedience cannot create a new entitlement to reward; one cannot claim reward for merely fulfilling a pre-existing duty. The proof relies on the principle that performing an obligatory act does not generate a claim for additional compensation.

استحقاق
علة

شكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that this proof denies any reward for obedience entirely.
Correction: The proof only denies that reward is a necessary entitlement; God may still grant reward as a free gift.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does obedience not entitle reward?
A) Because God is not obligated to anyone
B) Because past favors already obligate obedience, so no new right arises
C) Because obedience is impossible without God's grace
D) Because reward is only for the disbelievers

Key Takeaway
God's countless past favors obligate obedience, so obedience cannot be the basis for demanding reward; reward remains a divine gift.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you bow in prayer, do you feel as though you are doing God a favor, or as though you are merely beginning to repay an infinite debt? How does that feeling change your posture of humility? Starting today, consciously remind yourself before each prayer that you are not earning credit but responding to grace. Will you do it?
?
If you truly believed that God owes you absolutely nothing, would you still worship Him with the same fervor? Examine your heart: is there any part of your worship that is motivated by a sense of entitlement or expectation of reward? What will you do today to purify your intention so that you worship solely out of gratitude?
?
Consider the countless favors God has given you this past hour: breath, health, sight, thought. Now, if you were to spend the rest of your life in continuous worship, would that even begin to repay one favor? How does this realization strip away any pride you might have in your obedience? Will you ask God to accept your meager efforts purely out of His generosity, not because you deserve it?

Why Works Cannot Cause Reward
Razi presents a dilemma against works as cause of reward, then replies to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why making works a cause for reward leads to absurdities about divine freedom and perfection, and how an objection based on divine foreknowledge is resolved.

Core Idea
If works caused reward, God would be either coerced or deficient—both impossible.

This lesson presents Imam al-Razi's second proof against the Mu'tazilite claim that obedient works necessitate reward. The proof is a powerful dilemma showing that such a view forces us into one of two impossible conclusions: either God loses His free agency or He becomes subject to deficiency. We will also see how Razi defends divine free will against the objection that God's foreknowledge would necessitate His actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then study the translation. Note that the argument has three parts: the main dilemma, an objection, and the reply.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The proof begins with a conditional: if works cause reward, then either God cannot withhold reward (impossible) or He can withhold it. Trace the consequences of each horn.
First horn leads to God being a necessitating cause, not a free agent. Second horn leads to deficiency or no true obligation.
3
Understand the objection from knowledge
The opponent argues that if God knows something will occur, then His knowledge necessitates it, which would make Him a necessitating cause. See how this is similar to the first horn.
This objection aims to show that God's knowledge itself implies coercion.
4
Examine Razi's reply
Razi distinguishes God's knowledge from created causality: knowledge follows the occurrence (which follows intention), so it does not coerce. The servant's act, however, is independent of God's will according to the opponent, which would make the servant coerce God—absurd.
This reply preserves divine freedom while answering the objection.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that works cannot be a cause for reward. Consider the broader implication: God's actions are free and not compelled by created acts.
This aligns with the Sunni view of divine free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : لو كان العمل علة لوجوب الثواب لكان إما أن يمتنع من أن لا يثيب أو يصح فإن امتنع أن لا يثيب فحينئذ يكون الصانع علة موجبة لذلك الثواب لا فاعلا مختاراً، وإن صح فبتقدير أن لا يثيب إن لم يصر مستحقا للذم لم يتحقق معنى الوجوب، وإن صار مستحقا للذم لزم أن يكون ناقصا لذاته مستكملا بسبب ذلك الفعل الذي يفعله، وذلك محال. فإن قيل : إنه تعالى إذا علم وجود شيء فإن لم يصح منه تركه كان موجبا بالذات، وإن صح لزم أن يكون قادرًا على تجهيل نفسه، وهو محال. قلنا : قد ذكرنا أن علمه بالوقوع تبع للوقوع - الذي هو تبع للقصد إلى الإيقاع - والتابع لا يكون منافيًا للمتبوع، بخلاف فعل العبد فإنه عند الخصم واقع بقدرة العبد وإرادته، فلو صار ذلك الفعل سببا لأن يجب على الله تعالى فعل الإثابة لزم أن يكون العبد قد ألجأ الله تعالى بذلك الفعل إلى إيجاب الثواب الجاء لا يمكن من تركه وهذا محال.
The second proof: If the deed were a cause for the obligation of reward, then either it is impossible that He not reward or possible. If it is impossible that He not reward, then the Maker would be an efficient cause necessitating that reward, not a free agent. If it is possible, then in the case that He does not reward, if He does not become deserving of blame, the meaning of obligation is not realized, and if He becomes deserving of blame, then it follows that He is deficient in Himself, completing Himself by means of that deed which He does, and that is impossible. If it is said: He, when He knows the existence of a thing, if it is not possible for Him to leave it, then He is necessitating by essence, and if it is possible, then it follows that He is able to make Himself ignorant, which is impossible. We say: We have mentioned that His knowledge of occurrence is dependent on occurrence—which is dependent on the intention to bring about—and the dependent is not in conflict with the independent, unlike the deed of the servant, for it is, according to the opponent, actualized by the servant's power and will. So if that deed becomes a cause for God to be obliged to perform the act of rewarding, it would follow that the servant has compelled God by that deed to obligate reward, a compulsion such that He cannot abandon it, and this is impossible.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof (hujjah) against the Mu'tazilite claim that obedient works necessitate reward. The proof is a dilemma. If works were a cause for reward, either God cannot withhold reward (impossible) or He can withhold but then faces a problem. If withholding is impossible, God becomes a necessitating cause rather than a free agent, contradicting His free will. If withholding is possible, either God deserves no blame (negating obligation) or He deserves blame for not rewarding—but blame implies deficiency, which is impossible for God. The proof then addresses an objection: if God knows something will occur, His knowledge necessitates it, making Him necessitated. The reply (jawab) distinguishes God's knowledge from created causality: God's knowledge follows from the occurrence, not vice versa, so it does not negate free will. If works of the servant compelled God's rewarding, the servant would coerce God, which is absurd. Thus the proof concludes that works cannot be a cause for obligatory reward.

علة
وجوب
فاعل مختار
موجب بالذات
نقص
علم
الجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows something will happen, then it must happen, so God has no free will regarding it.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that God's knowledge follows from His intention to act; it does not compel Him. The objection confuses knowledge with causation.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof's main dilemma regarding reward?
A) Either God is unjust or the servant is innocent
B) Either God is compelled or deficient—both impossible
C) Either reward is eternal or temporary
D) Either the deed is good or evil

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that viewing works as a cause for reward inevitably leads to either compromising divine freedom or attributing deficiency to God, both of which are impossible—and the objection regarding divine foreknowledge is refuted by distinguishing knowledge from compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your good deeds 'deserve' reward from God, or do you see them as mere gifts from Him? How would this shift in perspective change the way you perform acts of worship?
?
If you could compel God to reward you by your actions, would you want that power? What does the impossibility of compulsion say about the nature of your relationship with your Creator?
?
This proof shows that attributing obligation to God based on human action leads to absurdity. Have you ever thought your righteousness puts God in your debt? What needs to change in your heart about how you view His rewards?

Human Acts Do Not Obligate God: Third Proof
Razi presents a causal proof and answers the scriptural objection about recompense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's causal argument that human obedience cannot obligate God to reward, and how he reinterprets Qur'anic 'recompense' verses as signs rather than causes.

Core Idea
Human acts are effects of God's creation; they cannot obligate Him.

This passage contains one of Imam Razi's proofs for divine freedom. He argues that since human acts are necessitated by the motive and power created by God, those acts cannot impose any obligation on the Creator. He then addresses a scriptural objection by explaining that verses mentioning 'recompense' use the term loosely; deeds are merely signs of reward, not causes that necessitate it.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: the proof builds on a prior discussion about human acts depending on motive and power, and how their combination necessitates the act.
Razi references a previous discussion—this is part of a larger system.
2
Trace the causal chain
The proof says: the occurrence of obedience from the servant is a necessitator (mujib) for God's act and an effect (ma'lul) of Him. Understand why this means the act cannot obligate God—an effect does not cause its cause to be obligated.
3
Consider the opponent's doubt
The opponent raises Qur'anic verses that use the word 'recompense' (jaza'). Identify the verse cited and the apparent problem it creates for Razi's argument.
The verse is from Surah al-Sajdah: 17.
4
Study the response
Razi's answer is that the deed is a sign (alamah) of reward, not a necessitating cause (illah mujibah). Reflect on why this distinction allows the term 'recompense' to be used without implying obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أنا قد ذكرنا في مسألة الأفعال أن صدور الفعل من العبد يتوقف على الداعي، وبينا أن مجموع القدرة والداعي يوجب الفعل، وإذا كان كذلكم كان حصول الطاعة موجبًا لفعل الله تعالى ومعلولاً له، وإذا كان كذلك كان حصول الطاعة الله تعالى بفعل الفاعل لا يوجب عليه ثوابًا، فوجب أن تكون طاعات العبد لا توجب الثواب على الله تعالى. من شبهة الخصم قوله تعالى : الجَزَاء بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ [السجدة: ١٧] وأمثاله من النصوص. والجواب : أن العمل عندنا علامة حصول الثواب لا أنه علة موجبة له، وهذا القدر يكفي في إطلاق اسم الجزاء على الثواب.
The third proof: We have mentioned in the question of acts that the occurrence of an act from the servant depends on the motive, and we have explained that the combination of power and the motive necessitates the act. If that is so, then the occurrence of obedience is a necessitator for God's act and an effect of Him. If that is so, then the occurrence of obedience from the act of the agent does not obligate Him to reward. So it is necessary that the acts of obedience of the servant do not obligate reward upon God Most High. Part of the opponent's doubt is His statement: 'The recompense for what they used to do' [al-Sajdah: 17] and similar texts. The answer: The deed with us is a sign of the occurrence of reward, not a necessitating cause for it, and this suffices for the application of the name 'recompense' to reward.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof and then answers a scriptural objection. The proof builds on a prior discussion of human acts: an act occurs when power and motive combine, and that combination necessitates the act. Since the servant's obedience is itself an effect of God's creation (as the agent's act is ultimately from God), that obedience cannot be a cause for God to be obligated; an effect does not obligate its cause. The opponent raises a doubt: Qur'anic verses like 'recompense for what they used to do' seem to imply that deeds cause reward. The answer is that the term 'recompense' (jaza') is used loosely; deeds are merely a sign (alamah) that reward will be given, not a cause (illah) that necessitates it. This suffices for the linguistic usage without implying obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that Qur'anic verses about 'recompense' prove that human deeds necessarily cause reward from God, implying an obligation on God.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that the word 'recompense' (jaza') is used because the deed is a sign (alamah) of reward, not a cause (illah) that necessitates it. God rewards freely, not out of necessity.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi interpret the Qur'anic use of 'recompense' (jaza')?

B) It is metaphorical and not to be taken literally
C) The deed is a sign of reward, not its cause
D) It only applies to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
Human acts do not obligate God; they are merely signs through which He freely bestows reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you secretly feel that God should answer you because you performed the prayer? How does this lesson's truth—that your act does not obligate God—expose the hidden transaction in your heart? Will you now pray as a pure gift, not a demand?
?
If your very best deeds are merely signs, not causes, of God's reward, what does that do to your sense of spiritual security? Does it humiliate you to realize you have no leverage with God? Can you accept that your only hope is His sheer grace, not your obedience?
?
What would change in your worship if you truly believed that God owes you nothing, even after a lifetime of obedience? Would you still strive to obey? Answer honestly: does that thought release you from pride, or does it feel unfair?

Fourth Proof from Quran 4:48 – First Reason
Razi presents Quran 4:48 and gives the first reason for its interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 4:48 to argue that major sins can be forgiven before repentance, based on the verse's structure of forgiveness as a favor (tafaddul).

Core Idea
Forgiveness of minor sins is obligatory; the verse speaks of favor, so it must refer to unrepentant major sinners.

Razi now turns to a direct Qur'anic proof. The verse 'Allah does not forgive shirk but forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' seems to speak of forgiveness as a favor. Since the Mu'tazila hold that forgiveness of minor sins is obligatory, the verse cannot apply to them; it must apply to the unrepentant major sinner. This lesson walks through Razi's careful reasoning and the analogy he uses to clarify the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the key terms 'tafaddul' (favor) and 'istihqaq' (right/obligation).
2
Understand the argument's structure
Razi argues that the forgiveness in the verse must be a favor, not an obligation. He uses an analogy about bestowing money to illustrate consistency.
Focus on the contrast between 'favor' and 'right'.
3
Identify why minor sinners and repentant major sinners are excluded
According to the Mu'tazila, forgiveness for these categories is obligatory (istihqaq), not a favor. Therefore the verse cannot refer to them.
The verse would be inconsistent if it referred to obligatory forgiveness.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the verse must apply to the major sinner before repentance, because only then is forgiveness a favor (unobligated).
This supports the Ash'ari position on forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قوله تعالى : إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ به ويغفر ما دون ذالك لمن يَشَاءُ [النساء : ٤٨] ووجه الاستدلال به أن قوله تعالى : ولمن يشاء الله لا يجوز أن يتناول صاحب الصغيرة ولا صاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة فوجب أن يكون المراد منه حمله على صاحب الكبيرة قبل التوبة. إنما قلنا : لا يجوز حمله على صاحب الصغيرة ولا على صاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة لوجوه : أحدها: أن قوله تعالى : إنّ اللهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بها [النساء : ٤٨] معناه أنه تعالى لا يغفره تفضلا، لأنَّا أجمعنا على أنه يغفر استحقاقا ، فإنه إذا تاب عن كفره وآمن فإنه تعالى يغفر له وإذا كان قوله : وإنّ اللهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ ﴾ [النساء: ٤٨] معناه أنه تعالى لا يغفر له تفضلا لزم أن يكون قوله : هو وتغفر ما دون ذالك لمن يَشَاءُ [النساء : ٤٨] أي وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاء الله تفضلا، حتى يكون النفي والإثبات متوجهين إلى شيء واحد، ألا ترى أنه إذا قيل : فلان يتفضل بمائة دينار ويعطي العشرة لمن استحقها كان الكلام فاسدًا، أما لو قال : لا يتفضل بالمائة ولكنه يتفضل بالعشرة، كان منتظماً، ولما كان غفران صاحب الصغيرة وصاحب الكبيرة بعد التوبة واجبًا امتنع حمل الآية عليهما، فتعين حملها على صاحب الكبيرة قبل التوبة.
The fourth proof: His saying, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [An-Nisa 4:48]. And the way of using it as evidence is that His saying, Exalted is He: 'for whom He wills' cannot include the minor sinner nor the major sinner after repentance, so it must be that the intended meaning is to apply it to the major sinner before repentance. We say: It is not permissible to apply it to the minor sinner nor to the major sinner after repentance for several reasons. First: that His saying, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' means that He, Exalted is He, does not forgive it as a favor, because we are agreed that He forgives it as a right. For indeed, if one repents from his disbelief and believes, then He, Exalted is He, forgives him. And since His saying 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' means He does not forgive it as a favor, it follows that His saying 'and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' means 'and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' as a favor, so that the negation and affirmation are directed to one thing. Do you not see that if it is said: 'So-and-so bestows a hundred dinars as a favor and gives ten to the one who deserves it,' the statement is corrupt? But if he said: 'He does not bestow a hundred as a favor, but he bestows ten as a favor,' it would be consistent. And since forgiveness of the minor sinner and the major sinner after repentance is obligatory, it is impossible to apply the verse to them, so its application to the major sinner before repentance is determined.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof from Quran 4:48 and its first reason. The author argues that the verse 'Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' must refer to the major sinner before repentance. The key point is that the structure of the verse requires the forgiveness of what is less than shirk to be a favor (tafaddul), not an obligation. Since forgiveness of minor sins and of major sins after repentance is obligatory according to the opponent (Mu'tazila), the verse cannot apply to them; it must apply to the major sinner before repentance. The author uses an analogy: if someone says 'he bestows a hundred as a favor but gives ten to the one who deserves it,' the statement is inconsistent; but if he says 'he does not bestow a hundred as a favor, but he bestows ten as a favor,' it is consistent. Thus the negation and affirmation must be on the same footing—both as favor. Since the forgiveness of shirk is denied as favor (because it is forgiven as right after repentance), the forgiveness of what is less than shirk must be affirmed as favor. Hence it applies to those for whom forgiveness is not obligatory, i.e., the unrepentant major sinner.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة
صاحب الكبيرة
صاحب الصغيرة
التفضُّل
الاستحقاق
المشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse unconditionally promises forgiveness for all sins except shirk, implying that all believers will eventually be forgiven without repentance.
Correction: Razi shows that the forgiveness is conditional on 'whom He wills' and that it is a favor, not an obligation. It does not cover minor sins or repentant major sinners because their forgiveness is obligatory. Therefore, it applies to unrepentant major sinners, whose forgiveness is purely at Allah's discretion.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first reason, why does the forgiveness in Quran 4:48 not apply to the minor sinner or the repentant major sinner?
A) Because their forgiveness is a favor, not a right
B) Because their forgiveness is obligatory (a right) not a favor
C) Because the verse only speaks about shirk
D) Because they are not included in 'whom He wills'

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the structure of Quran 4:48 to argue that the forgiveness it promises for lesser sins is a favor, not an obligation, so it must apply to the unrepentant major sinner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat some of your sins as 'small' and assume Allah will forgive them without you needing to repent? How does Razi’s reasoning challenge this assumption, and what does that reveal about your own attitude towards divine mercy?
?
Have you ever expected Allah to forgive you as a 'right' because of your good deeds or repentance? How does the concept of forgiveness as a favor (tafaddul) affect your sense of humility and gratitude?
?
The verse speaks of Allah forgiving 'for whom He wills'. Does this make you feel uncertain or hopeful? How does this lesson shape your understanding of God's absolute sovereignty over forgiveness?

First Proof from Quran 24:55 for Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Razi presents the first proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate using Quran 24:55.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quran 24:55 to argue for the divine approval of the first four caliphs, especially Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
Allah's promise of succession in the Quran was fulfilled by the four caliphs.

Imam Razi now shifts to presenting his own proofs. The first proof is a powerful argument from the Quran itself. By analyzing the language of a verse from Surah al-Nur, he shows that the caliphate of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali is not merely a historical accident but a direct fulfillment of a divine promise. Let's examine this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi introduces this proof after presenting the opponents' arguments.
The passage begins with a transitional statement.
2
Identify the key components of the proof
Break down the argument into its premises: the verse, the linguistic point about the plural, the principle that Allah always fulfills His promises, and the historical fact of the four caliphs.
Each component builds on the previous.
3
Understand the linguistic argument
Razi notes that 'alladhina amanu' is a plural form, which in Arabic for rational beings has a minimum of three. This links the verse to at least three caliphs.
This is a crucial step in the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the verse promises succession to at least three, and the actual succession was the four caliphs, their caliphate is the fulfillment of the promise and thus divinely approved.
This establishes the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate as part of that group.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب: اعلم أنا لما أوردنا أكثر ما يتمسك القوم به في مذهبهم فلنذكر أيضاً بعض ما نتمسك به في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر ثم ترجع إلى الجواب عن شبهاتهم، فنقول: في المسألة وجوه أخر من الدلائل سوى ما ذكرناه: لنا الحجة الأولى التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَدَ اللهُ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مِنكُمْ وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ لَيَسْتَخْلِفَنَّهُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ كَمَا اسْتَخْلَفَ الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ﴾ [النور: 55] فقوله: ﴿الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مِنكُمْ وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ﴾ صيغة جمع أقلها ثلاثة، فقد وعد الثلاثة فما فوقها من أصحاب محمد أن يستخلفهم في الأرض ويمكنهم من دينهم الذي ارتضى لهم، وكل ما وعد الله به فقد فعله، ولم يوجد إلا خلافة الخلفاء الأربعة، فوجب القطع بأنها هي التي وعد الله به في هذه الآية، وأثنى عليها وعظمها، وهذا يوجب القطع بصحة خلافة هؤلاء الأربعة.
And the answer: Know that when we have presented most of what the people use to support their school, we will also mention some of what we use to support the imamate of Abu Bakr, then we will return to answer their objections. So we say: In this question there are other proofs besides what we have mentioned: For us the first proof is the adherence to His statement: 'Allah has promised those who have believed among you and done righteous deeds that He will surely grant them succession [to authority] upon the earth just as He granted it to those before them' [al-Nur 24:55]. His statement: 'those who have believed among you and done righteous deeds' is a plural form whose minimum is three. So He promised the three and above from the companions of Muhammad that He will make them successors on earth and establish for them their religion which He has approved for them. And everything Allah has promised, He has done. And no succession has occurred except the caliphate of the four caliphs. So it must be decisively that it is what Allah promised in this verse, and He praised it and magnified it. And this necessitates the definitive judgment of the validity of the caliphate of these four.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage begins with a transitional statement: Razi announces that after presenting the opponents' arguments, he will now present some proofs for the imamate of Abu Bakr and then return to answer their objections. Then he presents the first proof from Quran 24:55. The verse promises that Allah will grant succession (istikhlaf) to those who believe and do righteous deeds. Razi notes that the Arabic plural form 'alladhina amanu' has a minimum of three. Thus the verse promises to at least three of the Prophet's companions that they will be made successors. Since Allah always fulfills His promises, the actual succession that occurred—the caliphate of the four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali)—must be the fulfillment. Therefore, the caliphate of these four is divinely approved and valid. This proof establishes the collective imamate of the first four, including Abu Bakr.

Key Terms
الاستخلاف
الصيغة
الجمع
الوعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse refers only to later caliphs or to the end of time.
Correction: Razi argues the verse applies to the immediate successors of the Prophet, as the fulfillment occurred shortly after his death.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say the verse from Surah al-Nur implies at least three caliphs?
A) Because the word 'خلفاء' is plural.
B) Because the plural form 'الذين آمنوا' indicates a minimum of three.
C) Because there were exactly three caliphs.
D) Because the verse singles out Abu Bakr by name.

Key Takeaway
The verse in Surah al-Nur promises succession to at least three believers, which was fulfilled by the caliphates of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah promised succession to those who believe and do righteous deeds. When you look at your own life, do you see the marks of that promise being fulfilled—or do you live as if you have no stake in that divine guarantee? What would need to change today for you to truly qualify?
?
Razi argued that the plural form implies at least three. Are you actively building a community of believers with whom you strive for righteousness, or are you content to walk alone? What is holding you back from seeking or forming that group?
?
If Allah's promise was fulfilled so precisely in the caliphs, how confident are you that His promises to you—like guidance, provision, or victory—will also be fulfilled? Does your daily life reflect that certainty, or have you let doubt creep in?

Second Major Premise: Necessity and Contingency
Al-Razi sets up the necessity-contingency premise and its first implication for God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second major premise of kalam—the division of existence into necessary and contingent—and how it leads to the first necessary consequence: that the Necessary Being is not a body and not in a direction.

Core Idea
Every contingent points to a Necessary Being who is utterly simple.

This lesson introduces one of the most honored premises in Islamic theology: the premise of necessity and contingency. Al-Razi presents it as the ultimate aim of rational thinkers, and then draws its first profound implication for understanding God's nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the elevated language Al-Razi uses to describe this premise.
2
Identify the logical structure
Trace the argument: existence is divided into necessary and contingent; contingents need a necessitator; that necessitator must be necessary in itself and in attributes.
Contrast this with the possibility of an infinite regress.
3
Focus on the first necessary consequence
Al-Razi states that the Necessary Being is transcendent from multiplicity. This implies it is not a body (since bodies are divisible) and not in a direction.
This is the first of two lazims; the second is not included in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي مقدمة الوجوب والإمكان - فهذه المقدمة في غاية الشرف والعلو، وهي غاية عقول العقلاء، قالوا : الموجود إما واجب أو ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من موجب وذلك الموجب لا بد وأن يكون واجبا في ذاته وفي صفاته، إذ لو كان ممكنا لافتقر إلى مؤثر آخر. أمران :
أما المقدمة الأولى وهي أنه واجب لذاته فهذا له لازمان:
الأول: أن يكون منزها في حقيقته عن الكثرة، ثم يلزم من فردانيته في ذاته أحدهما أن لا يكون متحيزا ؛ لأن كل متحيز ،منقسم والمنقسم لا يكون فردًا، ولما لم يكن متحيزا، لم يكن في جهة.
As for the second premise—and it is the premise of necessity and contingency—this premise is of the utmost honor and elevation, and it is the ultimate aim of the intellects of the rational. They said: 'Existence is either necessary or contingent. The contingent necessarily requires a necessitator, and that necessitator must be necessary in its essence and attributes, because if it were contingent, it would depend on another effect.' Two matters: As for the first premise, that it is necessary in itself, this has two necessary consequences: The first: that it be transcendent in its essence from multiplicity. Then, from its individuality in its essence follows one of them [i.e., one of the two consequences], which is that it is not a body, because every body is divisible, and the divisible is not an individual; and since it is not a body, it is not in a direction.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major premise, which is the premise of necessity (wujub) and contingency (imkan). It is highly honored and is the goal of rational thinkers. The premise states that existence is divided into the necessary (wajib) and the contingent (mumkin). Every contingent requires a necessitator (mujib), and that necessitator must be necessary in itself and in its attributes, otherwise it would itself be contingent and require another effect. Then the author mentions two matters: first, that the necessary being has two necessary consequences (lazims). The first consequence is that the necessary being is transcendent (munazzah) in its reality from multiplicity (kathra). From its individuality (fardaniyya) follows one of the two consequences, namely that it is not a body (mutahayyiz), because every body is divisible, and the divisible is not an individual. Since it is not a body, it is not in a direction (jiha). The passage ends mid-explanation of the second lazims, cut off by the page boundary.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الإمكان
الواجب
الممكن
الموجب
الفردانية
التحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the premise of necessity and contingency is purely logical and has no implications for God's nature.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that this premise directly implies God's transcendence from multiplicity and corporeality, thus shaping our understanding of God's attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the premise of necessity and contingency, why must the necessitator of a contingent be necessary in itself?
A) Because otherwise it would be contingent and require another effect
B) Because it is eternal
C) Because it is one
D) Because it is not a body

Key Takeaway
The premise that every contingent requires a necessary cause leads to the conclusion that the Necessary Being is not a body and not in a direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Everything you rely on—your health, your loved ones, your own existence—is contingent and could vanish in an instant. Does this truth make you cling to them more tightly, or drive you to anchor your heart in the only Necessary Being? What does your actual daily worry reveal about where you truly place your trust?
?
Al-Razi deduces that the Necessary Being cannot be a body or be in any direction. Have you ever unconsciously pictured God as being 'up there' or having a form? How does this argument challenge that mental image, and what does it mean for your worship to realize that God cannot be contained by space?
?
This premise is called 'the ultimate aim of the intellects of the rational.' How much of your thinking time is devoted to knowing God in this deep, rational way? If you were to seriously pursue this aim, what distraction would you need to let go of today?

The Division of Existence: Necessary and Possible
Razi classifies existence and argues for a necessary cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental division of existence into necessary and possible and the logical requirement that the possible needs a necessary cause.

Core Idea
Every possible being points to a necessary being.

Razi begins by stating the foundational premise of kalam: all existence is either necessary or possible. The possible cannot exist without a necessitator, which must itself be necessary. This sets the stage for proving God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the translation. Notice the crisp logical flow.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the division of existence
The passage divides existence into two categories: necessary and possible. Understand what each means.
Necessary: cannot not exist. Possible: can exist or not exist.
3
Trace the causal argument
The possible needs a necessitator (mu’aththir). That necessitator cannot be possible itself, otherwise it would need another, leading to an infinite regress. So it must be necessary.
This is a classic kalam proof for a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي مقدمة الوجوب والإمكان - فهذه المقدمة في غاية الشرف والعلو، وهي غاية عقول العقلاء، قالوا : الموجود إما واجب أو ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من موجب وذلك الموجب لا بد وأن يكون واجبا في ذاته وفي صفاته، إذ لو كان ممكنا لافتقر إلى مؤثر آخر. أمران : أما المقدمة الأولى وهي أنه واجب لذاته فهذا له لازمان: الأول: أن يكون منزها في حقيقته عن الكثرة، ثم يلزم من فردانيته في ذاته أحدهما أن لا يكون متحيزا ؛ لأن كل متحيز منقسم والمنقسم لا يكون فردًا، ولما لم يكن متحيزا، لم يكن في جهة. وثانيهما : أن لا يكون واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، إذ لو كان أكثر من واحد لاشتركا في الوجوب وبيانا في التعين وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما في نفسه مركبًا، وقد فرضناه فردا، هذا خلف. الثاني : كون واجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون حالا ولا مجلاً، وإلا لعاد الافتقار.
As for the second premise—which is the premise of necessity and possibility—this premise is of utmost honor and highness, and it is the ultimate goal of the intellects of the rational. They said: existence is either necessary or possible, and the possible must have a necessitator, and that necessitator must be necessary in essence and in attributes, for if it were possible, it would need another efficient cause. Two matters: As for the first premise—that He is necessary in essence—this has two necessary consequences: First: that He is transcendent in His essence from multiplicity. Then from His individuality in His essence follows one of them: that He is not a body, because every body is divisible, and what is divisible is not individual. And since He is not a body, He is not in a direction. And the second of them: that there is not more than one necessary of existence, for if there were more than one, they would share in necessity and differ in identity, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so each one of them would be composite, and we have assumed it to be individual—this is a contradiction. The second: that the necessary of existence in essence is neither a substrate nor an indweller, otherwise dependence would return.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage introduces the second premise in the overall discussion, which is the premise of necessity and possibility (muqaddimat al-wujub wa al-imkaan). It is described as the ultimate goal of the rational. It states the fundamental division of existence into necessary and possible, and that the possible needs a necessitator which must be necessary in essence and attributes, otherwise it would regress. Then it says there are two matters (amran). The first matter is the premise that God is necessary in essence, and this premise has two necessary consequences (laziman). The first consequence is that God is transcendent (munazzah) from multiplicity. This leads to two further points: first, that He is not a body, because bodies are divisible and thus not individual; second, that He is not in a direction. The second consequence is that there cannot be more than one necessary being, because two would share necessity and differ in identity, making each composite, which contradicts individuality. Then the second matter (the second of the two matters) is that the necessary being cannot be a substrate (mahall) or an indweller (hulul), because that would entail dependence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكن
موجب
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think possible things can exist without a cause, or that the causal chain can go on infinitely.
Correction: Razi states clearly that the possible must have a necessitator, and that necessitator must be necessary, otherwise the chain would not terminate.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why must the necessitator of the possible be necessary in essence?
A) Because it is the first cause.
B) Because if it were possible, it would need another cause.
C) Because possible things cannot cause other things.
D) Because the universe needs a creator.

Key Takeaway
The possible always points to a necessary being, which is the foundation of proving God's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything possible must have a cause, what is the most important possible thing in your life that you are taking for granted, as if it were self-sufficient? Will you today recognize its dependence on Allah?
?
Razi says this premise is the ultimate goal of rational minds. Does your mind stop at the possible—the created world—or does it reach the Necessary Being? When you see the sun, the sky, your own heartbeat, do you immediately see the pointer (dalil) to the Necessary, or do you get lost in the beauty of the possible and forget the Giver?
?
If the necessary being cannot be caused, then your very existence and every moment you breathe is a gift from Him, not from your own power. In what area of your life are you living as if you are the source of your own success or security? How will you shift your trust to the Necessary Being today?

The Two Consequences of God's Necessary Existence
Razi enumerates two consequences of God being necessary in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two necessary consequences of God being necessary in essence: His transcendence from multiplicity and the impossibility of multiple necessary beings.

Core Idea
If God is necessary, He must be one and indivisible.

Having established that God is necessary in essence, Razi draws two immediate implications: first, God is not a body and not in a direction; second, there cannot be more than one necessary being. These conclusions follow from the simplicity of necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two consequences
The passage states two matters (amran) that follow from God being necessary in essence. Find them: first, transcendence from multiplicity; second, impossibility of more than one necessary being.
The second matter (not being a substrate/indweller) is also mentioned but can be seen as a third point.
2
Analyze the first consequence
God is transcendent from multiplicity, so He is not a body (since bodies are divisible) and not in a direction.
Divisibility implies composition, which contradicts individuality.
3
Analyze the second consequence
There cannot be two necessary beings because they would share necessity but differ in identity, making each composite, which contradicts the simplicity of the necessary being.
This is a classic tawhid argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية - وهي مقدمة الوجوب والإمكان - فهذه المقدمة في غاية الشرف والعلو، وهي غاية عقول العقلاء، قالوا : الموجود إما واجب أو ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من موجب وذلك الموجب لا بد وأن يكون واجبا في ذاته وفي صفاته، إذ لو كان ممكنا لافتقر إلى مؤثر آخر. أمران : أما المقدمة الأولى وهي أنه واجب لذاته فهذا له لازمان: الأول: أن يكون منزها في حقيقته عن الكثرة، ثم يلزم من فردانيته في ذاته أحدهما أن لا يكون متحيزا ؛ لأن كل متحيز منقسم والمنقسم لا يكون فردًا، ولما لم يكن متحيزا، لم يكن في جهة. وثانيهما : أن لا يكون واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، إذ لو كان أكثر من واحد لاشتركا في الوجوب وبيانا في التعين وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما في نفسه مركبًا، وقد فرضناه فردا، هذا خلف. الثاني : كون واجب الوجود لذاته لا يكون حالا ولا مجلاً، وإلا لعاد الافتقار.
As for the second premise—which is the premise of necessity and possibility—this premise is of utmost honor and highness, and it is the ultimate goal of the intellects of the rational. They said: existence is either necessary or possible, and the possible must have a necessitator, and that necessitator must be necessary in essence and in attributes, for if it were possible, it would need another efficient cause. Two matters: As for the first premise—that He is necessary in essence—this has two necessary consequences: First: that He is transcendent in His essence from multiplicity. Then from His individuality in His essence follows one of them: that He is not a body, because every body is divisible, and what is divisible is not individual. And since He is not a body, He is not in a direction. And the second of them: that there is not more than one necessary of existence, for if there were more than one, they would share in necessity and differ in identity, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so each one of them would be composite, and we have assumed it to be individual—this is a contradiction. The second: that the necessary of existence in essence is neither a substrate nor an indweller, otherwise dependence would return.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage introduces the second premise in the overall discussion, which is the premise of necessity and possibility (muqaddimat al-wujub wa al-imkaan). It is described as the ultimate goal of the rational. It states the fundamental division of existence into necessary and possible, and that the possible needs a necessitator which must be necessary in essence and attributes, otherwise it would regress. Then it says there are two matters (amran). The first matter is the premise that God is necessary in essence, and this premise has two necessary consequences (laziman). The first consequence is that God is transcendent (munazzah) from multiplicity. This leads to two further points: first, that He is not a body, because bodies are divisible and thus not individual; second, that He is not in a direction. The second consequence is that there cannot be more than one necessary being, because two would share necessity and differ in identity, making each composite, which contradicts individuality. Then the second matter (the second of the two matters) is that the necessary being cannot be a substrate (mahall) or an indweller (hulul), because that would entail dependence.

?

?

Razi's Proof of the Oneness of the Necessary Being
Imam Razi presents a reductio ad absurdum proof that the Necessary Being must be one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's oneness based on the impossibility of composition in the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
Two Necessary Beings would each be composite, contradicting absolute simplicity.

Imam al-Razi presents a concise philosophical argument that there cannot be more than one Necessary Being. This proof relies on the principle that anything sharing a universal attribute while also having a particular distinction would be composite, but the Necessary Being is absolutely simple. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text silently, then the English translation. Notice how compact the argument is.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The argument begins with a supposition ('if there were more than one') and derives a contradiction ('this is contradictory'). Map out the two parts: assumption and conclusion.
3
Understand the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'sharing' (اشتراك), 'specification' (تعين), and 'composition' (تركيب). Ask yourself: why does sharing and differing lead to composition?
The argument treats the shared aspect and the distinguishing aspect as two distinct constituents of each being.
4
Reflect on the contradiction
The Necessary Being is assumed to be simple (fard). The argument shows that multiplicity forces composition. Why is composition impossible for the Necessary Being?
Composition implies dependence on parts, which contradicts necessary existence.
5
Summarize the proof
Write down the proof in your own words in two or three sentences. Ensure you capture the logical flow: assumption, analysis, contradiction, conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن لا يكون واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد، إذ لو كان أكثر من واحد لاشتركا في الوجوب وبيانا في التعين وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما في نفسه مركبًا، وقد فرضناه فردا، هذا خلف.
And the second of them: that the Necessary Existent is not more than one, for if there were more than one, they would share in existence (wujūb) and be distinct in specification (ta‘ayyun), and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ, so it would follow that each one of them is in itself composite (murakkab), while we have assumed it to be simple (fard); this is a contradiction (khalaf).
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's oneness (tawḥīd). It is the second of two points (the first is not included here). The argument is by reductio ad absurdum. Suppose there were two necessary existents. They would both share the property of being necessary (wujūb) – that is their commonality. But they must also be distinct from each other; their distinctness is due to some specification (ta‘ayyun) that differentiates them. If the shared aspect (participation) is different from the differentiating aspect (distinction), then each necessary existent would have two aspects: one shared, one unique. That would make each composite (murakkab). However, the Necessary Being is assumed to be absolutely simple (fard), not composed of parts. So the assumption of two necessary existents leads to a contradiction. Therefore, it is impossible for there to be more than one Necessary Being.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (Necessary Being)
اشتراك (sharing)
تعين (specification)
تركيب (composition)
خلف (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiplicity does not imply composition if the shared and distinct aspects are not real parts but only conceptual distinctions.
Correction: Razi's argument treats the aspects as real constituents in the quiddity; if they were merely conceptual, the beings would not be truly distinct, so the distinction must be real, leading to composition.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, why could there not be two Necessary Beings?
A) Because they would need a cause to distinguish them.
B) Because they would share in necessity but differ in specification, making each composite.
C) Because the Necessary Being cannot be located.
D) Because the Quran states there is only one God.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being cannot be multiple because multiplicity would entail composition, which contradicts the absolute simplicity required for necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Necessary Being is absolutely one and simple, with no division or parts, then your heart must be entirely directed to Him alone. What is one attachment, person, or thing that currently divides your attention away from exclusive devotion to God? How will you actively work to remove that division starting today?
?
Razi's proof shows that any multiplicity in the Necessary Being leads to contradiction. In your own life, do your actions ever contradict your claim of tawhid? For example, do you sometimes fear people more than God, or rely on your own plans more than on His decree? Choose one specific area where you will align your actions fully with His oneness, and take one practical step this week to demonstrate that reliance.
?
The simplicity of the Necessary Being means He is not composed of separate attributes. When you pray, do you sometimes think of God's mercy as separate from His justice, or His power as separate from His wisdom? How would your worship change if you constantly reminded yourself that His attributes are one with His essence, and He acts as a single, perfect whole?
